HEADLINE: State Presses Inquiry Into Corruption In Broadway Ticket Sales

BYLINE: By PETER APPLEBOME 

 BODY:
The New York State Attorney General's office is close to wrapping up an investigation of what officials say are rampant kickbacks and price-gouging practices involving the biggest theaters on Broadway and the most powerful entertainment and sports venues in the city, officials said yesterday. 
 One target is the Roundabout Theater, whose "Cabaret," temporarily closed because of the construction accident in Times Square, has become one of the most coveted tickets in years. Ellen Richard, the Roundabout's general manager, said the theater has been cooperating with the Attorney General's office since it was asked to turn over records.
The Roundabout was named as a subject of the investigation yesterday in the Daily Variety.
But David Corvette, a spokesman for the office of Attorney General Dennis C. Vacco, said similar requests for information had been made to more than a dozen other theaters and entertainment companies. He said the investigation goes far beyond the Roundabout to what investigators believe is rampant corruption that is inflating ticket prices for entertainment and sports events. The investigation includes many of the most recognized names in the theater and entertainment industry, Mr. Corvette said, but declined to name them.
The investigation was greeted with skepticism by many people involved in the theater and ticket industries, who said some degree of price inflation for coveted tickets had always been a part of the entertainment business. They added that campaigns against ticket scalping or price gouging have long been popular ploys for politicians in election years.
The most common form of corruption is what is called ice, in which box offices overcharge ticket brokers for tickets to choice events, adding costs that are then passed along to consumers, many of them out-of-towners intent on getting tickets to events that sold out long ago.
"We think that ice is rampant in the industry, and that it's a major reason people have to pay so much to go to Broadway or concerts these days," Mr. Corvette said. The practices being investigated would affect consumers who buy tickets through brokers rather than those who buy them directly from a box office. But, he said, the Attorney General's office believed that the ingrained price gouging over time had the effects of inflating ticket prices across the board.
Targets of the investigation, he said, include "the most prominent Broadway venues along with some of the most prominent concert and entertainment venues."
He said the department is taking depositions in connection with its investigation and is preparing to file lawsuits or take other legal steps. The department's investigation should be completed "within the next couple of months," Mr. Corvette said.
 One official of a company that deals in theater tickets also argued that ice, ticket scalping or other practices had long been part of the entertainment business. He said the investigation had more to do with politics than with lower prices. Attorney General Vacco, a Republican, is running for re-election in November.
"I have no doubt ice exists," said the official who spoke on condition of anonymity. "It does exist, it always has existed and it always will exist. But I think you have about as much chance of stomping it out as you have of outlawing prostitution. We see these kind of investigations all the time, particularly in election years, but I don't think it's likely to change anything."
Ms. Richard of the Roundabout said, "I have no knowledge of anything happening here, but if someone in our box office is selling tickets, I would really like to find out about it and see them prosecuted. We're a not-for-profit theater. Someone making money off our tickets is offensive to me."
But an official with the Attorney General's office said the abuse being investigated at the Roundabout appeared to be more the theater's common practice of selling tickets to brokers at prices above those allowed by law -- meaning not more than $5 or 10 percent above the ticket price -- rather than the actions of one rogue individual.
The Attorney General's office said it believed that the practice is endemic in the entertainment industry, an official said.
The Attorney General's office has taken action in other investigations related to ticket pricing. Joseph Nekola, the former manager of the Jones Beach Theater, pleaded guilty last month to second-degree grand larceny and third-degree computer tampering in the theft of more than 8,000 tickets for the theater's 1996 summer concert series. In April, Janet Rosenblatt and Gerald Nahay, owners of the Manhattan theater company Tickets on Request, were charged with selling tickets to sports events and concerts at rates as high as 830 percent above the face value on the tickets.
The office has also sought information from concertgoers about where they got their tickets and how much they paid.
No action has been taken against any of the big-name entertainment companies and theaters said to be the subject of the current inquiry.
Mr. Corvette denied that the two-year-old investigation had more to do with publicity than law enforcement.
"Just because this sort of thing has gone on for years does not make it right or justify the impact it has on the exorbitant prices for tickets that consumers have to pay," he said. "If we can put a dent in that, we've accomplished something."

HEADLINE: Sales of Existing Single-Family Homes Slip a Bit

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, June 27

 BODY:
Sales of existing single-family homes slipped in June from May's high level but remained robust.
The decrease, 2.3 percent, brought the seasonally adjusted annual sales rate to 4.72 million, down from 4.83 million, the National Association of Realtors said today. 
 Sales increases in the Midwest and the South were more than offset by drops in the West and Northeast. Although the overall decline was somewhat above what analysts had forecast, they were not concerned.
"We have all gotten so spoiled by seeing new records month after month in home resales that we should remember any reading above a 4.5 million annual level is spectacular," said David Orr, an economist for the First Union Corporation.
Low American interest rates, a side effect of Asia's economic turmoil, are helping to keep sales strong by keeping monthly mortgage payments affordable.
Rates for fixed-rate, 30-year loans averaged 7 percent in June, according to Freddie Mac, the mortgage company. Except for 1993, when the average briefly dipped to 6.75 percent, rates have not been lower since the late 1960's.
Also, with the unemployment rate near a 28-year low in June and stock market gains in double digits, more Americans can afford to buy a home.
"Given favorable interest rates and income trends, we expect home sales to remain strong," said Karen Dexter, an economist with Merrill Lynch & Company.
Fred Flick, the realtors' vice president for economic research, predicted sales for all of 1998 would total around 4.54 million, 7.7 percent higher than 1997, when a record 4.21 million homes were sold.
Sales in the West fell 10.9 percent in June to an annual rate of 980,000 units, while sales in the Northeast fell by 5.6 percent, to a rate of 670,000 units. Sales rose 1.7 percent, to a rate of 1.2 million units in the Midwest, and 1.6 percent, to a rate of 1.87 million units in the South. Nationwide, the median price of an existing-home sale was $134,600 in June, up 5.8 percent from a year earlier.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Malaysia Postpones $2 Billion Bond Sale

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: KUALA LUMPUR, Malaysia, July 27

 BODY:
Malaysia said today that it had postponed an international sale of $2 billion in bonds because two credit-rating agencies lowered their assessment of the country last week.
The delay in the fund-raising effort could prolong the nation's economic problems. Malaysia's economy contracted at a 1.8 percent annual rate in the first quarter, its first decline in 13 years, and the Government expects a contraction of between 1 percent and 2 percent during the full year. 
 The money from the bond sale was to be used to help retire bad debts held by the nation's banks.
The downgrading of Malaysia's sovereign credit rating by the two agencies, Moody's Investors Service Inc. and the Standard & Poor's Corporation, left the rating only a few levels above "junk" status. When it does bring the Government bonds to market, Malaysia will probably have to pay a higher interest rate to reflect the downgrading.
Still, bankers noted that Malaysia was already paying a high premium on bonds issued by the state-owned oil company. So although the ratings cut was embarrassing for Malaysia, it will probably not drastically increase the price the country will have to pay to raise money from the debt issue, they said.
The country's Finance Minister, Anwar Ibrahim, had planned to visit the United States to promote the sale of the bonds in a so-called road show. But the ministry said today that "the Government will only bring a transaction to the market once market conditions are conducive for an issue."
Ivan Lee, head of Asian fixed-income research at Salomon Smith Barney in Hong Kong, said: "Now the story line has to be different. They need to revise the road-show material. The old material was based on one rating; now it's got to be geared to the new one."
Moody's cut Malaysia's foreign currency debt rating to Baa2 from A2. S.& P. cut its rating one notch, to BBB+ -- three levels above junk -- from A-.
Hurt by the downgrading and a decline on the Tokyo market, Malaysian stock prices fell today, with the benchmark index decreasing 2.5 percent, to 408.04, its lowest point since 1989. The delay in the bond sale "may cause the market to drift down a bit," said Lai Tak Heong, head of Malaysian research at SG Research here. "It's important to get that under way."

HEADLINE: Henri Ziegler, Aviation Figure, Is Dead at 91

BYLINE: By JOHN TAGLIABUE 

DATELINE: ROME, July 27

 BODY:
Henri Ziegler, a trained pilot and commander of the Free French Air Force in World War II who helped start some of Europe's most ambitious postwar aviation projects, including the Concorde supersonic jetliner and the Airbus civilian airliner program, died at a nursing home in Paris on July 23. He was 91.
Mr. Ziegler, once denounced as a traitor by a French Government minister for suggesting that France should abandon its splendid isolation and cooperate with other European governments to produce jetliners, lived to see Airbus Industrie, the consortium that he founded and ran from 1970 to 1975, emerge as a vigorous challenger to the Boeing Company as a premier supplier of planes to the world's airlines. 
 Airbus has clawed its way from a fragile alliance of British and French aircraft manufacturers in the early 1970's, to dominance of roughly one-half of the orders placed around the world this year for civilian aircraft. But when Mr. Ziegler first hinted in 1969 that without a dramatic overhaul, Europe's atomized aircraft industries would fail to keep step with aircraft industries in the United States and the Soviet Union, he faced fierce resistance from the party of the go-it-alones in France. "My views raised hell," he was later quoted by The Times of London as saying. "My minister called me into his office and said, 'You are a traitor to your country.' "
Last week, the French Prime Minister, Lionel Jospin, lauded "the memory of one of those who was at the origin of the technological and economic success of the European aeronautic and space industries."
Born Nov. 18, 1906, in Limoges, Mr. Ziegler earned degrees in management and aeronautical engineering, then entered the French Air Force, where he rose quickly to become, in 1938, deputy director of the air force test flight center. One year later, as World War II engulfed Europe, he left for the United States as part of a procurement mission to purchase badly needed aircraft and parts for the French Air Force.
After the fall of France, Mr. Ziegler returned to his occupied homeland to help organize the anti-German resistance. In 1944, he smuggled himself to Britain where, under the nom de guerre Colonel Vernon, he first commanded the ragtag Free French Air Force, then served as chief of staff of the French resistance fighting under Gen. Charles de Gaulle to liberate France.
After the war, he served briefly in Washington and London as military liaison, then joined the French national airline, Air France, in 1946. Two years later, he was named chairman, a job he held until 1954.
Mr. Ziegler held various cabinet- level posts in the 1950's, but he is best remembered for his role in building the French and European aircraft industry. In 1968, he was appointed to run Sud-Aviation, a leading aircraft manufacturer in the south of France, and charged with reorganizing the badly splintered aircraft industry. Sud-Aviation was later absorbed by the larger Societe Nationale Industrielle Aerospatiale, or Snia, which in 1984, as the core of the French aerospace industry, was renamed Aerospatiale S.A.
During his tenure at Sud-Aviation and Snia, Mr. Ziegler championed the ambitious project to join British aircraft builders to jointly develop a supersonic civilian jetliner, the Concorde, which entered regular service, flying at twice the speed of sound, in January 1976. Mr. Ziegler later published a memoir of the project titled "The Great Adventure of the Concorde."
By the mid-1960's, Britain and France were casting about for further ways to unite the forces of their fragmented aircraft industries in a bid to challenge the dominance of the United States, which at the time produced 85 percent of the world's commercial aircraft. Contacts between the British Aircraft Corporation and Hawker Siddeley Aviation with French and German aircraft manufacturers led to the first design studies for a new range of civilian jetliners, beginning with a twin-engine wide-body airliner, which was started in October 1972, as the Airbus 300B. In 1970, Mr. Ziegler had been named to head a consortium of companies to build the plane, and against the bitter resistance of a part of the French bureaucracy he championed the idea of a unified management and sales organization. He stayed at Airbus until 1975, laying the groundwork for the later enlargement of the consortium by the addition of British and Spanish companies.
If Airbus is Mr. Ziegler's greatest legacy, the supersonic Concorde may have been his greatest commercial disappointment. Together with his British counterpart, Sir George Edwards, chairman of British Aircraft, he vigorously lobbied American political leaders and airline officials, and at least six American airlines originally took options to purchase 31 Concordes.
In 1971, President Richard M. Nixon toured a Concorde that had carried President Georges Pompidou of France to a meeting of the two leaders and expressed enthusiasm. To further promote the Concorde, Mr. Ziegler publicly urged a joint European-American project for an even larger supersonic plane for the 1980's. But Boeing, joined by environmental organizations that objected to its noise, fought the Concorde. Thus, the decision in 1973 by Trans World Airlines and Pan American World Airways, then the largest American carriers, to cancel their orders was a major setback. Other American airlines soon followed suit, and the Concorde's entry into the crucial American market was effectively blocked.
Mr. Ziegler is survived by four children. A son, Bernard Ziegler, served as senior vice president for engineering at Airbus Industrie until his retirement last year.

HEADLINE: Floribunda! The Business Of Blooms Consolidates;
A Mixed Bouquet of Acquisitions Has Built U.S.A. Floral Products

BYLINE: By SHARON R. KING 

DATELINE: MIAMI

 BODY:
The scene at 5 A.M. in the Miami International Airport cargo area hasn't changed much over the years. Refrigerated trucks with logos of companies like Atlantic Bouquet, CFX ("The Cut Flower Experts") and Continental Farms still rumble through the security gates, back up to the loading docks and fill their trailers with boxes of roses, lilies, chrysanthemums, baby's breath, bird of paradise and dozens of other kinds of flowers.
Then they make their way along Northwest 25th Street to the swath of industrial parks five miles away that is the hub of Miami's flower-distribution business. By 7 A.M., the flower companies' sales reps are hitting the phones, scrambling to find buyers for any inventory that hasn't already been sold. 
 Beneath all the hubbub, though, one thing is very different than it once was -- and as a result, American shoppers can expect to find a lot more flowers in a lot more outlets before too long. Those Atlantic Bouquet, CFX and Continental Farms trucks -- as well as rigs with a half-dozen other names splashed across their sides -- now all belong to a single company, U.S.A. Floral Products Inc.
The consolidation craze that has swept through industry after industry during the last few years -- from insurance and banking to publishing and railroads -- has percolated down to an unlikely corner of the American marketplace: flowers.
And leading the pack is U.S.A. Floral, which aspires to be the undisputed giant of the $14 billion flower trade. In the last year, it has snapped up 14 mom-and-pop distributors, wholesalers and bouquet makers for a total cost of $152 million. It owns companies in 22 states and Canada and says it will soon expand into Europe and Asia.
Just this month, U.S.A. Floral announced plans to purchase eight more floral businesses around the country for $17.4 million in cash and stock. These include Channel Islands Floral, a bouquet maker in California; Floramark, a Denver-based bouquet maker, and Evergreen Wholesale Florist in Seattle.
Few people will notice U.S.A. Floral's growing market presence because it is letting the companies that it acquires keep their names, largely because of the brand loyalty of many of their customers.
But already, its revenues have shot up to a projected $561 million this year, from $184 million in 1997, the company says, and it confidently predicts that sales will soar to $1 billion next year.
When asked how big U.S.A. Floral wants to be, Robert J. Poirier, its chairman and chief executive, responds with a question of his own: "How big is the floral industry?"
"I'm a small part of floral consumption over all," he said. "There is a tremendous amount of business that is left on the table."
Not everybody -- least of all a lot of investors -- shares Mr. Poirier's enthusiasm, however. The company went public last October at $13 a share, and its stock surged to a peak of $23.875 in February. But it has sunk since then, dropping as low as $15.375 in mid-June before recovering a bit. Analysts blame a lack of investor enthusiasm for consolidation companies. U.S.A. Floral Products' shares fell 25 cents on Monday, to $15.75, in Nasdaq trading.
Recently, the risks of the so-called roll-up strategy have become evident, with analysts saying that some companies have grown too quickly, failed to integrate operations, relied too much on a few large customers and used large acquisitions to mask serious operating problems.
None of that fazes Mr. Poirier, who says the current price still represents a premium from the $13 offering.
Mr. Poirier, a 23-year veteran of the flower industry, is overseeing U.S.A. Floral's expansion with the help of a pioneer in the relatively new field of consolidating small, fragmented industries, Jonathan J. Ledecky.
Mr. Ledecky, who is the nonexecutive chairman of the company and its largest shareholder with a stake of more than 8 percent, founded the U.S. Office Products Company in 1995 and built it into a $2.8 billion giant by buying hundreds of independent stationery stores and furniture and office-product suppliers that were struggling to survive against Office Depot and Staples. And last year, he formed the Consolidated Capital Corporation to string together businesses in similarly dispersed industries.
Mr. Poirier said he was following Mr. Ledecky's strategy of buying high-quality companies at a modest premium and letting them continue as independent operators with their old names and suppliers intact. "This is a video replay of U.S. Office Products," he said.
But the similarity may be what has jaded investors. And Mr. Poirier is also quick to point out the ways in which his business model differs from that of U.S. Office Products. His focus on buying only floral companies and moving quickly to consolidate operations has helped U.S.A. Floral escape some of the criticisms facing U.S. Office Products these days. And one significant difference -- the use of the purchase method of accounting versus pooling to record acquisitions -- should keep Federal regulators at bay. It may also stave off steep stock price declines like those experienced by U.S. Office Products, whose shares have plunged about 66 percent since June 1.
While some other flower companies are also growing, U.S.A. Floral is a standout. Its most formidable competitor is probably the Dole Food Company, the $4.3 billion California conglomerate, whose flower sales surged from nothing to $100 million this year when it bought the largest wholesaler in Florida along with its production farms in South America. (Such well-known companies as 1-800-Flowers or Florists' Transworld Delivery Inc., known as F.T.D., are not direct competitors because they are essentially marketing operations and retail florist groups.)
But the main reason for U.S.A. Floral's upbeat outlook is the enormous room that it sees for the growth of the entire flower market, which is split almost evenly between the $6 billion fresh-flower business and the $8 billion industry in potted plants and vases. Only about 25 percent of Americans buy flowers regularly, compared with 60 percent of Europeans, and U.S.A. Floral is confident it can get the numbers up.
"The average American is in a grocery store two to three times a week," Mr. Poirier said. "I'd like people to start thinking that if they haven't got flowers, then there's something missing."
Flowers are a diverse business. Imagine the stems of the industry spreading throughout the nation, creating a wild arrangement of largely family-owned businesses, including roughly 9,700 domestic growers, 1,000 wholesalers, 27,000 retail florists and 23,000 supermarket floral operations. The industry has its tap roots in Latin America, and the pot, so to speak, is centered around the Miami airport, where approximately 100 importers handle roughly 70 percent of all flowers consumed in the United States.
U.S.A. Floral isn't the only company with grand ambitions, of course. Dole's purchases last month gave it a foothold in the flower business, and it hopes that its presence in supermarket produce departments will give it a marketing edge to increase the revenues of its floral companies. (U.S.A. Floral does not own any farms but it has exclusive agreements with several farms it deals with.)
Several Miami companies are also forming alliances and considering mergers in order to make sure they are big enough to compete. And a smaller, Los Angeles-based public company, Sun Cut Floral Network, was formed earlier this year after it was acquired by a former medical technology company.
"We're not certain where all of it is going, but the trend is probably unstoppable at this point," said Alvaro Varela, president of Agriflora, a Miami importer. Mr. Varela said his company had spurned a takeover bid from U.S.A. Floral because "the group is so big already we don't know where we would fit." Instead, he said, Agriflora plans to merge with some other local distributors.
Peter Moran, the executive vice president and chief executive officer of the Society of American Florists, said the flower industry had long resisted the big-is-better mentality so prevalent in other sectors of the economy. Previous efforts to forge national floral empires have failed "because the business is really driven by relationships and outside people misjudge those relationships," he said. But things have changed. "There's definitely a mania going on," he said. "Everybody's concerned that if they're not part of a strategic relationship" they will not survive.
Still, meshing operations in the flower trade -- where maintaining the freshness of one of the world's most perishable products is the key and where "keep it moving" is the mantra -- won't be easy.
Within the space of 24 hours, flowers are harvested on farms in Ecuador, Colombia and Costa Rica; shipped to Miami; X-rayed as they pass through 33-degree refrigerated coolers and then examined by United States Department of Agriculture inspectors, all under the watchful eyes of security cameras; picked up by distributors; warehoused, and finally put on trucks bound for florists', Stop & Shops, Kroger's stores and Wal-Marts all over the country.
It is not an industry that lends itself to cookie-cutter production. At one Miami bouquet company owned by U.S.A. Floral, each worker assembles a single bouquet. At another, women stand in front of conveyor belts, dropping set numbers of particular flowers into each passing bouquet. Neither company plans to change its procedures.
Still, U.S.A. Floral believes it can save money by consolidating back-office operations like accounting, finance, insurance and employee benefits. For example, it says, it recently cut costs by more than $700,000 by combining the hazard insurance policies of 16 of its companies. It also saved almost $500,000 by channeling its telephone business to a single long-distance company. And it plans to fold its 10 Miami companies into six locations by year's end. Task forces of executives from several of the companies are exploring such other economy moves as adoption of a single computer system.
The company believes that its heft will also allow it to distribute more flowers at lower cost and save on transportation, flower handling and storage. Mr. Poirer wants to create a string of regional distributors in cities like Atlanta and Dallas to put flowers in customers' hands more quickly.
Down the road, U.S.A. Floral hopes to do joint marketing and merchandising with retailers. Today, the industry spends less than $100 million a year on flower promotions, according to Mr. Moran of the Society of American Florists. "For a $14 billion industry, that's not going to move the needle much," he said.
The ultimate goal is to make flowers so ubiquitous that Americans -- who now mostly buy them on impulse or as infrequent gifts -- will get into the habit of picking up bouquets once or twice a week. To achieve that, the industry will have to reach people like Mary J. Radford, a marketing executive in Washington, D.C.
When she lived in New York in the early 1990's, Ms. Radford bought flowers all the time at a local corner grocery store, she says. And she was an even more avid consumer when she lived in Europe in the late 1980's. "In Paris, flowers are all over the place," she said. "There are a lot of little florists' shops all over the city, and they were cheap enough to justify the cost."
But she hasn't bought a single bouquet since she moved to Washington two years ago. "I don't think about it," she said, "because they're not in front of me all the time and I have the perception that they are more expensive."

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS;
American Express Earnings Help Push Dow Past 9,000

BYLINE: By SHARON R. KING 

 BODY:
An earnings report that was stronger than expected lifted stocks out of the doldrums yesterday as most major market indexes closed higher.
But many analysts were not impressed by the rally, which pushed the Dow Jones industrial average up 1.02 percent. And the gains in the stock market did little to mask a growing bearish sentiment on Wall Street. 
 After gaining just 4.38 points on Friday, the Dow Jones industrial average roared back past 9,000 yesterday, rising 90.88 points, to 9,028.24. The rebound, led by strong gains in American Express came after the Dow fell more than 400 points last week.
Stocks began gaining ground after American Express reported second-quarter earnings that topped analysts' forecasts, helped by heavy increases in the use of its charge cards and sales of mutual fund shares.
American Express closed at 112 13/16, up 5 3/4 points, or 5.4 percent, contributing nearly 24 points to the Dow's advance.
The Standard & Poor's 500 index rose 6.47 points, to 1,147.27. And the technology-heavy Nasdaq composite index gained 2.27 points, to 1,933.26. Microsoft, Ericsson and Intel were among the leading gainers.
Small-company shares, however, continued to falter yesterday. The Russell index of 2,000 smaller company stocks fell 5.42 points, or 1.24 percent.
Analysts expressed concerns about the limited breadth of the rally, where gains were concentrated among a small group of large companies. And some cautioned that things could get worse for stocks.
"Predictably, after the big drop we had investors went back into the same big names," said Larry Rice, chief investment officer at Josephthal & Company. "The correction wasn't deep enough and the rally wasn't strong enough" to be sustained, he said.
Barton M. Biggs, chief global strategist at Morgan Stanley Dean Witter, warned investors yesterday that "we may have entered a cyclical bear market in the U.S. which could see the market go down 20 percent to 30 percent over a period of months or quarters."
And he cited Asia's financial crisis as a factor.
"The Asian economies are much worse than most people recognize, and they should continue to deteriorate," Mr. Biggs wrote. In addition, "Japan's election of Keizo Obuchi appears to be a negative development for Japan, Asia, and the world."
Mr. Biggs advised clients to reduce holdings of stocks because of Asia's woes. He recommended investors put 12 percent of their portfolio in large-capitalization United States stocks and 13 percent in large-cap European stocks, down from 13 percent and 14 percent, respectively. Investors should shift the money into cash or short-term securities, he said.
Asian stock markers were lower yesterday, as investors in the region expressed concern that the newly elected Prime Minister, Mr. Obuchi, would not take quick action to spur growth in the Japanese economy, which is still seen as a vital engine for the Pacific Rim's recovery.
Japan's benchmark Nikkei 225 index fell 2.55 percent to a one-month low. Stock markets in Hong Kong, Malaysia and Thailand fell 2 percent or more.
In addition, commodity prices were weaker, as investors expressed similar concerns about Japan's fiscal health.
Copper fell more than 1 percent, backing away from its highest price in more than two months, amid expectations that Japan's new Government will not revive the economy of the world's second-largest copper consumer. Rising supplies, at a time of weak demand in Asia, also hurt copper prices yesterday.
"When the market's sold off in the past it has always been a good time to buy stocks," said Richard Cripps, chief market strategist at Legg Mason Inc. in Baltimore.
But "earnings numbers are coming down," he said. "There's no major driver to move equity prices higher."

Bonds End Lower
By Bloomberg News
Bonds seesawed to end lower yesterday as stocks rebounded from early losses, reducing demand for less risky Treasury securities.
The 30-year Treasury bond declined 11/32, to a price of 105 30/32. The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, rose to 5.71 percent from 5.68 percent on Friday.
Early yesterday the price of the 30-year bond rose as much as 11/32 as stocks fell, but the gains evaporated as stocks rebounded.

HEADLINE: Asians, Reliant on Japan, React Gloomily to Leadership Choice

BYLINE: By MARK LANDLER 

DATELINE: HONG KONG, July 27

 BODY:
Japanese voters are not the only people who reacted tepidly to the election of Keizo Obuchi as the leader of Japan's governing party. The choice of Mr. Obuchi has deflated many in the rest of Asia, who were hoping that a forceful new leader would step up to recharge Japan's weak economy and repair its faltering financial system.
Because of their extensive trade, foreign investment and credit links with Japan, most Asian countries are effectively hostage to Japanese economic fortunes. As the country has slid into a recession, it has dragged down resilient Asian economies like those of Taiwan, Hong Kong and Singapore, and deepened the pain for weaker economies like South Korea's, Thailand's and Indonesia's. 
 "Much of Asia is looking for, and demanding that Japan be the locomotive to pull it out of this slump," William H. Overholt, head of research at BankBoston in Singapore, said in assessing the selection Friday of Mr. Obuchi, who will likely become the next Prime Minister of Japan. "The region was really hanging on this election, and people were hoping for a dynamic Prime Minister."
Stocks across Asia declined sharply today, as investors expressed pessimism that a Government led by Mr. Obuchi, who has been Foreign Minister, would perform the radical surgery that economists say is needed to revive the Japanese economy.
In Tokyo, the benchmark Nikkei index of 225 issues dropped 2.55 percent, while the main Hong Kong market index fell 3.3 percent. The dollar strengthened to 141.95 yen in Asia and rose further to 142.41 in New York.
The gloomy reaction came despite Mr. Obuchi's assurances in a meeting over the weekend with Secretary of State Madeleine K. Albright that he would move quickly to revive Japan's economy. "I can and will act promptly," American officials quoted Mr. Obuchi as telling Mrs. Albright, who was in Manila to attend a meeting of foreign ministers from Asian and Pacific nations.
Mr. Obuchi, 61, is expected to seek about $42 billion in tax cuts and $70 billion in additional public spending to stimulate consumer demand. But he has offered few proposals about how to reform the Japanese banking system, which is staggering under $600 billion in troubled debt. Until he does, analysts said, economies like Thailand's and Hong Kong's, which depend heavily on Japan for loans, will continue to suffer, as Japanese banks curtail their lending.
As leader of the governing Liberal Democratic Party, Mr. Obuchi is all but certain to be elected Prime Minister later this week. Analysts said that one measure of his determination to confront Japan's problems will be his choice of Finance Minister. Today, speculation centered on Kiichi Miyazawa, a 78-year-old party elder and former Prime Minister, though Japanese newspapers said he was also considering two other officials: Seiroku Kajiyama and Koiichi Kato.
Mr. Kajiyama, who challenged Mr. Obuchi in the race for the party presidency, would offer some hope to the markets, according to a few analysts, because he expressed a willingness to shut down troubled banks. But other experts said that no Finance Minister, or even Prime Minister, could rise above a system that prizes consensus and prefers incremental change to upheaval.
"If there is no radical change or speeding up of the reforms in Japan, I'm afraid it will delay the recovery of the rest of the region," said Chia Yew Boon, research director at Santander Investment Securities in Singapore.
Mr. Chia said Japan's economic problems were hurting other Asian markets in three ways. First, the weak yen makes Japanese products cheaper than those of neighboring countries, further depressing their already poor exports. Second, the troubles in Japan's financial system force banks to call in loans to other Asian countries, damaging businesses in the region.
Third, and potentially most destabilizing, the collapse of the Japanese economic model is eroding confidence in other Asian countries, like South Korea and Malaysia, which have imitated Japan by creating a handful of large, national conglomerates with close ties to banks and Government agencies.
On Friday, Moody's Investors Service lowered its rating on Malaysian Government debt, citing "the rapid deterioration in Malaysia's economy this year and the expected effects on the banking sector and Government finance." The downgrading has forced the Malaysian Government to postpone a $2 billion bond issue planned for later in the summer.
Japan's troubles affect Asian countries in different ways. Malaysia and Indonesia, for example, export huge quantities of raw material like timber to Japan. In 1997, such exports accounted for 11.5 percent of Malaysia's gross domestic product and 6.8 percent of Indonesia's, according to Santander. As Japan's housing market has stalled and demand for timber has declined, so have Malaysian and Indonesian exports.
Japanese banks have been prodigious lenders to Asian countries -- both to companies and governments. Hong Kong-based companies owe $40.7 billion to Japanese banks, an amount equivalent to 23 percent of the territory's gross domestic product. The Indonesian Government owes $22.8 billion, or 10.6 percent of its annual output. Now, the spigot has been turned off -- to the chagrin of property developers in Hong Kong and Cabinet ministers in Jakarta.
For Asians, the most alarming thing about Japan's travails is the possibility that the cure could be worse than the disease. In order for Tokyo to reform the banking system, analysts said it must first acknowledge that some of the biggest Japanese banks are insolvent, and then close them. The outgoing Prime Minister, Ryutaro Hashimoto, took the first steps by authorizing the development of a bridge-bank program to protect depositors in smaller banks.
"This is just one baby step in cleaning up the financial system," said Ronald Bevacqua, a Merrill Lynch economist in Tokyo, "I have no question that Obuchi will take the first step. But will he take the following steps?"
Mr. Bevacqua said Mr. Obuchi had as good a chance of success as any Japanese politician -- partly because he has many friends in his party and few enemies outside it. That may enable him to steer a painful financial package through the Parliament more effectively than a more confrontational leader.
As for the other Asian nations, they can really only be bystanders. Japan's economy is too big for its neighbors to export around and too powerful for them to control. They are in the frustrating position of hoping that Mr. Obuchi can deliver on his campaign promises -- and then go well beyond them.
Richard Margolis, a regional strategist with Merrill Lynch in Hong Kong, put it this way, "Even if the Japanese locomotive doesn't come out of the siding and needs a radical repair job, it's probably better to get on with that than deal with years of zero growth and consumer demand."

HEADLINE: Asia Crisis Hurts Union Carbide Profit

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: DANBURY, Conn., July 27

 BODY:
The Union Carbide Corporation said today that its profit declined 37 percent in the second quarter as falling prices, weak Asian markets and the strong dollar outweighed lower raw-material costs.
The company, which makes chemicals and plastics, said earnings fell to $118 million, or 85 cents a diluted share, from $188 million, or $1.28 a share, a year earlier. The results were close to analysts' expectations. 
 Union Carbide fell $2.125, to $48.875, on the New York Stock Exchange today.
Revenue decreased 13 percent, to $1.46 billion from $1.67 billion.
Union Carbide said the results reflected falling prices for products like polyethylene, the most widely used plastic, and weak demand in Asia, where the company had 14 percent of its sales last year. Additionally, the strength of the American currency meant that other currencies were translated into fewer dollars, reducing revenue from overseas.
Increased worldwide production and the Asian economic slump have kept prices low for basic chemicals such as ethylene, which is used to make a variety of other chemicals and plastics, and polyethylene.
Results were helped by rising prices for ethylene glycol, used in antifreeze and polyester fibers, and lower prices for raw materials including ethane, the company said.
Union Carbide said it repurchased 1.6 million shares during the quarter at a cost of $77 million. Since the start of its repurchase program in 1993, the company has bought 52 million shares out of the 60 million shares authorized for repurchase.

HEADLINE: Clinton May Use Wall Street To Ease Social Security Ills

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: ALBUQUERQUE, N.M., July 27

 BODY:
President Clinton said today that he was seriously considering relying on the financial markets to help solve Social Security's long-term problems but said he had not made up his mind about any of the details.
Speaking at a forum and town hall meeting here on Social Security, Mr. Clinton discussed the pros and cons of establishing individual investment accounts within the retirement system or having the Government itself invest a chunk of Social Security's reserves in stocks and bonds. 
 "I honestly don't know what I would do today," Mr. Clinton said when asked which option he would choose if forced to make a decision.
But he all but endorsed the general notion of seeking higher investment returns on Wall Street to reduce the need for benefit cuts, tax increases or other changes to insure that Social Security will be able to pay promised retirement benefits in coming decades to the baby boom generation. Without any changes, Social Security will run short of money starting in 2032.
"If there's any way we can get a higher rate of return in a market economy, while minimizing the risk, whether it's in either one of these approaches, we ought to go for it, because the other alternatives were much less pleasant already," Mr. Clinton said.
The President has not made any final decisions, his aides said, and some of his advisers still argue that Social Security should not invest at all in stocks and corporate bonds. Currently Social Security's reserves are invested in safe but relatively low-yielding Government bonds.
But White House officials said investing in the markets will more than likely be a component of whatever approach Mr. Clinton ultimately supports.
"The President was very much making clear that he is seriously considering some form of equity investment as part of Social Security reform," said Gene Sperling, the White House economic policy advisor. "But he is holding out the caveat that the public and Congress and the Administration are going to have to be willing to deal with the potential downsides of the risk."
Nearly all proposals for shoring up Social Security rely to some degree on reaping the presumed gains available on Wall Street. But there is a vast gulf between one camp -- led by Republicans but including a number of prominent Democrats -- that wants to allow individuals to invest a portion of their Social Security payroll taxes and another camp led by liberal Democrats in the House that is pushing for the Government to invest the retirement system's reserves.
The differing approaches were vigorously debated today by politicians of both parties and a panel of experts assembled here for the third in a series of Social Security forums organized this year by the White House.

HEADLINE: Stocks Plunge, And Russia Moves to Sell Energy Stake

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES 

DATELINE: MOSCOW, July 27

 BODY:
Russian stock prices plummeted again today, a clear indication that investors still doubt whether the Government can make good on a pledge to climb out of its seemingly bottomless hole of domestic and international debt.
The Government seemed to underscore its plight by announcing over the weekend that it would raise money by selling part of its controlling stake in the huge Gazprom natural-gas monopoly. 
 The Russian Trading System index of stock prices, which fell 17 percent last week, dropped another 9.1 percent today, to 143.46, roughly where it was before the International Monetary Fund agreed to prop up the Government's finances with billions in new loans.
The first of those loans, totaling $4.8 billion, reached Russia's central bank late last week and was used to increase foreign-exchange and gold reserves, which had been dropping rapidly. One condition on the loans was Russia's commitment to make sweeping and basic changes in how it assesses and collects taxes and in its spending policies, a promise that President Boris N. Yeltsin's Government has made but which the Russian Parliament so far has rejected.
An analyst in London for Salomon Smith Barney, Mohamed A. el-Erian, said the loan package and the promise of reforms alone are good news, but not good enough to satisfy skittish investors who have seen similar promises fail before.
"They've got a reform-minded team in place," said Mr. Erian, the firm's European chief of emerging market research. "The issue comes down to implementation, implementation, implementation. And the market is watching that very, very carefully."
Mr. Yeltsin's reforms still face at least two major obstacles: more opposition from the Parliament, which is expected to resume considering the fiscal overhaul next month, and opposition from consumers and big business, both of which face higher taxes.
Given the fall in stock prices, it seemed a poor time for the Government to sell shares in Gazprom. American Depository Receipts in Gazprom, which are traded on foreign stock markets, have fallen by more than one third in the last 10 days, to a 52-week low.
Mr. Yeltsin approved plans this weekend to sell 5.87 percentage points of the Government's 41 percent stake in Gazprom, the world's largest natural-gas company and a linchpin of the Russian economy. The shares being sold are worth about $1.4 billion on foreign markets, and experts suggested today that the Government would try to interest two foreign partners in Gazprom ventures, the Royal Dutch/Shell Group and ENI S.p.A., the Italian energy conglomerate, to buy the shares. The stock would be worth considerably less -- about $520 million -- were it sold to Russians on the Russian stock market.

HEADLINE: Guardians on the Streets of Despair;
New Jersey Task Force Focuses on Crimes Against Prostitutes

BYLINE: By ALAN FEUER 

DATELINE: NEWARK, July 24

 BODY:
The woman known as Soft Touch stood barefoot on the sidewalk by her house in the Central Ward and watched the customized van glide gently to the curb. At 9 o'clock on a Friday night, it could mean only one thing -- another "date."
But she was off tonight. She crossed her arms and began to walk away. She had reached the gate to her tiny yard when a man in a business suit stepped out of the van and called her name. At the sound of his voice, she stopped and turned around. 
 "That you, Malik?" she asked the man.
"It sure is."
"Well, all right," she drawled with a nod and a grin.
For the next 10 minutes, Soft Touch, a 33-year-old prostitute who refused to give her real name, and Malik, a captain in the Essex County Prosecutor's office whose real name is Louis Portella Jr., stood on the street doing what they have done almost every night for the last three months: discuss the unsolved slayings of 14 prostitutes killed in the county since 1993.
The neglected cases, all involving black women, touched a nerve in Patricia A. Hurt, who became the county's first black female Prosecutor in January. In her first official act, Ms. Hurt made these murders a high priority of her office and created a task force to investigate them.
While members of the task force have yet to solve any of the original 14 killings, their constant presence on the street has led directly to the arrests of seven men for crimes against prostitutes that range from an unrelated murder to sexual assault.
In making these arrests, members of the task force have tried to strike a delicate balance between getting close, but not too close, to the prostitutes. While investigators need the invaluable information that only these women can provide and sometimes turn a blind eye to their soliciting, they cannot afford to give the appearance of shielding the prostitutes, whose very occupations violate the law.
But even after just four months, the investigators have succeeded in shedding new light on a world of violence and despair that continues to exist in Essex County.
The task force, composed of 14 detectives from agencies around the county, embodies Ms. Hurt's profoundly held belief that law enforcement officers must go out of their way to protect vulnerable segments of society.
"At first, the prostitutes thought we would be bad for business," Ms. Hurt said in a recent interview. "But we won over the confidence of a lot of women once they realized that we are there, in a sense, as their advocates."
Though Ms. Hurt said that many streetwalkers live in fear of their lives, she added that her investigators had managed to convince the women that they were on their side.
Captain Portella, a tall but gentle-looking man who could pass for the owner of a bowling alley, is the head of the task force. In 22 years with the Newark Police Department, many spent patrolling streets where prostitutes worked their trade, Captain Portella, 60, developed a rapport with the women, a rap-and-banter friendliness that, he said, has won him credibility and respect.
"The most important thing you can give these women is your word," he said. "And these individuals are the best source of information in the early hours. Nobody sees it, nobody hears it quicker than a prostitute out on the street."
A few short blocks in central Newark form one hub of the county's prostitution trade. On a recent stormy night, children, shirtless and thin, spun black umbrellas on their shoulders and old men sipped at brown-bagged beers beneath the lighted shelter of a yellow bodega awning. Music, warped by speed, blared from passing cars. And in the midst of it came the Stroll, as investigators call it -- the nightly parade of prostitutes.
In a typical case developed from working the Stroll, the task force arrested Bharat Malde, 31, of Roselle, who was indicted on July 8 on charges that he posed as a police officer and assaulted or sexually harassed five women after luring them into what he claimed was his unmarked squad car.
The task force learned of Mr. Malde by canvassing central Newark and talking to prostitutes who knew him from the Stroll. Without the task force, Captain Portella said, there may never have been a case against Mr. Malde. The women who the task force says were his victims, all prostitutes, never filed claims with the police.
To make such cases, members of the task force have taught prostitutes to jot down license plate numbers each time they climb into a client's car. The task force members have taught the prostitutes to remember names, identifying features, even unusual sexual tastes.
In return, the investigators have given assistance where it is due: a well-placed word to a presiding judge, a couple of dollars on a quiet Friday night.
Despite the wealth of information they have reaped, investigators acknowledge that maintaining relationships with women who break the law is a delicate, risky business -- particularly in light of the discovery in New York City earlier this month that at least 20 police officers and commanders in Manhattan's Midtown South Precinct may have protected prostitutes in exchange for free sex for more than a decade.
Captain Portella adamantly denied that his investigators had anything but a working relationship with the prostitutes they use as informers, and he said he was concerned that the developments across the Hudson could hinder his efforts. "Any time cops get into trouble, whether it's with prostitutes or narcotics, it doesn't help us," he said.
"But what we're doing here is altogether different from what's going on in New York," he said, largely because the prostitutes involved in his investigations need help.
While his investigators might turn a blind eye to soliciting or to drug use, Captain Portella said, they have no qualms about arresting a prostitute who robs a man or breaks more serious laws.
The investigators have chosen to focus on protecting prostitutes. "There is no conflict here, because we're never going to eliminate prostitution," said Detective Al Burroughs, who joined the task force after serving 10 years in the Newark Police Department. "We're talking about people with nothing -- no education, no job skills, no family -- except a knowledge of the streets."
Detective Burroughs maintained that his leniency was beneficial both to the women and to his own investigative efforts. "They're the ones we turn to for information," he said. "Who was in the red car last night? Who was last seen with Soft Touch? Who was running from the house on Sherman Avenue?"
Although task force members say that information is easy to come by on the streets, the investigators face a difficult problem. They are compelled to build cases for a jury with testimony that comes from notoriously suspect sources: prostitutes.
"There's one thing to understand when you're dealing with prostitutes as witnesses," said Patrick Toscano, Essex County's first assistant prosecutor. "When you cast a play in hell, you can't expect to get angels as actors."
Nevertheless, Mr. Toscano said he did not believe that the indictments already returned would be damaged by the reputation of the witnesses. "Just because you've prostituted yourself," he said, "doesn't mean you weren't beaten or raped."
Newark has taken significant steps toward economic revival in the last nine months, largely because of the New Jersey Performing Arts Center, a grand glass-shelled structure that opened downtown last October. The arts center has not only drawn suburbanites, who once feared Newark, to the city, it has also formed the anchor for a miniature boom in real estate speculation in recent months and paved the way for other potentially lucrative tenants to relocate in Newark.
But by and large, the revival has failed to reach the wooden frame houses and brick apartment buildings of central Newark, where prostitutes say they have seen few signs of urban renewal.
Though Newark's rate of serious crime plunged to a 30-year low in 1997, the talk of Sherman Avenue and its surroundings in the last three months has been of the prostitute murders, not of falling crime statistics. "I'm scared every night I go out," said a prostitute in a tattered black dress who gave her name only as Theresa. "I've been out here three years, and it's just getting more violent."
Still, many of these women say they are grateful for the efforts of the task force.
Outside a lighted bodega window, a prostitute in a dark blue halter top and cut-off denim shorts, who gave her name only as Jackie, told Captain Portella and Detective Burroughs about a man she said she believed was responsible for the murder of a streetwalker whom the police know only as Yasmeen.
The woman, not one of the 14 prostitutes on Ms. Hurt's original list, was killed in 1991, investigators say, after stealing $2,700 from a client's pocket so smoothly that she was able to snatch the big bills and return the singles to his wallet unnoticed.
"I heard it was a pimp who did it," Jackie said. "He rides around in Jheri curls. I think his name is Prince. He be getting girls to take him out and then I don't see them anymore."
The stories one hears on the Stroll are often bound with the twin threads of violence and strength. And the women who tell them seem to possess a startling fortitude.
Moments after speaking with Jackie, a third prostitute walked past the two investigators, and Detective Burroughs, with a rapid sideways glance, approached her. Giving her name as only Iris, she said had been kidnapped by a customer in 1990 and taken to his house, where she was raped.
"It was in all the newspapers," Iris said, with a clinical tone. "I had to jump out the window and run up on the roof to hide." Emergency workers later found her there, battered but alive, she said.
Jackie, who had stayed to listen to Iris's tale, said, "We see all things." She added, "For myself, I let my clients think I'm not paying attention. But don't try nothing funny with me."
"If something happens," she said, as the investigators stood and listened, "we both going to the hospital. I'm going to take him out, too."

HEADLINE: Still a Puny Military Force By the World's Standards

BYLINE: By SETH FAISON 

DATELINE: BEIJING, July 27

 BODY:
China's locomotivelike economic growth and its brittle public face have helped foment a perception overseas that it poses a growing military threat to its neighbors. In the next century, some experts have predicted, China could even clash militarily with the United States.
Yet by current measurements, sketched out vaguely in a report issued here today, China's military forces are still rudimentary by international standards. China's military budget was officially less than $10 billion in 1998, not 4 percent of the United States budget and less than half of South Korea's. 
 But there is no mention of the estimated 15,000 businesses controlled by the army or how much they earn.
The report, "China's National Defense," is a rare public description of the scope of operations by the military. The study was apparently written for an international audience, laden as it is with assurances that China is a peaceloving nation.
Although the report discloses no previously unknown vital information about the military forces, Western military analysts said it was a milestone for a military whose most basic operations are ordinarily considered secret.
"The Chinese don't give very much away here," said Tai Ming Cheung, an expert on the Chinese military who works for the Hong Kong office of Kroll Associates. "The real issue is that it's a symbolic breakthrough just to release this kind of report."
Under pressure from other countries, especially the United States, to be more open about its basic operations, Beijing has used the report to outline the army's basic aims, duties and operations. Yet much of the study is simply bland pronouncements about the commitment to peace and regional cooperation.
"Facts show that China is a responsible big country and a firm force safeguarding world peace and stability," is a typical sentence.
The report reiterates Beijing's long-held position on Taiwan, that reunification is favored through peaceful means, but that resorting to force is always an option.
Perhaps the most relevant section is on expenditures, even though it relies only on official statistics.
Dividing its military spending into three categories, the report says that $3.5 billion was spent last year on personnel costs, $3.2 billion on "maintenance of activities" and $3.1 billion on equipment.
Over all the report insists that the national military policy is defensive in nature and is geared toward resisting aggression. It extols the country's "peace-loving tradition" and says China has resorted to military force only after an enemy had struck first -- though border disputes with India in 1962 and Vietnam in 1979 might suggest otherwise.
"The Chinese people are willing, together with the people of the other countries in the world," the study says, "to make unremitting efforts for the lofty cause of promoting world peace and development."

HEADLINE: Albright Rebukes Chinese On Arrest of Dissidents

BYLINE: By PHILIP SHENON 

DATELINE: MANILA, July 27

 BODY:
Secretary of State Madeleine K. Albright protested to the Chinese Foreign Minister today over the arrest of several political dissidents since President Clinton's visit to China, a development that other Administration officials said had alarmed and embarrassed the United States.
"I made quite clear that arresting people is not the way that we see follow-through," Ms. Albright said at a news conference here after meeting with Foreign Minister Tang Jiaxuan. "So I can assure you that the subject was raised." 
 Ms. Albright also called a special news conference to denounce Myanmar's leaders for their treatment of the leading Burmese dissident.
Human rights groups say at least 15 democratic activists in China have been detained since President Clinton's meeting in Beijing last month with President Jian Xemin. While some dissidents were released within days, others remain jailed and appear likely to face long prison sentences.
One dissident, Zhu Zhengming, 35, was released on Sunday after 17 days of detention in the eastern city of Hangzhou, but he said his freedom was still restricted by the police. "The police told me I shouldn't meet certain people," Mr. Zhu said in an interview with Reuters. "I shouldn't say certain things and shouldn't do certain things."
The detentions have dismayed senior Clinton Administration officials, who had portrayed the President's nine-day visit to China as having advanced the issue of human rights there. President Clinton was allowed to make broadcasts on Chinese television and radio to call for greater political freedom in China.
"One of the major successes of the President's trip was to raise human rights issues publicly in China," said Ms. Albright, who is in the Philippines to attend an annual meeting of foreign ministers from the Association of Southeast Asian Nations and other countries with an interest in the region. "I did raise our concerns about some of the recent arrests."
Clinton Administration officials traveling with the Secretary of State, speaking on condition of anonymity, said she had protested forcefully to the Chinese delegation led here by Mr. Tang.
"They couldn't miss the point," a senior official said, adding that the arrests "go in the opposite direction that we're looking for -- that has an impact on the overall relationship." Another American official said Ms. Albright expressed concern to the Chinese Foreign Minister "about these recent steps backward in the human rights situation."
Several Chinese dissidents are reported to have been arrested in the last week alone. Human rights groups said at least four people were taken into custody on Friday night in southern Hunan Province.
On Wednesday the police arrested Zhang Shanguang, a prominent dissident who had already spent seven years in jail, for trying to organize a group of laidoff workers. Last Tuesday a Chinese court sentenced Fan Yiping to three years in prison for helping a fellow activist escape from China.
Despite her criticism of the Chinese, Ms. Albright said she and Mr. Tang "talked about the successes of the summit" between the American and Chinese leaders, saying progress was made "towards developing a strategic partnership with China."
And she noted the Administration's pleasure in last week's refusal by the House of Representatives to block President Clinton's granting of so-called normal trade relations status to China, formerly known as most-favored-nations status.
Ms. Albright said the vote signals "the increasing acceptance of the fact that having a relationship with China in trade does not in any way change our stress on the importance of the human rights agenda."
Whatever her criticism of Beijing, it paled by comparison to her attack today on the Government of Myanmar, the Southeast Asian nation formerly known as Burma, which is a member of the Association of Southeast Asian Nations and has found itself the target of growing criticism by its neighbors over its human rights record.
Ms. Albright called a special news conference today to denounce the Burmese junta for its treatment of the nation's most prominent dissident, Daw Aung San Suu Kyi.
Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi, who won the Nobel Peace Prize for her campaign to bring democracy to Myanmar, was reported today to be in the fourth day of a standoff with the military, which stopped her car outside the capital, Yangon (formerly Rangoon), as she tried to drive to a meeting with opposition colleagues. News reports say she has refused to budge from the car.
"We are gravely concerned about the health and safety of Aung San Suu Kyi and will hold the Burmese authorities directly responsible for insuring her health and welfare," Ms. Albright said.
In a speech earlier in the day to Asian Foreign Ministers, Ms. Albright warned that Myanmar is in danger of social collapse. "This is a moment of truth and of urgency for Burma and for all of us concerned about its fate," she said.

HEADLINE: 2d Trial in Killing of Officer Ends With Acquittal

BYLINE: By SUSAN SACHS 

DATELINE: JERSEY CITY, July 27

 BODY:
Vincent James Landano, who paid dearly for a crime he steadfastly denied committing for 22 years, won the battle of his life today when a jury here acquitted him in his second trial for the 1976 murder of a Newark police officer.
Mr. Landano had served 13 years of a life sentence for the killing, was freed after a judge ruled the state had suppressed evidence at the first trial and lived for 9 more years under constant law-enforcement surveillance as prosecutors continued to pursue him. 
 Along the way, he taught himself law and attracted a loyal following of people who believed that he was framed for the murder of Officer John Snow. Among them were a lawyer who devoted thousands of hours to his case for free and a sprightly woman who met him on a visit to prison and married him.
"All I ever asked was for 12 jurors to listen to the facts," said Mr. Landano after the verdict, as he stood on the courthouse steps amid a crowd of jubilant supporters, including one woman who had served on the jury that initially convicted him and said she had regretted it ever since.
The conclusion of Mr. Landano's long roller-coaster ride through the justice system passed swiftly in comparison with his fight for vindication. The trial in Superior Court lasted three weeks. The jury of 10 women and 2 men deliberated just under five hours. When the forewoman softly pronounced the words "not guilty," the graying 58-year-old Mr. Landano thrust his fists in the air and shouted, "All right!"
An instant later, the trial judge, Shirley A. Tolentino, told him he was free to go.
Mr. Landano did not tarry. With his arms wrapped around his wife, Camille, he proclaimed his first worry-free plan. "Right now," he said, grinning, "all I want to do is get as far from this building as I can."
An assistant Hudson County prosecutor, Donald L. Gardner, said the Snow murder would not be pursued further. "This matter, for all intents and purposes, is closed," he said.
He said he accomplished what he set out to do. "We reached a verdict which brings closure to this case," he said.
It did not bring closure to the family of the murdered officer, whose daughter, Kathleen Jensen, broke down and wept in the courtroom as the verdict was announced. She had said she remained convinced that Mr. Landano was her father's killer.
Mr. Landano was an unemployed construction worker with a heroin habit and a three-year stint in Attica state prison for grand larceny when he was charged with shooting Officer Snow in the neck during a robbery of the Hi-Way Check Cashing Service in Kearny, N.J., on Aug. 13, 1976.
At the time of his arrest, two other men were already in custody, both members of a motorcycle gang called "The Breed" that specialized in armed robberies. They cooperated with the state, naming Mr. Landano not only as their accomplice in the robbery, but as the gunman. Officer Snow, who was on duty at the time, was in Kearny unofficially to deliver $46,000 in cash to Hi-Way's management.
From the first, Mr. Landano protested his innocence. He said he had been set up by a childhood friend, Victor Forni, who was described by gang members as the one who planned their crimes.
Mr. Forni, in fact, was charged and pleaded guilty to conspiracy in the Hi-Way robbery. But that only happened later -- after he lost a two-year battle against extradition from New York to New Jersey. In the interim, his old friend Mr. Landano, who was not a gang member and who said he was not even in New Jersey at the time of the robbery, was tried, convicted and sent to jail.
In prison, he told his story to Neil Mullin, a lawyer from Montclair, who eventually became consumed with the Landano case, devoting countless unpaid hours and nearly 20 years to overturning the conviction. In 1989, Mr. Mullin convinced Judge H. Lee Sarokin of the Federal District Court in Newark that the state possessed undisclosed evidence.
In an unprecedented raid, Federal marshals, acting on Judge Sarokin's orders, scoured the files of police departments and the prosecutor's office for all information related to the Snow murder. Based on what was found, including evidence that some of the witnesses were pressured by the police to identify Mr. Landano as the gunman, the judge said the state had "systematically" withheld evidence, and he ordered Mr. Landano released from state prison.
The state fought back. The case ricocheted for another seven years through various courts. By then, however, Mr. Landano's fight to clear his name had acquired another foot soldier.
Andrea Lepore-Kaneko was a juror in the first murder trial in 1978. She said she went along reluctantly with the others in their guilty verdict. "I thought maybe they had understood things I didn't," she said.
But reading Judge Sarokin's decision 11 years later, Mrs. Lepore-Kaneko decided her courtroom misgivings might have had some basis after all. She contacted Mr. Landano and followed him from hearing to hearing. She flew back from Osaka, Japan, where she now lives with her husband, for the second trial.
When the verdict was read, Mrs. Lepore-Kaneko began to weep and tremble. "So relieved! So relieved!" she gasped. "The intensity!"
In the second trial, the star witness was the one Breed gang member who agreed to testify again on behalf of the state -- Allen Roller, who had been convicted of actually robbing the Hi-Way Check Cashing Service and served eight years in prison. Mr. Forni, whom the defense claimed framed Mr. Landano, was subpoenaed by the state and brought to Jersey City. He refused to testify and remains in the Hudson County Jail on contempt charges.
As he has before, Mr. Roller named Mr. Landano as the gunman. But on many other issues, he contradicted his earlier statements. Paul Casteleiro, Mr. Landano's trial lawyer, called him a "misfit" and a "liar."
Mr. Casteleiro, who called no witnesses for the defense, also sought to discredit the state's main piece of evidence, a blue denim hat splattered with Officer Snow's blood and found on the seat of the getaway car. The hat may well have been Mr. Landano's, he said, but it was stolen and planted in the car by the Breed gang as part of their carefully planned crime.
Mr. Gardner, the prosecutor, portrayed the Hi-Way Check Cashing robbers as a gang of bumblers who forgot to take a bag for the money and then got lost in their getaway car as they raced away from the scene. Framing Mr. Landano, he argued in his summation, was beyond their talents.
The jury, however, simply did not believe either the state's hat story or Mr. Roller.
"They kept pushing the hat, the hat, the hat," one of the jurors, Frieda J. Smith, said in a telephone interview after the verdict. "But that didn't make him the killer." There was also general agreement in the jury room, she said, that Mr. Roller lacked credibility.
"Everybody was thinking the same thing," Ms. Smith said. "There were a lot of 'ifs.' "

HEADLINE: Democrats Cast Wary Eye on One of Their Own

BYLINE: By DIRK JOHNSON 

DATELINE: CHICAGO, July 27

 BODY:
After an unbroken 22-year streak of Republican governors here in Illinois, some prominent Democrats are lamenting that Republican views will prevail yet again in this year's race -- even if the Democratic candidate wins.
The Democratic nominee, Representative Glenn Poshard, has faced a storm of criticism from liberals in his party for a starkly conservative voting record on social issues like abortion, gun control and homosexual rights. 
 Worried that liberal voters might sit out the election or, worse, cast a ballot for the Republican nominee, George Ryan, some Democrats have met privately with Mr. Poshard, imploring him to reach out to the party's base.
State Representative Carol Ronen, a Chicagoan who sits on the party's state central committee, met with Mr. Poshard and later sent him a letter urging him to "reassure progressive voters that you are in the mainstream -- not the fringe."
Mr. Poshard's opponent, Mr. Ryan, the Illinois Secretary of State, is certainly no liberal. He is generally opposed to abortion. During the 1970's, when he was a member of the State Legislature, Mr. Ryan was instrumental in defeating the Equal Rights Amendment.
And Mr. Poshard is a liberal on economic matters, a champion of organized labor and a supporter of efforts to raise the Federal minimum wage.
But Mr. Poshard's conservative positions on social issues have given Mr. Ryan a chance to appeal to moderate and liberal voters. Mr. Poshard opposed the ban on assault weapons and supported a bill to forbid same-sex marriages. While Mr. Poshard skipped the recent Gay Pride Parade in Chicago, Mr. Ryan had a car in the parade. Mr. Ryan, who has supported some gun control measures, has also run television advertisements that seek to cast Mr. Poshard as a right-wing extremist on gun issues.
Illinois is a state that tends to elect political moderates as governor -- candidates who favor gun control and abortion rights, and take a pro-business approach, like the current governor, Jim Edgar, and his predecessor, Jim Thompson.
The social conservatism of Mr. Poshard, a five-term Congressman, suits the sensibilities of his rural district, which reaches the tip of Southern Illinois, but riles many voters who live in Chicago and other northern parts of the state.
Politicians in Congress have frequently had a difficult time winning statewide office in Illinois. One politician who was able to make that jump between its southern and northern reaches, former Senator Paul Simon, said Mr. Poshard was "in the process of growing" as he "becomes exposed to the very different perspectives" of residents of the northern part of the state on issues like gun control.
Mr. Simon, who is regarded as something of an archangel among the state's liberals, said Mr. Poshard's stands have typically reflected the views of "a rural region dominated by Southern Baptists."
The former Senator, who once represented that same Southern Illinois district, has endorsed Mr. Poshard's candidacy. "Glenn has a gut commitment to helping people who struggle in our society," he said.
But Mr. Simon also expressed hope that the nominee would "shift his position" on some social issues, given that he would no longer be representing a single conservative region but rather a big, culturally diverse statewide constituency.
Mr. Poshard has shown little willingness to abandon long-held positions on abortion and other issues, however, saying that principle is more important than popularity. He also says that he will not accept PAC money, which means he is likely to be significantly outspent in the campaign by Mr. Ryan.
Backers of Mr. Poshard, even those who disagree with the Democrat on certain issues, say that he is a man of strong convictions and that he would not govern according to the latest public opinion survey.
"He is very much his own man," said Don Johnson, the president of the Illinois A.F.L.-C.I.O., which played a crucial role in Mr. Poshard's triumph in the Democratic primary, as did the fact that it was a divisive three-way race.
In the view of Mr. Johnson, the positions of Mr. Poshard on social issues will not be critical to most voters.
"When our members are going fishing with a friend, or they're out having a beer, they're talking about paychecks and pensions," he said, not social issues.
The labor leader lauded Mr. Poshard's "value system" and his compassion. Mr. Johnson told of Mr. Poshard's concern for poor children at the school in Southern Illinois where Mr. Poshard taught many years ago. Noticing that many children wore skimpy coats during the cold winters, Mr. Poshard started a drive to collect donated coats for them.
"And that was before he ever thought about entering politics," Mr. Johnson said.
It is a side of Mr. Poshard that is little-known among many voters. In recent days, the Democratic candidate has been publicly spurned by some elected members of his own party.
State Representative Larry McKeon, the only openly gay member of the Illinois Legislature, announced earlier this week that he could not endorse Mr. Poshard, who has been cool to measures that would protect gay Illinoisans from discrimination.
Before that, three Democratic mayors of Chicago's southern suburbs announced that they could not endorse Mr. Poshard because he has not favored construction of an airport in Peotone, about 50 miles south of the city. Representative Jesse Jackson Jr. has also withheld his endorsement, citing the airport issue, which he believes would bring development to some struggling towns south of Chicago.
Ms. Ronen, whose district is more than two-thirds Democratic, said she had heard plenty of skepticism about Mr. Poshard among her constituents. She said she remained optimistic that Mr. Poshard would court liberals, but that time was slipping away.
"If he's not doing well in a district that's this strongly Democratic," Ms. Ronen, said, "that does not auger well."
