HEADLINE: A DEAL IS STRUCK FOR COLISEUM SITE

BYLINE: By CHARLES V. BAGLI 

 BODY:
The Metropolitan Transportation Authority is expected to approve the sale tomorrow of the New York Coliseum, one of the most treasured and star-crossed development sites in Manhattan, to Time Warner and the Related Companies for a hotel and residential complex that will also include a world headquarters and digital television studio for Time Warner.
Under the terms of a tentative deal that will go to the M.T.A. board tomorrow for final approval, real estate executives and government officials say the Related-Time Warner partnership will pay about $345 million for the tract on Columbus Circle, at the southwest corner of Central Park. 
 Related plans to build a pair of towers that would stand 750 feet over Columbus Circle and instantly create a soaring landmark for a parcel of real estate that is the gateway to Times Square, the Upper West Side and the luxury hotels on Central Park South.
The 2.1-million-square-foot project would house Time Warner's headquarters and broadcast facilities for the Cable News Network and NY1, as well as a 425-room Mandarin Oriental Hotel, 325 condominiums and a 1,000-seat concert hall for Jazz at Lincoln Center.
The sale would mark another milestone in a tortuous 14-year effort to sell the land, and it would conclude a wary political dance between Gov. George E. Pataki and the M.T.A., which owns the land, and Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's administration, which has effective veto power over any project.
The first attempt to sell the property began during the Edward I. Koch administration in 1985 and ended nine years later amid a recession and a flurry of lawsuits and community opposition. A year ago, the M.T.A. was on the verge of selling the Coliseum site to Millennium Partners when Mayor Giuliani suddenly vowed that he would block the deal unless it included a live theater or performance space.
"It will be a tremendous relief to the city, the state and many New Yorkers to see the disposition of this trophy site finally settled," said Mary Ann Tighe, executive managing director at Insignia/Edward S. Gordon, a real estate company. "The good news about the years of struggle is that it appears that the winner is going to create a structure that reflects Columbus Circle's role as a crossroad of business, entertainment and tourism."
But there is no guarantee that the proponents of the latest plan will fare better than previous efforts. The Related proposal does appear to have the support of some civic groups, including the Municipal Arts Society, which helped block earlier proposals for the largely dormant Coliseum site.
But some local residents are vowing to derail what they consider an overly large project. The Committee for Environmentally Sound Development, a neighborhood group, has already filed a lawsuit in Federal District Court in Manhattan challenging the proposed development on environmental grounds.
"We'll proceed with the lawsuit, an injunction, whatever's necessary," Olive Freud, vice president of the committee, said yesterday. "It's a very sad day. The city's being choked and nobody seems to care. The only people getting anything out of this are the developer and the bondholders."
Deputy Mayor Randy Levine and the M.T.A. declined comment, as did Stephen M. Ross, the chairman of Related Companies, whose Madison Avenue office provides a spectacular view of the Coliseum site, and who, friends say, has long wanted to own the parcel.
E. Virgil Conway, the chairman of the M.T.A., met yesterday afternoon with Mayor Giuliani at City Hall to settle on the winning bidder. The M.T.A.'s selection committee is meeting this morning to review the deal, before making a formal recommendation to the M.T.A. board tomorrow.
In recent months, city officials and the M.T.A. have worked hard to keep their top choices from becoming public knowledge, in hopes of avoiding the kind of delay that occurred a year ago when Mayor Giuliani suddenly blocked the M.T.A.'s plan to sell the site to Millennium after word had filtered out that Millennium was the agency's top choice.
After months of delays, the city and state required developers to include a 100,000-square-foot home for Jazz at Lincoln Center. Bidding by five finalists finally resumed in April, although Millennium had been negotiating with Jazz at Lincoln Center for two years to build a new home at a different location.
During the last week, the Related-Time Warner proposal edged out its four competitors, including Donald J. Trump, the high bidder, who threatened a lawsuit if he did not win. But there was little support for the bulky tower proposed by Mr. Trump, who was offering about $390 million for the land. The real competition was between Millennium, which was planning two luxury hotels, a large ballroom and 450 apartments, and Related, which had the support of City Hall, mainly because of its prominent tenant: Time Warner.
During the bid negotiations of the last four months, Benjamin V. Lambert, the real estate consultant hired by the M.T.A., used Mr. Trump's bid to force Millennium, Related and another bidder, Tishman Speyer Properties, to increase their offers by tens of millions of dollars, according to people involved in the bidding. At the same time, he pushed the bidders to match Millennium's promise to close the deal regardless of any potential litigation, with the aim of guaranteeing that the M.T.A. would get its money.
With Related offering nearly the same contract terms as Millennium, the M.T.A. negotiators eventually came around to the city's choice.
In the coming weeks, Time Warner is expected to ask the city for tax breaks and other incentives, a request that may put the Giuliani administration in a quandary. The city has provided similar incentives to many large corporations, including Time Warner competitors like Disney, Viacom, Bertelsmann and the major television networks. But in May 1997, Mayor Giuliani withdrew a tax break worth $50 million for luxury apartments to be built on the Coliseum site, saying a rebounding real estate market made the subsidies unnecessary.
For many people involved in the disposition of the Coliseum site, the latest deal provoked a sense of having been there before. In 1985, city and state officials agreed to sell the parcel to Mortimer B. Zuckerman's Boston Properties for $477.5 million. In the face of enormous opposition, Mr. Zuckerman reduced the size of his project several times before giving up in 1994.
Mr. Trump was also a losing bidder in 1985, when he proposed what would have been the tallest building in the world, a 137-story tower that resembled a spaceship. Joseph Rose, the chairman of the City Planning Commission, who was involved in the negotiations with Related, was chairman of the local community board in 1985 that reviewed the Zuckerman project. And Related's architect, David Childs at Skidmore, Owings & Merrill, designed Mr. Zuckerman's second, ill-fated tower.
This time around, Mr. Childs may be lucky. Brendan Sexton, president of the Municipal Art Society, whose lawsuits stalled Mr. Zuckerman's plans, said that this time that the developers as well as city and state officials considered urban design and economic development, not just price.
"Assuming Related wins, their proposal has a good design, active public spaces and a tenant that contributes to the life of the city," Mr. Sexton said. "Their design breaks up the bulk of the towers in a way that minimizes the shadows and its impact on the skyline."

HEADLINE: Sports of The Times;
A Pioneer In Her Sport And Beyond

BYLINE: By HARVEY ARATON 

DATELINE: UNIONDALE, N.Y.

 BODY:
THE choice was reaching people or saving lives. Julie Foudy decided there is too big a world out there to confine herself to a single residency. She junked medical school, choosing the life of an explorer over one as a healer.
With the Stanford Medical School administrators calling again to ask whether she'd ever show up, Foudy finally admitted the answer was no. She had interviewed several doctors, including the Olympic softball star, Dr. Dot Richardson. They all spoke of medicine as a calling, not as a career. Mark Adams, the traveling physician for the United States women's soccer team said: "Foudy, let me put it to you this way. I've lived on a golf course for the last five years, and I haven't played once." 
So much for science. Foudy was too good a midfielder, too much a free spirit, and a game that barely registers on the global sports economy had already opened the doors to some remarkably remote places.
She invaded a traditional male sanctum this summer, as a well-received ESPN analyst for the men's World Cup. She made it to China long before Bill Clinton. Last year, she took herself to the village of Sialkot, Pakistan, at the foot of the Himalayas on a one-woman mission to better understand third world labor.
She won an Olympic gold medal before 80,000 in Atlanta and went around the world twice in one year and now looks forward to the world coming to her.
"The most encouraging and exciting thing about the World Cup is the impact it's going to have, and not just here," Foudy said before the United States women's national soccer team defeated China, 2-0, last night on Mia Hamm's 96th and 97th career goals in the second half of the Goodwill Games gold medal match at Mitchel Field.
Foudy is 27 and knows this frequent-flier life cannot last forever, no matter how sympathetic her husband, Ian Sawyers, has been back home in Laguna Niguel, Calif. The women's World Cup will be in the United States next summer, but then there is the matter of the 2000 Olympics in Sydney, Australia. She's planning on going because women's soccer is on the move and Foudy believes there is no turning back.
If out of the next World Cup comes an American women's pro league, that would be splendid, but when Foudy speaks of "impact," she is not necessarily talking about an expanding job market. Having toured the world with the American team since she was 17, she knows that gender equity is a soccer concept that doesn't play beyond our suburban grass roots. Women's soccer is the establishment turned upside down, Americans colonizing the Old World with revolutionary feminist ideas.
"Everyone plays soccer here," Foudy said. "Girls are encouraged. But you travel abroad, and the game is considered a man's world in so many cultures. A girl is considered a freak if she plays. We've been in Spain, and jumped into a men's game and been looked at like we were crazy. Places in Europe, Africa, they're so far behind.
"Five years ago, the Brazil women got no funding and we beat them routinely. Now, they're competitive. It's not just for opportunities in soccer. It's what we can do for girls everywhere with the World Cup."
She has earned the right to express this overview. In March of last year, on her way home from a tournament in Australia, Foudy took a detour deep into Pakistan. She wanted to see for herself how Reebok, the company she had contractually agreed to represent, treated the people making its soccer balls.
Foudy spoke with workers in the factory Reebok built, under pressure, to better monitor child labor. She found out they were earning about $2.25 a day plus benefits, that while conditions might have been better, there are many places where they are much, much worse. At least she took the time to do what Michael Jordan and countless others have not. She went ahead and told people that she believed it was all right to buy the balls, then got back to the business of selling her sport.
League or no league, there is no pot of gold at the end of the soccer rainbow, not even for Hamm, whose five goals in two Goodwill Games were four more than all the United States men scored in the World Cup. Hamm, Foudy, Michelle Akers and Christine Lilly made a world power out of a country whose men made it a soccer laughingstock. The crowd of 11,307 last night was the largest for any Goodwill Games event. There must be a moral in there. A legacy. When Reebok last year named a soccer shoe for Foudy, that's what they called it. The Foudy Legacy.

HEADLINE: NEWS SUMMARY

 BODY:

INTERNATIONAL A3-7
 
China Leader Imposing New Controls on Military
President Jiang Zemin of China publicly ordered the armed forces to relinquish all of their many business roles, ostensibly to combat smuggling, as the country faces a transformation that aims to separate military consumers of weaponry from civilian producers and to create a fighting force controlled by generals allied with the President. A1 

China Arrests Anger Albright
Secretary of State Madeline K. Albright lodged a protest with the Chinese Foreign Minister over recent arrests of political dissidents, a development that other Administration officials said had embarrassed the United States so soon after President Clinton's visit to China. A6
 
Counting Votes in Cambodia
International observers expressed cautious optimism over the conduct of Cambodia's parliamentary election, as villagers and townspeople tallied Sunday's ballots. Results are not expected until Saturday. A7
 
Inspectors Remain Wary of Iraq
The International Atomic Energy Agency said in a report to the Security Council that it had found no evidence that Iraq had nuclear weapons, but it left open the possibility that Iraq had hidden the necessary expertise and material for future use. A3
 
Serbian Gains in Kosovo
Government forces continued their intensive attacks against separatist guerrillas throughout Kosovo, Serbian officials said, with soldiers taking stretches of major roads that had been held by the rebels. The Government attacks threaten Western efforts to stop the war through negotiations, diplomats said. A3
 
Russian Stocks Plunge Again
Russian stock prices dropped 9.1 percent, to about the level they were at before the International Monetary Fund agreed to prop up the Government's finances. The continuing slide of the Russian market indicated that investors still doubted whether the Government could make good on a pledge to reduce debt. A5
 
Perilous Time for U.N. Workers
An Italian expert in famine relief was killed in Burundi, the sixth worker in the World Food Program to die this year. The United Nations also said that three unarmed military observers and their translator were killed in Tajikistan this month. All told, the United Nations said, more civilians than soldiers have been killed this year in missions around the world. A4
 
Three Nuns Slain in Yemen
Three nuns of Mother Teresa's order were shot and killed as they left a clinic for the disabled in western Yemen, officials said. Police arrested a 25-year-old suspect, who they said had fought in Bosnia and Afghanistan. They described him as unstable and said he had been treated at the clinic. The Missionaries of Charity order has been in Yemen since 1970. (AP)
 
NATIONAL A8-13
 
Lewinsky and Prosecutors Meet to Explore a Deal
Prosecutors met with Monica S. Lewinsky in New York in a possible prelude to an immunity deal that would secure Ms. Lewinsky's grand jury testimony. In Washington, a Federal appeals court rejected the President's invocation of lawyer-client privilege to bar the testimony of his closest aide, Bruce Lindsey. And Mr. Clinton's private lawyers continued negotiations with the independent counsel Kenneth W. Starr over the subpoenaing of the President. A1
 
Justice Department Rebuff
The Justice Department refused to let a House committee see two internal memorandums in which the F.B.I. Director and another high-ranking official urged the appointment of an independent counsel to investigate fund-raising practices in President Clinton's re-election campaign. A9
 
Was White House First Target?
Federal law-enforcement officers are investigating whether Russell E. Weston Jr., who is accused of killing two Capitol police officers on Friday, first sought to attack the White House, officials said. A12
 
Mr. Weston's father expressed sorrow for the violent rampage. His son, who remained hospitalized with gunshot wounds, was charged in Federal court with murder. The bodies of the two officers will lie in flag-draped coffins in the Great Rotunda today for a special memorial service. A12
 
Clinton Warms to Markets Idea
President Clinton said he was seriously considering relying on the financial markets to help solve Social Security's long-term problems, but he said he had not made up his mind about any of the details. A9
 
New Fraternization Policy
The Department of Defense plans to order the Army to adopt strict new prohibitions on dating, sexual intimacy and other close relationships between officers and enlisted personnel, officials say. A8
 
Guilty Plea in C.I.A. Case
A former C.I.A. officer, Douglas F. Groat, pleaded guilty to trying to extort $1 million from the agency in exchange for refraining from giving away Government secrets. Prosecutors dropped espionage charges against Mr. Groat. A8
 
Teamsters Leader Ordered Out
A court-appointed review board expelled Ron Carey, the teamsters president, from the union, apparently ending the career of a labor leader who once seemed destined to clean up one of the nation's largest but most corrupt unions. A1
 
Human Testing of Pesticides
Pesticide companies have conducted tests on humans and told the Government that they expect to do more, saying the tests may provide more reliable data than animal tests and may justify less stringent rules on pesticides. An environmental group called on the E.P.A. to impose a moratorium on such testing. A9
 
NEW YORK/REGION B1-12
 
Sale of Coliseum To Media Conglomerate
The M.T.A. is expected to approve the sale of the New York Coliseum to Time Warner and the Related Companies for a complex that will include a headquarters, television studio, hotel and residences. A1
 
Acquitted, After 13 Years in Jail
Vincent James Landano, who served 13 years for the 1976 murder of a Newark police officer, was acquitted by a jury in his retrial. B1
 
Arrest Fuels Parole Debate
A 17-year-old ex-convict accused of shooting a police officer was captured, and Mayor Giuliani cited his criminal record as an example of why the parole system should be changed. B1
 
Times Square Reopens
Times Square was reopened to traffic, but optimism at the pace of work on the damaged scaffolding at the Conde Nast building gave way to renewed safety concerns when a 15-foot-long aluminum pipe fell onto a theater roof next door. No one was injured. B3
 
Appeal to Brawley Jury
The lawyer for the Rev. Al Sharpton pleaded with jurors in the Tawana Brawley defamation case not to punish Mr. Sharpton with a large damage award, which he compared to a financial death sentence. B4
 
OBITUARIES A16
 
ARTS E1-8
 
Ticket-Selling Investigation
The New York State Attorney General's office is near concluding an investigation into ticket-selling practices involving the biggest theaters on Broadway and the most powerful entertainment and sports venues. E1
 
FASHION B7
 
SPORTS C1-8
 
U.S. Track Athletes Suspended
The world governing body of track and field said two of America's top athletes, Dennis Mitchell and Randy Barnes, had been suspended for possible doping offenses. But the United States track and field federation will not enforce the suspensions, calling the allegations unproven. C1
 
BUSINESS DAY D1-22
 
Telecommunications Deal
Bell Atlantic is expected to announce today its acquisition of GTE, the nation's largest independent local and long-distance phone company, for $52.8 billion in stock. A1
 
Reinsurance Consolidation
Swiss Reinsurance said it would acquire Life Re for $95 a share, or about $1.8 billion in cash, furthering a consolidation that has recently accelerated in the reinsurance industry. D1
 
Looking Toward a G.M. Ruling
Talks to end two G.M. strikes continued as union officials, in particular, had an incentive to settle before an arbitration ruling on the strikes' legality, possibly tomorrow. D2
 
Market Maker Fined $950,000
The N.A.S.D.'s regulatory arm censured and fined Troster Singer for fraud in connection with a series of 28 intentional Nasdaq trade-reporting violations during 1994. The firm neither admitted nor denied the findings, but agreed to pay $950,000. Six employees were sanctioned and fined a total of $100,000. D8

ZELNIKER-Arthur, 78. On July 26, 1998. Loving and beloved husband of Elaine. Devoted father of Mark and Ilene Zelniker and Jan and Jeffrey Scherr. Cherished grandfather of Lindsay, Jordan, Brian, Jeremy & Robert. Caring brother of Sheila and Sidney Hammer and Rhoda and Irving Spielvogel. Founder & President of Econoco Corporation, he was greatly admired & a giant in the store fixture industry. Former President of Fresh Meadow Country Club. He will be sorely missed by his many friends and family. Contributions may be made to the American Cancer Society, 400 Post Avenue, Suite 201, Westbury, New York 11590. Funeral services were held Tuesday, July 28, 1998.

HEADLINE: $25 AND UNDER;
On the Upper West Side, Something French in the Air

BYLINE: By Eric Asimov 

 BODY:
AVENUE, which opened six weeks ago in the pleasant corner site formerly occupied by Joe's Fish Shack, offers a little self-serve breakfast in the mornings with flaky croissants, granola and yogurt, house-smoked salmon platters and fresh-squeezed juices. And if you want something more substantial, a waiter will bring you a huge, sweet, Austrian apple pancake, with warmed maple syrup ($8.95).
In the afternoons, you can choose from attractive sandwiches and salads like chicken curry with Granny Smith apples ($4.50) and haricots verts with fennel and potato crisps ($4.25). At night, Avenue becomes a full-service restaurant, offering a French-American hybrid menu prepared by Scott Campbell, the chef and an owner, who is formerly of Vince & Eddie's near Lincoln Center. 
 Avenue clearly wants to offer something for everyone. The delight is that so much of the food is so good, and such a good value. Even at night, Avenue has toyed with the traditional menu, dividing appetizers into small, medium and large plates. In fact, the difference between small and medium has nothing to do with size, but rather with the costliness of the ingredients.
A decade ago, Mr. Campbell was one of many chefs who responded to recession by scaling back on the elaborate fare of the mid-1980's. Instead, they offered roast chicken and mashed potatoes, maternal comforts for a traumatized clientele.
At Avenue, Mr. Campbell has stepped gingerly away from those homey foods, staying with familiar flavors but dressing them a little more stylishly. His meat dishes are especially good, like an astounding smoked loin of pork, a portion the size of half a football, which has absorbed the enticing aroma of hickory but remains tender and excellent, served on a bed of asparagus and morels. This is a huge portion of food for $14.95.
Another main course, slices of tender lamb crusted with rosemary ($14.95), is almost its equal. The lamb is served with potatoes Louis, a rich and delicious potato cake cooked in duck fat, a homage to the famous potato dish served at L'Ami Louis in Paris. Sliced steak with crisp, salty fried potatoes ($16.95) is formidable as well.
Mr. Campbell does other fine things, too. His risotto with morels, asparagus and sweet peas ($12.95) is creamy and superb. It is classified as a medium plate, and the portion I had was plenty for a main course. Asked to prepare a vegetarian entree for a friend, he might have offered a perfunctory platter of grilled vegetables, as many restaurants do. Instead, his offering was an artful weaving of grilled fennel, asparagus, tomatoes, peppers and more ($10.50), which showed thought and care.
Not everything works so well, though. Grilled salmon ($16.25), a nice piece of fish, came with a sweet and delicious pea coulis. The problem was the unnecessary layer of duxelles interposed between the fish and the coulis, obscuring the otherwise clear interplay of flavors.
But this was the exception. Appetizers included a terrific special of cold lobster bisque ($5.50), a broth tasting of lobster shells and seashores with a dollop of creme fraiche, which slowly dissolved, adding a velvet creaminess to the blend. A flaky little tart of leeks and Gruyere ($7.50) came with a small green salad -- it would make a perfect light lunch. Fried calamari ($7.95) is delicate yet crisp, served with a lemongrass dipping sauce.
While the menu is not strictly French, the atmosphere is tres Francais, with crisp, efficient service. The wine list is also French, which probably accounts for the high prices; it's tough to find a bottle under $25. One within that limit is a fruity 1995 Chinon from Alain Lorieux ($24), a rare red that is delightful chilled. Listing this wine from the Loire as a Burgundy, however, indicates a lack of care in putting together the list.
There is no such laxity in the dessert list, though. Walnut tart ($5.95) is wonderful, crusty and not too sweet, served with maple ice cream. A dense chocolate cake with mint ice cream ($5.95) and a berry cobbler ($5.95) are excellent, too.
The Upper West Side is notorious as a restaurant wasteland, but the early signs here are all good. From the terrific food to the daylong service and the good values, Avenue is a formula that works.
 
Avenue
 
520 Columbus Avenue, at 85th Street, (212) 579-3194.
BEST DISHES: Fried calamari; leek and Gruyere tartlet; risotto with morels, asparagus and peas; lobster bisque; house-smoked loin of pork; sliced leg of lamb; sliced steak; walnut tart; chocolate cake; berry cobbler.
PRICE RANGE: Two classes of appetizers: first, $5.50 to $8.95, and second, $9.95 to $14; main courses, $13.95 to $16.95.

WHEELCHAIR ACCESS: Everything is on one level; restrooms are narrow.

HEADLINE: AT LUNCH WITH: Malachy McCourt -- How a Rogue Turns Himself Into a Saint;
The Blarney Fails to Hide an Emotional Directness

BYLINE: By ALEX WITCHEL 

 BODY:
Malachy McCourt says his railing days are over.
"I had a murderous rage in my heart of Limerick, the humiliation of coming out of the slums," he says of his hometown in Ireland, the setting of his brother Frank McCourt's Pulitzer Prize-winning memoir, "Angela's Ashes." "It made you feel like nothing and there was no place to go but down. It was assumed we'd be low-class the rest of our lives. But who can you blame? Governments and churches that are gone now? It's useless. Let those things live rent-free in your head and you'll be a lunatic. Resentment is like taking poison and waiting for the other person to die." 
 These are good days for Mr. McCourt, 66, whose own memoir, "A Monk Swimming," has spent six weeks on the New York Times best-seller list. Not bad for a man who has had careers as an actor, radio personality and bar owner. His book starts in 1952 with his arrival in New York at the age of 20, when he sold Bibles on Fire Island, ran the first singles bar in Manhattan (named after himself), smuggled gold to India, drank, womanized and raged his way through life as if sheer will and excess alone could make up for every childhood hurt. His writing style is brashly confident, his irreverent brand of storytelling laced with dark humor and sometimes pathos.
But it hasn't taken long for "the ubiquitous they" as Mr. McCourt calls the press and other hangers-on, to snipe at him for trying to cash in on the success of "Angela's Ashes" and readers' insatiable interest in the McCourt clan. Mr. McCourt couldn't care less.
"They say what I did was ego-driven and name-dropping," he says. "Damn right it is. Who am I going to write about? People I didn't meet?"
It seems impossible to imagine anyone left in that category. At Eamonn Doran on Second Avenue at 53d Street in Manhattan, where he is a regular, he greets everyone by name, settling into a booth beneath his picture with President Clinton. "I was toastmaster when he was named Irishman of the Year at the Waldorf," he says proudly. The photograph's inscription reads, "Blessings on all at Eamonn's from my pal Bill and myself, Malachy."
The chef comes from the kitchen to deliver Mr. McCourt's pot of tea personally as he signals the waitress for menus. "Would you bring me some water, love?" he asks in a tone just familiar enough to make her blush.
Yes, he's still a charmer after all these years, a fact of which he's well aware. His blue eyes are clear and smart, thatched with shaggy white brows from the Brothers Grimm. His wrists are as thick as his hands, a reminder of his days as a longshoreman. His picture on the back cover of his book shows a handsome young man, or in the parlance of America, a "cute" one. A term to which he says he at first took offense. "Cute in Ireland meant cunning and devious," he said, then smiled. "And I'm not sure that I'm not."
But past his well-honed blarney shtick, which he shines on and off again at whim, the gratifying thing about Mr. McCourt is that he can drop his professional character act and segue into a smart, emotionally direct conversation faster than you can say "Top o' the morning."
 
Milk of Kindness, Slums of Death
 His double layers of personality seem to echo what he says about Ireland itself. Talking of the period covered in the book, he says, "That was a tumultuous time for me. I was stepping into a new life, casting off the rigidity of the church and its smothering society. The corruption and degradation covered with Irish oak. This image of a pink-cheeked mother offering cups of milk and peace and happiness wherever you go, with the road rising to meet you, and there in the slums they were dying of disease, despair and depression."
The waitress brings the water, and Mr. McCourt orders filet of sole, since he's become a vegetarian. He quit smoking 10 years ago, drinking 13 years ago. "Just sainthood now," he says dryly.
Which is the polar opposite of himself as a young adult. "All of a sudden here am I, a stripling, so freeing," he says of coming to New York, where people "trusted what I appeared to be, didn't know what a messed-up fellow I was in my head. I left school at age 13. I was illiterate with no role models. The great psychobabble today is the dysfunctional family. Well, I've never met one that was functional. In Limerick, a family that was dysfunctional was one who could afford to drink but didn't."
"I had the taste of the alcohol since I was 11," he goes on, sipping tea. "It allowed me to be clever, charming and to behave outrageously. Acting also allowed me not to be me. So I could indulge every fantasy in this paradise of America."
Every paradise has its price, and so did Mr. McCourt's. He married Linda Wachsman and they had two children, Siobhan and Malachy, which did not stop Mr. McCourt's drinking and carousing. Divorce was inevitable. He met Diana Galin in 1963, and they have been married 33 years. They have two sons, Conor and Cormac, and Mr. McCourt is stepfather to her daughter, Nina. Through the years he acted in daytime television dramas, including "Ryan's Hope" and "Search for Tomorrow," had successful shows on talk radio and tried his hand at opening more bars. "I'm a rotten businessman," he admits. "I've closed more saloons than any alcoholic." He has worked often in the theater, sometimes with his brother Frank, performing the musical revue they wrote together, "A Couple of Blaguards," about their childhood.
But as he says: "At a certain age the lights begin to go out as an actor. I was not getting work, I was broke, having never held a steady job, really. When I told Frank I was thinking about writing a book, he said great." His smile is rueful. "But the money's all gone," he says of his advance. "I gave it to my kids. You can't keep it unless you give it away."
His daughter Siobhan lives in Boston with her two children. Malachy teaches diving in Bali. Nina, who is retarded, lives in a group home in New York. Conor is a filmmaker whose documentary "The McCourts of Limerick" was on Cinemax. He is also a police sergeant in Manhattan, where Cormac teaches integral yoga.
Their mother is studying for a master's degree in community economic development. "And she puts up with me," Mr. McCourt says gratefully.
Mr. McCourt's other favorite person is his brother Frank. "He's an amazing man," he says. "When he was 12, one of our schoolmasters said: 'My boy, you are a literary genius. My strong suggestion is to go to America. They will appreciate you there.' Over the years I've read what he's written that never got published, and I always said it still holds. He is a literary genius. Also the most nonjudgmental decent guy. He forgives."
Has he read "A Monk Swimming," whose title comes from Mr. McCourt's mishearing the Hail Mary's "Blessed art thou amongst women" as "Blessed art thou, a monk swimming"?
"He's writing now, so he hasn't read it," he says. "He told me: 'I can't be hearing your voice. For years it's been very loud.' "
And he laughs, though he admits he is still not free of the long-reaching shadows of Limerick. He and Frank were born in Brooklyn, but the family returned to Ireland when Malachy was 3, after the death of his 7-week-old sister, Margaret. His father drank and could not hold a job; the McCourts lived in desperate poverty both in New York and Ireland. His twin brothers also died of illness. Eventually his father left them.
"When desertion is involved, the absent parent seems romantic and caring," Mr. McCourt says. His mother, Angela, "was in a depression," he says. "My father had taken to the alcohol, and she was helpless in the face of it. My mother wanted to go back to Ireland after Margaret died. And then she was thrown out of one slum and only found shelter in another with a drunken beast of a thing who would sit tearing at a steak with his bare hands and throw meat to the dog while we children sat hungry. Then he beats us and sleeps with my mother so we could hear it. The horrors of that, the shame. There was a sadness of that for years and years. I once wrote to her and said I loved her. She laughed at it. She couldn't accept that.
"Coming out of that life, the things that get you are the two evils of shame on one shoulder, the demon fear on the other. Shame says you came from nothing, you're nobody, they'll find you out for what you and your mother have done. Fear says what's the use of bothering, drink as much as you can, dull the pain. As a result shame takes care of the past, fear takes care of the future and there's no living in the present." He sighs. "The thing about the low self-esteem rubbish is thinking you have no gifts. Eventually I became my father, who I hated the most."
There has been some healing, though. The success of both his book and his brother's has helped, of course. And while Conor McCourt was researching his documentary, he located the grave in Queens where Margaret was buried.
"We all went out, Frank, Alphie, Mike and myself," Mr. McCourt says, referring to his surviving brothers. "It was a pauper's grave. My wife, Diana, had decided to keep some of Angela's ashes in our house, even after we had scattered them. But when Conor found the grave, we brought the ashes and reunited the mother and the daughter after 64 years." His eyes fill. "It still makes me weep to think about it. The recognition that there was something of that child. We honored her and we honored my mother."
He starts on his third pot of tea, and soon enough the talk returns to Ireland. "I picked up an old man on the road once," Mr. McCourt says, "during the hardest driving rain. When he got out he said: 'Thank you sir for your kindness. May you have a happy death.' "
He smiles. "When you think about it, a happy death means you had a happy life. And I think I have. These days, the simple things are appealing. Diana, our closeness and love. The grandchildren. I'm having my dream, one day at a time. I've learned acceptance and letting go and to just keep a sense of humor about this absurd condition."
Which is?
"That we're a species with a hundred percent mortality rate and not one of us accepts it. So, it's true. Live every day as if it's going to be your last, and one day, you'll be right."

E1); Angela McCourt with three of her sons, Mike, at left, Malachy and Frank, in a family photo. (Cinemax) (pg. E3)

HEADLINE: Business Travel;
Business Air Fares Are Holding Steady

BYLINE: By EDWIN McDOWELL 

 BODY:
Don't look now, but typical business air fares finally appear to be holding steady. The reasons why are a matter of some dispute. But after having to contend with steadily rising fares from 1995 through 1997, as the airline industry struggled to return to financial health after its $14 billion hemorrhage in the first half of the decade, business travelers are likely to welcome any leveling off, whatever the reasons.
The most recent evidence of stability is contained in the American Express domestic air fare index, which shows that from October 1997 through May of this year, typical one-way business fares rose only 1 percent, to $452. In contrast, those fares climbed 16 percent in 1997 over the previous year. 
 In its first-ever domestic air fare report, released a week ago, the Air Transport Association found that average ticket prices over all (that is, business and leisure fares combined) increased last month only 2.2 percent over fares in June 1997. So far this year, the association added, average fares remained relatively unchanged, increasing only 1.7 percent for a 1,000-mile one-way trip, to $141.80 from $139.38.
In an obvious dig at American Express, which has compiled its fares index for eight years, the association said its report "is based on actual prices paid by the vast majority of the flying public," rather than relying "on estimates or only on tickets purchased by customers of a particular company or on a limited number of routes."
Melissa Abernathy, manager of public affairs for American Express, shrugged off the implied criticism. "In all the years we've put out the index, we haven't heard a word out of them." she said of the association.
Differences aside, there is general agreement that stable fares are not the result of a single event, but a combination of factors. Corporate travel managers cite the success of negotiated discount fares and corporate policies that impose restrictions on everything from what carriers to fly to flight times to routes and even to preferred airports. But the main reason, many business travelers say, is that the airlines are almost certainly responding to election-year pressures from Washington, which is hinting that it may reregulate some air fares.
"The fact that pricing suddenly stopped escalating, that's suspicious," said James B. Haynes, president of the Atlanta-based Commercial Travelers Association.
The carriers have another explanation, obviously, including lower-priced aviation fuel, improved productivity, lower interest rates for leases on planes and, of course, competition.
 
Airline Update
On Saturday, members of the frequent-flier programs of AMR's American Airlines and U S Airways can begin claiming awards for travel on either airline. And at a date to be announced "in the near future," the carriers said yesterday, members of both carriers' programs will be able to combine miles from each program in order to claim awards on either airline.
Because of hot temperatures in many cities, Delta Air Lines will not check live animals as baggage through Sept. 15. Passengers who had planned to fly with their pets during that period can cancel their reservations without penalties.
 
Third Time Around
Although it's too late to book passage on tomorrow's maiden voyage of the Disney Magic, the Walt Disney Company's twice-delayed ship, the company said limited berths were still available for most of the Magic's cruises in August. For September and later, there is ample space on the ship's weekly sailings from Port Canaveral, Fla.
All sailings include three or four days at Walt Disney World Resort followed by a three- or four-day Bahamas cruise.

HEADLINE: Journal;
This Way Lies Madness

BYLINE: By FRANK RICH 

 BODY:
The Capitol police officers Jacob Chestnut and John Gibson had hardly been declared dead when Senator Robert Torricelli, the New Jersey Democrat, sent out a press release arguing that tighter gun control could have prevented the tragedy. Not missing a beat, Trent Lott was soon arguing that a $125 million bunker-barricade camouflaged as a visitors' center would repel future assailants. But in a city where most politicians are so ignorant about mental illness that they still think Whitewater, not the disease of depression, drove Vincent Foster to suicide, no one said the obvious: It is the gaping cracks in American mental-health care, not in Capitol security or gun-control laws, that most clearly delivered Russell Weston Jr. to his rendezvous with history.
Mr. Weston's paranoid schizophrenia surfaced long ago. Yet, as The Times reported, this now 41-year-old man "received no regular psychiatric treatment or medication over the last two decades and his family seemed to understand little about how to seek help for him." This is hardly an anomaly. E. Fuller Torrey, a psychiatrist who campaigns for better mental-health care through the Treatment Advocacy Center in Arlington, Va., says that of the 2 to 2.5 million Americans with schizophrenia, "40 percent are not receiving treatment on any given day." Cases like Mr. Weston's -- in which a mental patient eludes follow-up care and medication after a hospital release -- number "in the hundreds of thousands." 
 How does this happen? Nearly as heartbreaking as the preventable murders of officers Chestnut and Gibson is the plight of Mr. Weston's family. They obviously love their child; they knew he was sick; they wanted to get him help. But, as Russell Sr. said: "He was a grown man. We couldn't hold him down and force the pills into him." A comprehensive system of mental-health services, including support for parents with sick adult children who refuse treatment, doesn't exist. If it had, the Westons might have had more success in rescuing their son -- as might the equally loving family of Michael Laudor, the Yale Law School prodigy charged last month with murdering his fiancee.
That safety-net system doesn't exist because mental illness is still in our culture's shadows -- stigmatized, misunderstood and therefore the beggar of American health care. Though Mr. Weston's home state of Montana offers particularly skimpy services, the national baseline is "not high," says Dr. Torrey. Poorly covered by health insurance and spottily served by overcrowded and underfinanced public institutions, mental illness is "the last discrimination," as Michael Faenza of the National Mental Health Association puts it, even though we now have the science to treat mental illness at a success rate comparable to physical illness.
It's not only politicians who are complicit in this discrimination. The media sometimes compound the ignorance that feeds it. Too many commentators look at Mr. Weston's symptoms -- such as his paranoid delusions about the C.I.A. -- and lump him in with gun-toting, anti-government ideologues, making no distinction between the clinically ill and political extremists. A Time reporter, on the hapless CNN show "Newsstand," expressed surprise that Mr. Weston would so easily be diagnosed as a paranoid schizophrenic given that he had no previous "episodes of violence."
In fact, the majority of those ill with paranoid schizophrenia are not violent, and the disease has no ideology. As Sylvia Nasar's new book, "A Beautiful Mind," documents, many of Mr. Weston's oddest symptoms (including the conviction he was being beamed encrypted messages) also characterized the paranoid schizophrenia of John Nash, the brilliant, nonviolent Princeton mathematician who won the Nobel Prize in Economics in 1994.
Back in 1835, one of the very first patients at Washington's Government Hospital for the Insane -- as St. Elizabeth's Hospital was then known -- was Richard Lawrence, a pistol-armed man who tried and failed to assassinate Andrew Jackson in the Capitol's Rotunda and was then pronounced not guilty by reason of insanity in a trial whose jury deliberations took five minutes. More than a century and a half of medical and economic advances later, what kind of progress is it that we still so often fail to treat the mentally ill until after tragedy strikes?

HEADLINE: Foreign Investors Need Good News, Top Russian Official Says

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES 

DATELINE: MOSCOW, July 28

 BODY:
An architect of Russia's fiscal strategy said today that foreign investors needed some good news to stem renewed fears that the Government was losing control of its financial destiny, a belief that has driven stock and bond markets to near-record lows this week.
The official, Deputy Prime Minister Boris Nemtsov, said some important indicators were beginning to move up. He added that the Government would soon begin to issue regular and detailed economic reports to investors that would prove his point. 
 In an interview Mr. Nemtsov said the markets' slide had mostly stemmed from a "public relations problem" caused by a vacuum of reliable information about the recovery.
The tax-collection rate, a crucial indicator of the progress toward solvency, will show a modest increase this month, Mr. Nemtsov said, adding that the Government would issue policies this week aimed at stabilizing the value of the ruble and laying out plans to issue new debt.
In particular, he stressed that the Government would fight any drop in the value of the ruble in relation to other currencies. Devaluation would raise prices domestically and slash the value of foreign investments.
"There will be no devaluation," Mr. Nemtsov said, adding confidently, if jokingly, "Devaluation will happen with other governments, not ours."
Russian financial markets have been on a stomach-churning ride since May, when it became clear that the Government was headed toward insolvency unless international lending agencies stepped in to bolster currency reserves and calm investors who were pulling their money out of the country.
The International Monetary Fund voted on July 13 to add immediately $4.8 billion to the gold and currency reserves of Russia after President Boris N. Yeltsin had decreed a sweeping overhaul of tax and spending policies. The I.M.F. and other lending agencies have promised to increase the package to a total of $22.6 billion if the Government carries out its new policies.
In the days after that announcement, the Russian stock market leaped more than 25 percent, and short-term interest rates dropped from as much as 150 percent to 50 percent. But little has happened since then, in part because the Parliament, a stumbling block to the full array of reforms, will not take up some of the most important changes until the middle of next month.
In the meantime the stock market has given up all its gains. The price of one much-watched Government bond, a repackaged version of international debt run up by the old Soviet Union, has hit record lows. And today the rate on the Russian version of an American Treasury bill neared 80 percent, up 30 points from a few weeks ago.
Blue-chip stocks regained 5 percent of their value today, after six days in a row of losses that had sliced 25 percent from the most popular index of the market's value.
Mr. Nemtsov said today that investors were overlooking promising aspects of the economy, including industrial sectors like automobile production, that are registering large gains in output and a major reduction in strikes. He also noted that the Government was drafting plans to sell vast segments of state-owned companies like oil and telecommunications, in an effort to encourage competition and net the Government a healthy profit.
Mr. Nemtsov noted in particular the Government decision this week to sell nearly one-seventh of its 41 percent stake in Gazprom, the natural-gas monopoly that is the largest company in Russia. Investors have reacted skeptically. Gazprom shares have fallen more than a third in the last two weeks.
Mr. Nemtsov said today that it generally made economic sense to shed state interests in private companies and that the Government would not sell part of its Gazprom stake until the stock price had recovered.
"What Russia needs, what the Government needs, is a very transparent policy to explain to everyone what is happening" on economic issues in the executive branch and the Parliament, he said.
The Government hopes to begin issuing weekly reports on important economic issues, including statistics on tax collection, currency and markets.
A leading analyst of Russian economic issues, Charles Blitzer, director of emerging-market research at the investment bank of Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette Inc., said he agreed that foreign investors had overreacted to the dearth of good news.
"Basically," Mr. Blitzer said, "the sentiment is negative, and the market is sort of lost confidence in the Government and in a whole string of positive actions the Government is taking, selling off part of Gazprom, cracking down on tax evasion and so on. The Government hasn't done a very good job of getting its information across."

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Ex-Im Bank Chief, in Asia, Urges Priority on China Lending

BYLINE: By MARK LANDLER 

DATELINE: HONG KONG, July 28

 BODY:
In a sign of the new emphasis the United States is placing on China as an economic engine for Asia, the chairman of the Export-Import Bank says he wants China to become its largest recipient of loans to buy American products and equipment.
The official, James A. Harmon, promised to push for an increase in lending to China, which currently has loans and trade guarantees of $5.5 billion, and is the Ex-Im Bank's second-largest borrower after Mexico. 
 "China is a phenomenal opportunity for U.S. companies and a great challenge," Mr. Harmon said here on Monday, adding, "It should be our No. 1 market." He was in Hong Kong as part of a 12-day tour of Asia that includes stops in Japan and Thailand. But it was clear that his focus was squarely on China, where he said he hoped to seize upon the momentum established by President Clinton's recent visit. Of the 12 days, he is spending nine in China, with visits to Beijing, Shanghai and the industrial city of Chengdu.
The Ex-Im Bank is an independent United States Government agency that promotes exports by subsidizing loans and providing credit guarantees so that developing countries can purchase American goods. In China this year, the bank will help finance about $1 billion worth of purchases, primarily aircraft and power turbines. Mr. Harmon said he would like to extend loans for the Chinese to buy everything from medical equipment to water-filtration systems.
He predicted that China would be hungry for loans, as it starts a campaign, orchestrated by Prime Minister Zhu Rongji, to spend $750 billion on new roads, bridges, hydroelectric projects and other public works.
Mr. Harmon said he also wanted to redress China's trade imbalance with the United States, which ballooned to $20.4 billion in the first five months of this year, compared with $16.9 billion in the similar period last year. An aide to Mr. Harmon said that during meetings in Beijing, senior Chinese officials acknowledged that the trade gap was a problem and pledged to help close it.
The Ex-Im Bank also wants to mend relations with Beijing, which were strained in 1996 when it refused to subsidize loans to buy equipment for the huge Three Gorges Dam, saying the $25 billion project did not meet its environmental standards.
Mr. Harmon's intense focus on China is in marked contrast to that on Japan, where he spent only a day, despite extensive ties between the Ex-Im Bank and its counterpart in Japan. He said he tried to persuade officials there to consider environmental standards in deciding which countries should get loans.
Economists said the Ex-Im Bank's emphasis on China made sense, given that it is one of the few Asian countries still showing growth. "If you want to save Asia, you'd better save China first," said William H. Overholt, the head of research at BankBoston in Singapore.
Indeed, the situations in other Asian nations worry the bank. It has lent several billion dollars to troubled economies like those of Thailand, South Korea and Indonesia. Given their suffocating debt and economic turbulence, some of them might have trouble paying back their loans on time. A spokesman for the bank acknowledged that it might have to stretch out the repayment period, particularly with Indonesia. But he said no Asian country had yet signaled it would default on an Ex-Im Bank loan, adding, "A rescheduling is not a loss; it is a stretching out of a repayment."
Mr. Harmon, who was a New York investment banker before joining the Ex-Im Bank in 1997, said he believed that Asia would take three to five years to recover from its economic crisis. "A lot of people in Asia haven't lived through bear markets," he said, "so it's harder for people here."

HEADLINE: Confidence Index Hovers Near Record

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 28

 BODY:
Consumer confidence in the United States economy stuck close to a 30-year high in July, buoyed by the powerful combination of benign inflation and an unemployment rate close to a three-decade low.
The Conference Board, a private research group, reported that its index of consumer confidence fell to 135.4 this month from a revised 138.2 in June. Last month's original estimate of 137.6 was its highest level since a reading of 142.3 in October 1968. Before the report, analysts expected a July index of 135.9. 
 Near-record levels of confidence could lead to a burst of spending, and that would increase United States economic growth, because consumer activity makes up two-thirds of the economy.
The indexes that gauge plans to buy a home, automobile or major appliance over the next six months all rose in July. And more respondents than last month expect their incomes to rise in the next six months. The index showing expectations for higher incomes rose to 27.1 in July from 26.6 in June.
"While consumers are not expecting economic growth to accelerate, they are looking for business growth to continue through the remainder of 1998," said Lynn Franco, associate director of the Conference Board's Consumer Research Center.
At the same, more survey participants said this month that they expected that fewer jobs would be created in the next six months.

HEADLINE: William McChesney Martin, 91, Dies; Defined Fed's Role

BYLINE: By MELODY PETERSEN 

 BODY:
William McChesney Martin Jr., the chairman of the Federal Reserve for 19 years, spanning five Administrations, from that of Harry S. Truman to Richard M. Nixon, died on Monday at his home in Washington. He was 91.
Mr. Martin, a plain-spoken Democrat from Missouri, detested inflation. He once said that the role of the Federal Reserve was "to take away the punch bowl just when the party gets going." 
 While he was criticized alternately for making credit too easy and too tight, Mr. Martin was known as an honest, responsible man, who often stood up against the political pressures of the executive and legislative branches. And, he retained the confidence of five Presidents, even when he had opposed them.
Mr. Martin set the course of the nation's monetary policy during an economic golden age. His tenure included the nation's longest economic expansion, from 1961 to 1969. That growth period ended when the central bank was forced to begin raising interest rates to fight the rising inflation that was caused by Government spending during the Vietnam War.
In one of his most controversial moves, in December 1965, Mr. Martin voted to raise the discount rate -- the rate at which commercial banks borrow from the Fed -- despite President Lyndon B. Johnson's objections. After the vote, he was summoned to Johnson's ranch. He soon emerged to say that the meeting had changed neither man's mind.
Mr. Martin often verbally battled with his most bitter critic, Representative Wright Patman, a Democrat from Texas, for whom interest rates could never be low enough. But he largely avoided public squabbles and had a knack for stating his gloomy predictions in a pleasant way.
At the bottom of one economic slump, Mr. Martin warned Congress that you can't "spend yourself prosperous."
Another time, he remarked, "A perpetual deficit is the road to undermining any currency."
In a written statement yesterday, Alan Greenspan, the current chairman of the Federal Reserve, said that Mr. Martin had set a "masterful example."
"Crucially, Chairman Martin moved the Federal Reserve from being an adjunct of the Treasury Department," Mr. Greenspan's statement said, "to the independent status we know today."
In private, Mr. Martin was known as a relaxed, friendly man who had an engaging, boyish grin. He was an avid tennis player, who often played on a court across the street from the headquarters of the Federal Reserve.
His father, a prominent banker in St. Louis, had helped President Woodrow Wilson draft the Federal Reserve Act that created the central bank in 1913. Later, his father served as president of the Federal Reserve Bank of St. Louis, and Mr. Martin followed, working as an examiner at that same bank after his graduation from Yale University with a B.A. in Latin and English.
After a move to New York, Mr. Martin, at 31, became the first paid president of the New York Stock Exchange -- a position that earned him the nickname of "boy wonder of Wall Street."
In 1941, he left his $48,000-a-year job at the exchange to serve in World War II, where he began earning a salary of $21 a month. He rose to the rank of colonel, serving in several key wartime agencies.
After the war, Mr. Martin became chairman of the Export-Import Bank and then served as Assistant Secretary of the Treasury under President Truman, a fellow Missourian.
His 19-year chairmanship of the Fed under both Democratic and Republican Presidents was longer than the term of any other chairman so far.
When President Truman interviewed him about taking the chairmanship, Mr. Truman recalled that he had bought some Liberty Bonds in the First World War that had later declined in value. "You wouldn't ever let that happen again, would you?" the President asked.
Mr. Martin replied that he wouldn't want it to happen, but unless the Administration followed responsible fiscal and monetary policies, it probably would happen again.
President John F. Kennedy had told Mr. Martin in 1961 that most of the Kennedy advisers wanted a new Fed chairman and questioned him closely about whether he intended to "flaunt" the independence of the Fed. In the end, Mr. Kennedy asked him to stay on and explained that he did so because "responsible men" were not easy to find.
A similar conversation occurred early in President Johnson's Administration, with the President telling Mr. Martin that he wanted low interest rates all the time. The chairman replied that he would have to get another man to achieve that.
Mr. Martin stepped down from the central bank in January 1970. He then served on the board for many corporations and nonprofit organizations, including I.B.M., American Express and the National Geographic Society. For many years, he worked as a lawyer for Riggs National Bank in Washington. He also served as president of the National Tennis Hall of Fame.
He is survived by his wife, Cynthia Davis Martin; two daughters, Diana Martin of Washington and Cynthia Martin Ghenea of Portland, Me.; a son, William McChesney Martin 3d of Cambridge, Mass., and two grandchildren.
In an interview with The New York Times in 1985, Mr. Martin said there was much double talk in most discussions about monetary policy, which ranks as one of Washington's most arcane subjects.
When he took office, Mr. Martin said he had told himself: "My gracious, here I am the new chairman of the Fed, and I'm doing my best -- I'm not the brightest fellow in the world but I'm working hard on this -- and I haven't the faintest idea of how you figure the money supply. Yet everybody thinks I have it at my fingertips.
"They don't really know what the money supply is now, even today," Mr. Martin continued. "They print some figures -- I'm not trying to make fun of it -- but a lot of it is just almost superstition."

HEADLINE: Los Angeles Goes Small Town in Courtship of Democrats

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES, July 28

 BODY:
Over two solid days, the cream of this city's civic leadership has courted the Democratic Party's national convention for 2000 with every carrot at its command, and somewhere between the margaritas and the mariachis, the exploding wall of balloons and the flying angel, the intensity of the effort took on a sweetly comic small-town air.
By the time a chorus of irresistible inner-city children serenaded the 50-odd members of the party's site-selection committee this morning with the soothing strains of "California Dreamin,' " the message was clear: the Democrats would be safe and warm . . . if they were in L.A. 
 For this much-mocked metropolis, winning a national political convention for the first time since John F. Kennedy was nominated here in 1960 would mean more than playing host to thousands of delegates and journalists, with their attendant tourism and perhaps $150 million in spending. It would also amount to a kind of massive international coming-out party for a city that has largely rebounded from the economic, natural and social disasters of the early 90's and is bursting to tell the world.
"The city frankly gets a bad rap in the eastern press and the international press," said Eli Broad, the billionaire chairman of Sun America Inc. and one of a bevy of high-powered local leaders, including the entertainment mogul David Geffen, recruited to lead the city's lobbying effort and $35 million public-private pledge of financial support for the convention. "They think of the Simpson trial, the recessed economy, the loss of aerospace and defense jobs, and perceptions lag reality. We want the opportunity to show what L.A. is today."
Los Angeles is not alone in wanting the psychic and economic lift that a convention would bring, and though party officials say it has a very good chance, senior Democrats both here and in Washington say that Vice President Al Gore -- who will get the final say -- has a definite fondness for Philadelphia, where his old friend Mayor Edward G. Rendell is making his own strong pitch to revive a city beleaguered by economic and social woes.
The site-selection committee, which is also considering Boston, Denver, Miami, Minneapolis and New Orleans and is to make a recommendation late this year, was supposed to visit Philadelphia last month. But it arrived only to find picket lines in place for a bitter transit strike and decided to postpone its visit just hours after it arrived. The strike has since been tentatively settled and the visit is to be rescheduled for this fall. Meanwhile, the Angelenos are pressing every perceived advantage, chief among them California's 54 electoral votes, which all experts agree any Democratic candidate must have to win the White House.
"I think we get it," said Susan Estrich, who managed Michael S. Dukakis's 1988 campaign and is now a professor at the University of Southern California law school, as she munched crispy rolled taquitos at a welcoming reception for the committee in downtown Los Angeles on Monday. Her reasoning: the nominee "either comes here in the summer, or he spends the whole fall here campaigning. He's got to spend the spring here anyway raising money, so he might as well come in the summer when he can do both effectively."
This morning, the committee members toured the sprawling Convention Center downtown, which would serve as media work space and staging area for the convention, and saw a spiffy video on the new Staples Arena, the state-of-the art stadium that is being built just across the street and that would house the convention itself. The visitors all got Los Angeles Kings hockey jerseys emblazoned with their names and the number 54.
The committee members entered the cavernous Convention Center through a wall of hundreds of balloons, which spelled out "LA 2000" and exploded on cue to let them pass, then sat down to a breakfast of golden raspberries and other fresh fruits, smoked scallops and salmon and wild mushroom hash with poached egg. Mr. Broad stood up to welcome a performer, who was slowly lowered, feathery angel wings and all, from the rafters of the glass-domed center, bearing the city's official bid to the Democratic Party.
On Monday night, the committee had dinner on a converted sound stage at Universal Studios. This afternoon, it toured the new Getty Center and prepared for a dinner at Mayor Richard J. Riordan's home before heading off to dance at the Conga Room, a new Latin music nightclub partly owned by the actor Jimmy Smits.
But in a city that has been the past-master of self-promotion since colorful orange crate labels lured frozen Midwesterners here at the turn of the century, the overall effect was oddly low key, low tech and homey. The organizers missed no chance to emphasize the city's racial and ethnic diversity, as a string of union workers from various trades stepped up to make brief speeches beginning, "I want the 2000 Democratic National Convention because. . . ."
"We want to know what the outreach is in a city to minority business, we want to know what the labor situation is," said Rosalind Wyman, a Los Angeles native and the doyenne of California Democratic politics, who is a member of the site-selection committee and confesses a preference for her hometown. "We care about having an environmentally sensitive city, and whether there's access for the disabled and whether the host committee is broad based. Obviously, I think we would put on an incredible convention."
The Republicans are said to be leaning to cities in old-line swing states like Chicago and Philadelphia, and at least some Democratic officials have worried that the risk of coming to Los Angeles is that liberal celebrities might overshadow the proceedings. Then again, since no one expects conventions to decide anything of importance in an age of primary elections, why not come to the home of show-business in hopes of at least producing interesting television?
"The good reason to come here would be if you wanted to try and re-invent the convention," Ms. Estrich said. "That would be the exciting challenge. Because the truth is, if they do it the old way, no one will watch anyway."

HEADLINE: Liberties;
An American Tragedy

BYLINE: By MAUREEN DOWD 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
When I was a teen-ager, in the 60's, I never told people that my father was a cop. It wasn't cool.
In the Vietnam era, with the National Guard killing kids at Kent State, with the Chicago police billyclubbing yippies, with the stench of tear gas and class hatred everywhere, the last thing you wanted to do was admit you were related to someone in a uniform. 
 Cops were pigs. The military was fascist. Shaggy-haired Ivy Leaguers like Bill Clinton were protesting Vietnam and writing letters about "loathing the military."
It seemed easier to say my father was in politics. After he retired from the police force, he was an aide to a Senator and a Congressman.
For his last 13 years on the force, he was an inspector in charge of Senate security. In those days, D.C. detectives were assigned to guard the Capitol, and the Capitol police worked on a patronage system.
It wasn't a high-risk job day to day, compared with his earlier assignments walking beats or in homicide. The most treacherous part was the temper of the Democratic Senate leader, Lyndon Johnson, who would fire underlings at any provocation.
But sometimes crazy people, or people made crazy by a cause, would show up. And then the job of guarding lawmakers and tourists could suddenly turn very dangerous.
On March 1, 1954, three Puerto Rican nationalists pulled out guns and randomly fired down from the spectators' gallery above the House floor. Five Congressmen were wounded. My father ran over from the Senate and wrestled one of the shooters to the ground.
He marked the Puerto Rican's gun by carving his initials with a penknife on the handle. Later, at the trial, when the defense lawyer superciliously grilled him about how he could be sure that the gun in evidence was the same one used in the crime, my father told him to look on the bottom of the handle. There was the "MJD."
That bloody day 44 years ago was the most famous outbreak of violence in the Capitol until last Friday, when two Capitol police officers died heroically trying to stop a crazy man from shooting up the place.
There has been an intense emotional response to the deaths of John Gibson and Jacob Chestnut. So much that happens in Washington now seems trivial and craven and tawdry. But these two men died doing something large and brave and important, defending freedom.
As I watched their memorial service, it struck me that my generation had come a long way from the days when anyone in uniform was a fit target for an epithet. Bill Clinton may have once shunned the military, but as President he has given respect and support to the police and armed forces. His eulogy for Officers Gibson and Chestnut was spare and elegant. "They consecrated this house of freedom," he said.
Some who came of age in the painful Vietnam era are now making movies glorifying the military and TV shows celebrating cops.
Law and order, once a phrase that elicited elitist contempt, is now the coolest entertainment pitch going. Old Mayor Daley is dead. Long live Sipowitz and clans of Irish cops.
Steven Spielberg has been giving interviews, talking about having made "Saving Private Ryan" to honor the men who fought in World War II and risked so much for freedom.
Suddenly, baby boomers realize that, despite a buzzing economy and a passel of luxury goods, we are going to die without experiencing the nobility that illuminated the lives of our parents and grandparents. They lived through wars and depressions, life and death, good stomping evil. Our unifying event was "Seinfeld."
We live in a culture more concerned with celebrity than morality, more centered on self than others.
Seeing the flag-draped coffins in the Rotunda, the thousands of mourners, it seems a long time from the days of cops as pigs. Seeing the long queues for the patriotic "Saving Private Ryan," it seems a long time from the days of the military as fascist.
We understand now that it was the politicians who sent out the Chicago cops and the Ohio National Guard. It was the politicians, Johnson, McNamara and Kissinger, who sent all those young men to die after they knew Vietnam was unwinnable. We may be more cynical. But we're no longer demonizing the wrong people.
Nowadays, I never say my father worked in politics. I simply say, with the greatest possible pride, that he was a cop.

HEADLINE: The Cruel Leaders of Myanmar

 BODY:
The fate of democracy in Myanmar, formerly Burma, has been closely associated with the fate of one courageous woman, Daw Aung San Suu Kyi. The military junta ruling that country has now blocked her effort to travel to a meeting with members of her political party. She has refused to turn back and yesterday spent her fifth day in a stifling hot car, surrounded by armed troops, on a road outside the capital city of Yangon.
This treatment of the 1991 Nobel Peace laureate, which drew strong protests yesterday from America, the European Union and other countries, is despicable. Even if the junta backs off, the episode shows again that it is unfit to govern Myanmar. 
 The junta has made a considerable public relations effort in recent years to create a more favorable image. Yet it has now destroyed whatever little international standing it had gained. It is also failing in the most basic responsibilities of government. Earlier this week Secretary of State Madeleine Albright noted that conditions in Myanmar were worsening, with political restrictions, economic and educational stagnation, a spreading AIDS epidemic and rampant drug trafficking making what she called a "social breakdown or explosion" increasingly likely.
Myanmar's junta should respond to international demands and let Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi travel in peace. But that is not enough. She, and all the people of Myanmar, must be allowed to express themselves freely, and be governed by the representatives they freely choose.

HEADLINE: Wanted: Retired Workers To Fix Year 2000 Problems

BYLINE: By MATTHEW L. WALD 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 28

 BODY:
Two senior citizens groups have agreed to recruit retired computer programmers to help find year 2000 bugs in old computer systems, the head of the President's Council on Year 2000 Conversion said today.
The American Association of Retired Persons, assisted by the National Council of Senior Citizens, will conduct an "aggressive outreach campaign," said John A. Koskinen. He was appointed by President Clinton in February to lead the Federal effort to minimize disruptions to the Government and the economy from the anticipated computer problems. 
 Many computers record the date as a two-digit number and will recognize the day after Dec. 31, 1999, as Jan. 1, 1900. Fixing this requires searching through millions of lines of computer programs and finding where date information is stored. Some programs were written decades ago, and in languages that are no longer widely used.
"Many people think you have to be 20-something to understand this stuff," said Ted Bobrow, an association spokesman, "but there are people who've been working on it for 30 or 40 years."
Some industries are having trouble finding the programmers they need. Mr. Koskinen began a public campaign today to improve awareness of the problem and unveiled a Web page (www.y2k.gov) with a job bank maintained by the Labor Department where potential employers and employees can register.
A search of the job bank showed that since July 14 more than 440 people looking for work as programmers have filed resumes listing the term "Y2K," which is computer shorthand for year 2000.
Deborah R. Russell, an association staff member, said, "The money is good, and it's an opportunity to go back into the work force for a finite amount of time, an opportunity to be useful, to make a difference." She said people who programmed early computer languages like Cobol and Fortran were in high demand.
Mr. Koskinen also said that a bill would be introduced in Congress on Wednesday to make it easier for companies to share year 2000 fixes without fear of liability lawsuits, should those fixes cause new problems. The bill would make companies liable only if they knew that the information was false.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Union and G.M. Reach Pact To End Strikes at 2 Plants
General Motors and the United Automobile Workers union agreed to end nearly eight weeks of strikes at two auto parts plants in Flint, Mich., that had shut down car assembly plants across North America, pushed hundreds of thousands of workers into temporary unemployment and slowed the nation's economic growth.
The settlement, which must be approved by workers at both factories, settles a wide range of local issues at the two factories and at several other G.M. factories where workers had threatened strikes. But it did not address any national issues, such as G.M.'s push to open new factories overseas or its effort to reduce its work force in the United States through attrition. Page A1. 

Nasdaq Composite Index Falls 1.9%
The Nasdaq composite index fell 36.73 points, or 1.9 percent, to 1,896.53. The Dow Jones industrial average, which was down 212.15 points before bargain hunters trimmed the loss, closed at 8,934.78. The market declines were caused by continuing concerns about corporate profits, Asia's financial crisis and the specter of political upheaval in Washington. D7.
 
The yield on the 30-year Treasury bond rose to 5.74 percent, from 5.71 percent. D7.
 
E-Mail Programs Vulnerable
A serious security flaw has been discovered in E-mail programs from Microsoft and Netscape Communications that could permit a malicious person to send a message containing a virus that could crash a computer, destroy or even steal data. A1.
 
BMW Acquires Rolls-Royce Name
Volkswagen was forced to agree to hand over the Rolls-Royce name to BMW in 2003. Volkswagen and BMW had competed for the British luxury car company with Volkswagen agreeing to pay $780 million for Rolls-Royce Motor Cars. But analysts now say BMW appears to be the real winner because the name is considered by many to be Rolls-Royce's most valuable asset. D3.
 
Senate Approves a Credit Union Bill
The Senate passed a bill that would help credit unions compete with banks for customers. The House had approved a similar measure in April. If approved, the legislation would override a Supreme Court ruling by letting federally chartered credit unions continue to include more than one group in their memberships. D2.
 
Workers Stop Reopening of Plants
South Korean workers blocked an attempt by Hyundai Motor to reopen factories that had been closed for more than a week. D3.
 
Microsoft Calls Case 'Groundless'
Microsoft called the Government's antitrust case against it "completely groundless" and offered a glimpse of the legal strategy it will pursue in its first court filing in its defense. Both sides are scrambling to take depositions from potential witnesses. William H. Gates, the chairman and co-founder of Microsoft, may testify by videotape in the case, which is scheduled to begin on Sept. 8. D2.
 
China Seen as Asia's Growth Engine
The chairman of the Export-Import Bank, a United States Government agency, said he wants China to become its largest recipient of loans to buy American products and equipment. His statement reflects the new emphasis the United States is placing on China as an economic engine for Asia. D3.
 
Consumers Still Confident
Consumer confidence in the United States economy stuck close to a 30-year high in July, supported by a combination of benign inflation and an unemployment rate near a three-decade low. D8.
