HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS;
Dow Plunges 93.46, and It Could Have Been Far Worse

BYLINE: By SHARON R. KING 

 BODY:
Although investors might not think so, yesterday's stock market performance might not even race the pulse of some die-hard roller-coaster aficionados.
But it probably came close.
There were twists and turns and wild gyrations, as continuing concerns about corporate profits, Asia's financial crisis and the specter of political upheaval in Washington pushed stocks lower. 
 At one point, the Dow Jones industrial average was down 212.15 points, before bargain hunters pushed it upward to trim its loss to 93.46 points.
The blue chip index closed at 8,934.78, erasing Monday's 90.88 gain. J. P. Morgan, United Technologies and Sears, Roebuck led the decline, a 1.04 percent drop.
Adding to the persistent concerns about Asia and corporate profits, was news that the former White House intern Monica Lewinsky had received immunity from prosecution, paving the way for her to testify before a grand jury seeking to determine whether President Clinton tried to obstruct justice to hide any relationship with her.
"We've had a very high run-up, and there's some uncertainty about the sustainability of earnings," said Shannon Reid, a portfolio manager for the Evergreen Select Strategic Growth fund.
"Investors are more willing to pay up for predictability of earnings versus earnings growth, which is what you see at the end of a cycle," he added.
Asian markets were mixed yesterday. Japan's benchmark Nikkei 225-stock index rose 170.18 points, recouping less than half of Monday's 417.53 point loss. Analysts predict Japan's fundamental economic situation will take at least 12 months to improve.
"I don't read this as indicative of an uptrend," said Dan Seto, a senior economist at Nikko Securities International.
"There's still a lot of skepticism that this new leadership will shift to anything aggressive" to spur the Japanese economy, he said. "Therefore, the other Asian markets will have limited upside."
In domestic markets, the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, fell 17.03 points, to 1,130.24, a 1.5 percent decline. The Nasdaq composite index had an even steeper decline, falling 36.73 points, or 1.9 percent, to 1,896.53. Microsoft, Worldcom and MCI led the declines.
All of these factors still leave investors searching for clues to determine whether corporate profit growth will improve in the second half of the year.
Based on the earnings of the 385 member companies in the S.& P. 500 that have reported so far, profits are expected to grow about 4 percent in the second quarter compared with the first quarter's 3.8 percent increase.
Analysts now forecast growth to improve in the third quarter, up 7.6 percent. They are looking for even stronger growth in the fourth quarter, up 14.1 percent.
But Chuck Hill, head of research at First Call, cautions investors that analysts have already begun making substantial downward revisions in their third-quarter forecasts. Those changes, at a sharper than usual pace, are a signal that analysts believe the impact of Asia's woes have not yet entirely filtered through the domestic market, he said.
In addition, smaller company shares continue to reel from a lack of investor interest, another signal some analysts point to when questioning the sustainability of market gains.
The Russell index of 2,000 smaller company shares fell 5.62 points yesterday, to 427.54, its eight consecutive decline. It is the lowest close for the index since Jan. 27.
Small company share prices may not improve until there is more stability in the overall market, which would help investors feel more confident about putting money in these less liquid shares, said Keith Mullin, emerging growth stock analyst at Salomon Smith Barney.
"I still don't think people care about small caps yet," said Charles W. Johnson, head equity trader at Blaylock & Partners in Baltimore. "If you put your money in small caps, it's just going to sit there and sit there and sit there" -- a circumstance, return-conscious portfolio managers cannot afford.

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
Treasuries Falter Again
By Bloomberg News
Treasury prices fell for a third session yesterday as investors were reluctant to buy without more signs of slowing economic growth and tame inflation, which may show up in reports later this week.
"I'm not willing to commit any money to this market" with yields where they are, said Charles Ullerich, who helps manage $2 billion at Pilgrim America in Phoenix. He said he would consider buying if long-bond yields climbed to 5.80 percent.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond fell 15/32, to 105 15/32. The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction, rose to 5.74 percent, from 5.71 percent on Monday.

HEADLINE: World News Briefs;
Deal to Finance 2 North Korea Reactors

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Negotiators reached a tentative agreement yesterday on cost-sharing arrangements for supplying two nuclear reactors to North Korea.
But the officials with the Korean Peninsula Energy Development Organization, the consortium building the reactors, refused to disclose exactly how the $4.6 billion in costs would be divided. The European Union, Japan, South Korea and the United States are the major contributors to the project. 
 The program was set up in 1994 after North Korea agreed to freeze and eventually dismantle its nuclear weapons program in exchange for two light-water nuclear power plants.
Before yesterday's agreement, it had been reported that South Korea would pay $3.2 billion, with Japan adding another $1 billion. The other nations would apparently pay the remainder. But South Korea was severely hit by the financial crisis in Asia and wanted to reduce its share of the cost.
The agreement must still be approved by the respective governments.

HEADLINE: 7 Nations Protest as Burmese Besiege Democracy Leader

BYLINE: By PHILIP SHENON 

DATELINE: MANILA, July 28

 BODY:
The United States joined with six other nations today in demanding that the army generals who run Myanmar end their five-day standoff with the leader of that country's democratic opposition, whose car has been surrounded by armed security forces on a rural highway since last Friday.
The opposition leader, Daw Aung San Suu Kyi, was stopped about 30 miles south of Yangon, the capital of Myanmar, formerly Burma, as she drove to a meeting with supporters. Her party won an overwhelming majority in a national election in 1990, but the military junta refused to turn over power to a civilian government. 
 Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi, who won the 1991 Nobel Peace Prize for her campaign to bring democracy to Myanmar, has refused to budge from her white sedan, which was reported today to have been surrounded by soldiers and police officers.
Secretary of State Madeleine K. Albright and the foreign ministers of six countries who are here for a meeting of the Association of Southeast Asian Nations -- Australia, Canada, Japan, New Zealand, South Korea and Austria, representing the European Union -- met today with the Burmese Foreign Minister to insist that the junta end the impasse.
Western diplomats said the seven nations had urged the Burmese Government to allow the American and Japanese Ambassadors in Yangon, formerly Rangoon, to see Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi on the roadside and to serve as mediators in the dispute.
 On Wednesday the junta, which calls itself the State Peace and Development Council, rejected the offer, Agence France-Presse reported. Foreign Minister U Ohn Gyaw said at a news conference that such a meeting "is not urgently needed, and authorities will comply with their request when necessary." Western foreign ministers also at the news conference expressed disappointment over the statement.
The opposition leader was freed from house arrest in 1995 after nearly six years of confinement.
"Aung San Suu Kyi should be able to travel freely in her own country," Ms. Albright said at a news conference here after meeting with the Burmese Foreign Minister. "We hope very much that there will be a resolution to the issue."
When she was United States delegate to the United Nations, Ms. Albright met with Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi in Yangon, and she continues to correspond with the dissident leader. Ms. Albright said the United States had received encouraging reports on Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi's condition.
"She is still with her car," the Secretary said today. "She has enough food and water and medical attention, and her health is O.K.
"Frankly it is usually kind of a normal practice for countries to allow their citizens to travel freely within them and to be able to meet with whomever they want. I think this is an international norm that many countries obviously support."
During this week's meetings in Manila, Ms. Albright has repeatedly singled out the Burmese junta for criticism.
On Monday she warned in a speech that Myanmar, which joined the Association of Southeast Asian Nations last year, had reached "a moment of truth" and that the nation faced "a social breakdown or explosion" after decades of repression. She said the United States held the junta directly responsible for Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi's welfare.
In a statement issued in Yangon, Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi's political party, the National League for Democracy, said she had been visited on the road today by her doctors. The statement gave no details on the doctors' findings.
"The provisions in the car -- water and edibles -- are nearly exhausted," the party said. "Before her health conditions fail," it said, she and her two drivers "should be allowed to proceed on their journey."
It was the third time in the last month that Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi, the Oxford-educated daughter of Myanmar's national independence hero, U Aung San, has been stopped as she tried to travel outside Yangon to meet with members of her party.
The protest by the United States may mean less to the Burmese junta than the criticism offered by the Governments of Japan and South Korea, which have much closer economic and diplomatic ties with Myanmar. The United States has relatively little economic leverage left, since it has already imposed severe economic sanctions on Myanmar as a result of its human rights record.
The junta responded to the international criticism today, issuing a statement in Manila in which it said Mrs. Aung San Suu Kyi had provoked the standoff by failing to give notice of her travel plans. Her car was stopped to prevent an "untoward incident from occurring which could affect the peace, tranquillity and security," the statement said.
It accused her of trying to create "a political issue" by staging the incident during this week's meeting of foreign ministers from the Association of Southeast Asian Nations and other countries with an interest in the region.
Her car was stopped, the junta suggested, for her own protection. "Myanmar authorities," it said, "have always provided her with meticulous security measures at her request to ward off any untoward foul play which could impinge on her security and safety."

HEADLINE: THE G.M. SETTLEMENT: THE OVERVIEW;
GENERAL MOTORS AND U.A.W. AGREE ON END TO STRIKE

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: FLINT, Mich., July 28

 BODY:
General Motors and the United Automobile Workers agreed today to end the nearly eight-week strike at two auto parts factories here, a dispute that has shut down G.M. assembly plants across North America, pushed hundreds of thousands of workers into temporary unemployment and slowed the nation's economic growth.
The settlement, which is subject to ratification votes on Wednesday morning by workers at both factories, settles a wide range of local issues at the two factories and at several other G.M. factories where workers had threatened to strike. 
 But the agreement does not directly address national issues that the union had publicly declared to be driving the strike, such as G.M.'s push to open new factories overseas or its effort to shrink its United States work force through attrition. These issues can only be changed through G.M.'s national agreement with the U.A.W., which expires in September 1999 and was not changed today.
The agreement includes a somewhat surprising element, a promise by both sides' leaders to have regular meetings as a way to rebuild their relationship and head off further confrontations. But company and union officials already meet occasionally and have been unable to bridge disagreements that produce several factory-level strikes almost every year. Page D1.
The accord deals with a series of narrow issues. G.M. promises not to sell or close before January 2000 either a speedometer and spark plug factory on strike here or two brake factories in Dayton, Ohio, where workers had threatened a strike this summer. G.M. also agrees to invest $180 million in new equipment at the other factory on strike here, which makes auto body parts.
In exchange, the auto maker gets changes in work rules, notably a 15 percent increase in the number of parts that some welders are required to make before stopping work. In February, G.M. canceled its plans to invest the $180 million because workers were drawing eight hours pay for four to six hours of work and were resisting increases in production quotas.
Each side suffered enormous losses. Analysts estimate that G.M. lost $3 billion after taxes and saw its sales drop by $12 billion, the heaviest losses ever incurred by an American company during a strike, at least before adjusting for inflation. The U.A.W.'s 200,000 members lost paychecks totaling $1 billion.
G.M. can recover as much as half of the lost profits by running its factories around the clock for months to make up for lost production. U.A.W. workers will earn ample overtime in the coming weeks, although not enough to balance the paychecks lost during the strike.
Shares of G.M. had been rising for the last few days on optimism about an end to the strike. They rose $1.125 today, to $74.25, the highest since their $75.25 price when the dispute began on June 5.
The broad outlines of today's deal began to emerge in the first week of talks. The strike lasted so long partly because the two sides mistrusted each other and partly because G.M. raised the stakes by suing the U.A.W. for the first time since 1937, contending that the strikes violated the national agreement.
G.M. withdrew its lawsuit today, along with a grievance in which an arbitrator had been expected to rule as soon as Wednesday. While U.A.W. leaders insisted at a news conference this afternoon that they had not been intimidated by G.M.'s legal offensive, a U.A.W. official who insisted on anonymity said that the union had become worried that it might lose and stepped up talks on Saturday.
If the threat of litigation deters the U.A.W. from further strikes until the national agreement expires, this summer's strikes can be counted as a small but costly victory for G.M. But G.M. was fortunate in that its case was assigned to a conservative Federal judge who repeatedly prevented the union from stalling during arbitration, and the company might not be so fortunate in the future.
Some Wall Street analysts said that the company's losses far exceeded its gains. "This seems like it's just exactly the way it was before," said Michael P. Ward, an auto analyst at Paine Webber. "I think the market expected G.M. to deliver a knockout blow, and they didn't."
There will be four separate votes on ratification; each of the factories on strike will hold separate votes for assembly line workers and for skilled craftsmen like electricians. Agreements to end other recent strikes at G.M. factories have been easily approved, and U.A.W. officials expected that today's agreement would also pass, although they predicted some grumbling among workers facing higher production quotas.
In a provision that is likely to raise eyebrows on Wall Street but improve the odds of ratification, G.M. will give a week's pay, about $800, to all striking and laid-off workers who missed a week of holiday pay early this month because of the strike. G.M. said that the payments to more than 100,000 workers would not affect its earnings because it would use money earmarked for joint training programs with the union.
If ratified, today's accord would allow more than a hundred G.M. assembly plants and parts factories nationwide to resume production over the next week, with the factories on strike going back to work by Wednesday afternoon. G.M. would recall 189,000 workers that it temporarily laid off across North America, while its suppliers would bring back tens of thousands of workers who had been pushed into unemployment.
G.M. desperately needs to restock its dealerships, many of which have been picked bare of popular models during the strike. Ronald Zarrella, G.M.'s group vice president for sales and marketing, warned earlier this month that G.M. could lose thousands of customers, never to return, if it did not settle the strike by mid-July. G.M.'s greatest asset has been the millions of Americans who keep coming back to its dealerships every several years for a new car. But once these customers try rival brands, they seldom come back, which is why G.M.'s market share has shrunk from half in the late 1970's to 31 percent this spring.
Restarting the world's largest manufacturing system will be a formidable task. With the strike costing $75 million a day, G.M. is likely to undertake a huge airlift of parts to assembly plants once the factories here reopen, said Michael Robinet, the managing director of CSM Forecasting, an auto production consulting firm in Farmington Hills, Mich.
The strikes here were particularly acrimonious because both sides came to see them as struggles over principles. Indeed, Donald Hackworth, G.M.'s group vice president for North American car operations, even said early this month that the cost of the strikes could not be justified financially. Both sides kept raising the stakes, bringing up new issues or making new threats in the mistaken belief that the other side would quickly back down.
At the beginning, G.M. executives saw the strikes as a challenge to their right as managers to decide which factories deserved new investments and which did not. Union leaders viewed the strikes as necessary to hold G.M. to past announcements of investment plans, notably the $180 million.
G.M.'s decision to remove parts-making equipment from one of the factories over Memorial Day weekend, fearing a strike, not only prompted the walkouts but also caused the union's rank and file to worry that their livelihoods were being threatened, making a compromise even more difficult. G.M. returned the equipment on Sunday.
As the strikes wore on, the U.A.W. began publicly depicting them as a struggle over whether the world's largest company would remain committed to investing in its home market or would pursue fatter profits in foreign countries. But the final settlement here did not address G.M.'s plans to build assembly plants in Argentina, Brazil, China, Poland and Thailand, as well as auto parts factories in dozens of countries. G.M. has been refurbishing factories in the United States but seldom building new ones, mainly because its steadily falling market share has already left it with too many inefficient factories.
For its part, G.M. aggravated workers' fears about job losses by talking publicly during the strike about possible factory closings. But Richard Shoemaker, the U.A.W.'s vice president for G.M. issues, said this afternoon that G.M. officials had not even mentioned the possibility of factory closings during negotiations.
Mr. Shoemaker and Gerald A. Knechtel, G.M.'s vice president for North American personnel, said in separate news conferences today that there had been no changes in the national agreement, particularly in the sections dealing with how quickly G.M. can reduce its work force. So even if G.M. chooses to eliminate unprofitable car models and close factories -- a decision that G.M.'s board will consider on Monday -- it will have to continue paying any workers for whom there are no longer jobs. This makes it unlikely that G.M. will embark on an ambitious reorganization, although it might make a few small moves.
G.M. began calling for arbitration on June 23, contending that the strikes here violated the company's national agreement with the union. The national agreement only allows factory-level strikes over increases in assembly line speed, subcontracting and health and safety issues. The U.A.W. has cited all these issues here but also demanded that G.M. invest more money here and guarantee that jobs be preserved -- the same demands that the union has made in more than a dozen other local strikes over the last five years.
When the U.A.W. balked early this month at prompt arbitration, G.M. filed suit and won a Federal court order requiring that both sides proceed without delay. U.A.W. leaders concluded on Saturday, the last of four days of testimony in the arbitration case, that there was at least a chance that the arbitrator might issue a precedent-setting decision barring investment issues from discussion in future factory-level strikes.
Today's agreement covers the two factories on strike here as well as a car assembly plant here and parts factories in Indianapolis and Dayton, Ohio, where workers had also threatened strikes. The settlement did not include the Saturn factory in Spring Hill, Tenn., the Corvette factory in Bowling Green, Ky., or a sport utility vehicle factory in Janesville, Wis., where workers have also asked permission from the U.A.W.'s national leadership to strike. These factories are unlikely to receive that permission any time soon.
During the strikes, G.M. has lost the production of nearly 500,000 vehicles, the equivalent of four weeks of production, and could lose an additional 100,000 vehicles as factories gradually reopen when they receive fresh shipments of parts.
The strikes would have been even costlier had they not included G.M.'s regularly scheduled summer shutdown over the first two weeks of July, when all G.M. factories are closed anyway. By comparison, a 1996 strike at the two brake factories in Dayton, Ohio, lasted only 17 days but prevented G.M. from producing 180,000 cars and light trucks.
Neither the current strikes nor the 1996 strike come close to the scale of the U.A.W.'s 67-day national strike against G.M. in 1970, which wiped out 10 weeks of production and produced a brief contraction in the nation's economic output.
Paul Ballew, who was the Federal Reserve's expert on the auto industry for many years and who is now the chief economist at J. D. Power & Associates, said that the current strike slowed economic growth by half a percentage point in the second quarter and by an additional eight-tenths of one percent in the third quarter. But once G.M. has finished converting factories to the 1999 model year over the next couple of months, it is likely to run many assembly plants around the clock, which could increase the nation's economic output this winter.

HEADLINE: Security Fears Still Plague Cybershopping

BYLINE: By TINA KELLEY 

 BODY:
WHILE not many of the 19.7 million Americans who visited commercial Web sites from their homes in 1997 have reported problems with Internet credit-card use, some surfers -- and security experts -- say they are concerned enough about Internet crime that they would rather auction off their firstborn child than use a credit card to buy something on the Web.
Kirk Bailey, a computer security expert with Regence Blue Shield in Seattle, will not shop on line now. But he said he could foresee a time when he might. 
 "Right now, it's a provocative environment and lots of things can fail, even with good intentions and best practices," he said, citing some instances in which criminals have obtained credit-card numbers taken from inadequately protected servers on the Internet. "It's much more dangerous than people think. It's not the $50 you get charged for each unauthorized use, but the crooks use this information to steal your identity."
James McQuivey, an analyst in on-line retail strategy at Forrester Research in Cambridge, Mass., said on-line security was a problem of perception, not technology. Instead of bragging about "SSL level 5 encrypted browsers," he said, sites would do well to post slogans on every page like "Worried about your credit card? So are we," and offer a toll-free number for the electronically timid to call to give their card numbers.
As a precaution, some retailers will not store credit-card numbers, or they assign identification numbers to their customers for on-line use while keeping credit-card numbers behind a fire wall.
"I have yet to see any big data warehouses get hacked," Mr. McQuivey said.
But Molly Bell, an information specialist with a communications firm in Washington, said she suspected she had been hit by a hacker when, soon after using her credit card in January to sign up for Compuserve, the Internet service provider, she had to contest several unauthorized credit-card charges to something called "XPICS Publishing." When she called the company, which distributes pornography on line, it told her there had been charges to her card from two other E-mail accounts, neither of which were on Compuserve.
After two more unsolicited charges appeared in April, she canceled her credit card and said Compuserve required her to give her new card number unless she opened a corporate account for $75 more.
"All this confusion demonstrates just how easy it is for someone to commit Internet retail fraud," she said after quitting Compuserve.
Frank Fiore, an on-line shopping guide for the Mining Company, the Web search engine, said he had never heard of an on-line theft of a credit-card number. But he said he was wary of person-to-person sales through auctions or news groups when nothing was known about the parties except their E-mail addresses.
He advised obtaining and verifying phone numbers, street addresses and as much information as possible about prospective partners in E-commerce, whether they are individuals or businesses, and using an Internet escrow service like www.iescrow.com or www.tradesafe.com, which will, for a fee, act as security middlemen, holding payment for an item until both seller and purchaser are satisfied with the deal.
Bidders in on-line auctions should be careful to research the market value of the desired item so as not to bid higher in the heat of the moment, he said. And beware of auction sites that do not control the transactions and the products sold, as these are no more secure than transactions with individuals.
"Then there are the more direct rip-offs, where they have every intention of taking your money," he said, citing pyramid schemes, work-at-home plans and offers to clean up credit reports. "Now instead of reaching thousands of people, they can reach hundreds of thousands, through E-commerce."

HEADLINE: Internet Shopping: A Mixed Bag

BYLINE: By TINA KELLEY 

 BODY:
IT was the best of shopping, it was the worst of shopping. When Stephen Kornelis of Waconia, Minn., found the mint-condition vinyl album -- still in cellophane and only $8 -- that he had spent the last 15 years searching for, it made the fruitless, two-month Web search for paintball gear almost worth it. And his discovery of an obscure Keef Hartley CD almost erased the annoyance of those unreturned phone calls from the paintball Web site guys who work from 2 P.M. to 4 P.M. and, he said, were probably "just trying to hustle a few bucks."
"I have had as many negative experiences as I have good experiences" with shopping over the Internet, said Mr. Kornelis, a software engineer. 
 In 1997, 19.7 million Americans visited retail Web sites from their homes, according to Mediametrix, a Web rating service. Jupiter Communications, a research company, measured revenue numbers for American on-line consumer transactions and found $707 million in 1996 and $2.6 billion in 1997. It projected $5.8 billion in 1998, in what a LJR Redbook Research report said would be a $2.6 trillion retail economy. E-commerce is expected to generate between $34 billion and $37.5 billion by 2002.
Some people love it, some tolerate it, others fear it. Fans of Internet commerce find it tremendously convenient. Consumers can shop when it is raining without getting wet. No need to dress, let alone find taxis, tokens or quarters for the meter. No more surly sales clerks, no more mall Muzak. In a recent survey by Forrester Research, active Web shoppers rated convenience the No. 1 reason to shop on line, although in off-line shopping, they found price and service as important.
In return for the convenience they offer, digital shops like CD Now, Amazon.com and Etoys don't need to discount items so deeply, said James McQuivey, an analyst in on-line retail strategies at Forrester. "If they build the brand so it's going to be convenient, you're not going to say, 'Oh, I can get it for 5 percent less at a bargain bookstore,' " he said.
Laurie Petersen of Hoboken, N.J., an editorial director for Barnesandnoble .com and an early Web shopper, knows how easy it is to go on a buying binge by the blue glow of a monitor.
"Sometimes they'd run specials and you'd find yourself buying stuff just because it was really easy to buy it," she said of certain sites in the Web's earlier years.
When her daughter, who is 15 months old, was born, Ms. Petersen became a major fan of Ibaby, which sells supplies for infants.
"I found myself checking every day because they'd have specials," she said. "If you're the kind of person who can spend the night with a catalogue and spend $300 on stuff, you're equally susceptible on line, even more so perhaps, because it's that much easier."
The Web's wide selection is also a major draw. "You have available to you a global, worldwide selection, and that is perhaps the real advantage" of Web shopping, said Kurt Barnard, president of Barnard's Retail Trend Report, a forecasting business in Upper Montclair, N.J., who added that so many choices could be paralyzing.
"Fancy going through a department store looking for a blouse, like Macy's on 34th Street, carrying nothing other than blouses from the basement to the top floor," he said. "It would not be very attractive."
But on-line shopping is not for everyone. It can be more time-consuming than a simple trip to a local store, especially if delivery times are considered. Some items, particularly brand-new ones, have limited availability and no discount. The marketplace changes from day to day, it is lonesome and impersonal, and it can be slower than a left-turn exit lane from the mall at 6:30 P.M.
And while 54 percent of American businesses have a Web page, according to a study this year by the Simmons Market Research Bureau, there are some items that lose something in the translation to cyberspace.
"Tupperware is really discouraging people from setting up Web sites because they want people to burp the Tupperware, to feel it," said Michael Solomon, a professor of consumer behavior at Auburn University. And it is harder to generate peer pressure to buy from a Web site than from a kaffeeklatsch.
Without some serious revision, he said, the Web will lose out on the buying potential of a whole breed of shoppers: those who travel in packs.
"When people go shopping in groups, they buy more than when they're on their own," Professor Solomon said. "Until you can simulate that, you're losing some sales potential." He suggested the creation of a Virtual Shopping Partner to say, "Go ahead, treat yourself." After all, shoppers separated from their peers are less vulnerable to making spontaneous, serendipitous purchases.
"The more people going with you, the more likely you are to be taken to places you wouldn't go to on your own," he said. A partial fix would "program some spontaneity in these sites," he said, maybe by having them pipe up with random suggestions.
Mr. McQuivey, at Forrester, said some sites are already catching on to the social needs of surfing shoppers.
"We don't just want to transact," he said. "We like being with people, and having that interchange, the feeling of people being with us. So when we go to Amazon.com we can read what everybody else thinks about a book. The site is putting people in touch with other people."
But Professor Solomon noted that Internet shopping can emphasize the gap between the haves and the have-nots. "Poor people are obviously not going to avail themselves of these things, which is ironic because they have the most to gain because of their limited access to stores in urban neighborhoods," he said. And even though physically disabled people and the elderly have a great deal to gain from the convenience of shopping on line, he said, "these are the people that right now are the most Web-averse."
Another flaw in shopping-by-surfing comes from unrealistic expectations, some analysts say. On-line shoppers looking for instant gratification become disappointed when they discover the World Wide Wait.
Mr. McQuivey not only studies on-line shopping; he also does it. "When I'm on line I have illusions of getting perfect information, that I'm going to go to Junglee on Yahoo and find all the jeans that are going to fit me," he said, referring to a shopping software robot that searches a large number of stores. "When I surf to Slate's Web site, I get so ticked off when I can't find any way to locate the pants I want to buy.
"I really got mad at them on line" -- until he remembered that he could easily have gone all the way to a store and found that the slacks were out of stock without feeling quite so wronged.
Still, it's not unreasonable to think that with such comprehensive information, educated E-consumers would be able to find killer bargains and that anyone charging above wholesale would be gradually pushed out of business.
"Is this a boon for consumers?" Mr. Barnard said. "Theoretically, but only theoretically. Prices on the Internet have not proven to be exactly bargains, but to the extent that they are, our own research has shown that the costs of shipping, mailing and handling seem to obliterate whatever price difference there may be between the Internet and brick-and-mortar stores."
Services like Priceline, a travel site at which consumers name the price they are willing to pay for an air fare, are "theoretically the best thing since sliced bread," Mr. McQuivey said, but not the economic model of the future. Besides, when consumers name a price that is higher than the value of the item for sale, they lose out.
"It's not as easy to turn the whole thing upside down to let the consumer name the price," he said. "I don't see that becoming a major model for trading goods and services."
Kevin Key in Berwyn, Ill., says conventional pricing will have to change. He has bought everything from rare stamps to a 65-gallon rain barrel on line, usually for less than he would have paid elsewhere.
"I think the margin of profit in regular retail is going to have to go down, and stores are going to have to be pulling people in with sales," he said.
While some analysts predict that Internet shopping will simply attract the same kind of customers who use mail-order catalogues, others think it will draw more people into new marketplaces.
"I buy CD's like candy, but never at the mall, because the 18-year-old kid behind the counter is going to make fun of me if I put down the CD I want, say, John Tesh," Mr. McQuivey said. "Plus they are playing horrible music by somebody called the Fumbling Planets. Where's my space in that store?"
But he knows his place in cyberspace.
"I can listen to selections on line, and I can't do that in the store," Mr. McQuivey said. "I can't take the shrink wrap off there. The point is, the potential to expand the total market is here."
Does that mean the modern store's days are numbered? Will the kindly yarn shop owner be available when a customer has dropped seven stitches and can't pick them up? Will Salvation Army minions ring their bells in empty streets at Christmas, and the malls be leveled into soccer fields?
Faith Hope Consolo, senior managing director of Garrick-Aug Worldwide, a large retail leasing firm, doubts that E-retail can conquer traditional retail. "Tourists, they'll never do Web shopping, and your ladies who lunch will never do Web shopping," she said. "I really think it takes the touchy-feely out of the whole experience."
But Mr. Barnard said he thinks obsolescence for some stores is a possibility. "I can easily see that groceries could perhaps become the victims of the Internet," he said, "which would be a pity." And without stores, he wondered, how would people be exposed to new products?
"Very often people are attracted just by the package of the thing, where they can take it off the shelf, hold it in their hand," Mr. Barnard said. "That's not really going to be possible on the Internet, the way we know it at this time." But today's Web technology is comparable to the dial telephone, he said, and advances will very likely change the on-line marketplace.
So why not a scratch-and-sniff pad on the desktop for sampling perfumes? Or cyber-sandboxes to sink your feet into, so a distant cobbler can create the world's most comfortable, custom-fit shoes? An on-line makeover salon with an uploaded snapshot of each customer, who could try on different hair styles and colors in the privacy of home?
"I think the horizons of imagination are the limit of what can be done," Mr. Barnard said. "You imagine it, and it will be done."

Chart/Photos: "Real-Life Browsing in E-Stores (or What the Dog Ate)"
Venturing into Internet shopping can be head-thunkingly tedious. Armed with an America Online browser and a fair amount of surfing experience, this reporter was vexed by inscrutable error messages, frequent crashes, the many screens that had to be clicked through before ordering and prompt calls from car salespeople. She found the dead-ends on the information superhighway to be grim and tedious. For finding a book and perfume, the Web provided bargains relatively quickly, but the cyberselection decreased for more obscure necessities, like French bittersweet chocolate. Comparison shopping is difficult when it is unclear whether one is comparing identical items; availability varies from day to day. Sites employing software robots were more elegant, but not necessarily more fruitful.

HEADLINE: Economic Scene;
A new project will measure the cost and effect of regulation.

BYLINE: By Peter Passell 

 BODY:
WITH anti-tax Republicans in charge of Congress and a New Democrat eager to distance himself from his party's welfare-state excesses in the White House, big government is on the wane.
Well, yes and no. While much of the New Deal heritage -- the safety net, progressive taxation, market regulation -- has been rolled back, Washington has more and more to say about health, safety and environmental issues. And the cost of that regulation, as my grandmother never said, isn't chopped liver. At the last reckoning by the Office of Management and Budget, private outlays to comply with social regulation topped $200 billion a year, and they are rising rapidly. 
 This is not necessarily bad, as people who have breathed the air in Denver lately or taken a swim in Lake Erie can attest. But ask an economist who follows regulatory issues, and you will hear that Federal regulation is a hodgepodge of the smart and the perverse, the elegantly precise and the miserably flat-footed, the extraordinarily cost-effective and the grotesquely wasteful.
That's where the new Joint Center for Regulatory Studies fits in. The center, sponsored by the American Enterprise Institute and the Brookings Institution, will act as a nonpartisan watchdog, assessing big-ticket initiatives and producing an annual report on the effect of regulation. "The real purpose is to keep the regulators -- and the legislators who regulate the regulators -- on their toes," said Robert Hahn, the center's director.
Washington has been struggling with questions of how much social regulation is enough since the early 1970's, when the Government's role in health, safety and the environment began to mushroom. Presidents Richard M. Nixon and Gerald R. Ford made modest attempts to coordinate executive agency rule-making and to review proposed rules. President Jimmy Carter established two permanent oversight groups within the executive branch to formalize the process.
But as the effect of social regulation grew in the years of Presidents Ronald Reagan and George Bush, oversight took on a more partisan cast. And though the adversarial atmospherics have largely cleared since the election of President Clinton, regulatory review remains a game that only insiders play. "The only comments regulators generally get are self-interested comments," said Robert Litan, co-director of the joint center.
A decidedly mixed bag has resulted. At one end of the spectrum, the rules phasing out lead additives from gasoline are widely viewed as a model of efficiency, flexibility and solid science. At the other, inconceivably expensive Superfund rules require the Government to make every toxic waste site as clean as it was before human beings populated the planet. Meanwhile, bipartisan efforts to anchor rule-making in good economics and good science -- for example, a measure sponsored last year by Senators Carl Levin, Democrat of Michigan, and Fred D. Thompson, Republican of Tennessee -- have gotten nowhere.
The tension between those who fear the quiet accretion of social regulation and those who see regulatory change as a plot to roll back a quarter-century of progress won't be easily resolved. But the American Enterprise Institute and the Brookings Institution are trying to narrow these differences by opening the regulatory process to the light of day.
"The Congressional Budget Office routinely analyzes budgets, and think tanks routinely second-guess them," Mr. Litan said. "But we'll be the first to look systematically at Federal regulation, which now costs consumers more than total discretionary Government spending costs the taxpayers."
The joint center, which just opened solely on the strength of foundation grants, says it will release in-depth analyses of a few proposed big-ticket rules each year. It will also publish an annual assessment of the costs and benefits of all Federal regulation, backstopping the once-over-lightly assessments by the Office of Management and Budget, which have been required by law since 1997.
As important, the center will assess the effect of existing regulation. At the moment, neither Congress nor the White House has the stomach to evaluate hugely expensive rules that may have outlived their usefulness but are still defended by entrenched interests. Mr. Hahn cites the example of the corporate average fuel economy standards for cars, which add billions of dollars to the nation's transportation bill with questionable effect on energy use or the environment.
Attitudes toward regulation expose the gulf between the way economists and just about everyone else thinks. To economists, rules are undesirable unless the benefits -- tangible and intangible -- exceed the costs. Otherwise, there must be some other way to organize the world that leaves everyone better off. But to many, the cost-benefit analysis that forms the bedrock of regulatory analysis is just a fancy way to rationalize self-interest.
The joint center project probably won't change many minds. But in Mr. Hahn's view, it is a promising place to start. "We're not going to have a real debate over regulatory reform until people have a source of information they can really trust," he said.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: Market Place -- Conflict Over Conflicts;
Class-Action Lawyers Defend Their Political Contributions

BYLINE: By DIANA B. HENRIQUES 

 BODY:
Leonard Barrack, a prominent fund-raiser for Democratic causes and the finance chairman of the Democratic National Committee, has been a generous contributor to the campaigns of Mayor Ed Rendell in Philadelphia and H. Carl McCall, the State Comptroller in New York.
Mr. Barrack, a prominent securities class-action lawyer, represents the Philadelphia and New York State public pension funds in huge class-action cases against companies whose stocks have tumbled because of undisclosed setbacks or accounting irregularities, earning substantial fees in the process. 
 Did the contributions influence officials who hired him? Not at all, Mr. Barrack says. "I'm not sure that there's anything wrong with political contributions like these, as long as we have vigorous representation and the best recovery for the shareholders," said Mr. Barrack, a partner in the Philadelphia firm of Barrack, Rodos & Bacine. The firm represents Mr. McCall's fund and two others in a securities case against the Cendant Corporation, potentially one of the most lucrative class-action cases in history.
But with the number of securities class-action cases up sharply this year, some people think there is something very wrong here, something that runs counter to the goals Congress had in 1995 when it enacted a law giving pension funds and other institutional investors an enhanced role in class-action litigation.
One critic is Howard Sirota, a veteran class-action lawyer in New York. "There is a non-arm's-length arrangement between Len Barrack and the politicians hiring him," Mr. Sirota said. "And what is wrong with that is that it puts last the economic interest of the people in whose name the cases are being filed -- all the small shareholders in the class."
Mr. Barrack and Mr. Sirota will be battling on Tuesday in Federal court in Newark over whether campaign donations by Mr. Barrack and his co-counsel disqualify their firms from adequately representing Cendant shareholders. He wants the legal work to go to whichever qualified firm will to do it for the lowest fee.
Another critic of lawyers who cultivate public business by contributing to politicians -- a practice known as "pay to play" -- is Harvey Goldschmid, the new general counsel of the Securities and Exchange Commission. "If we allow wild political contributions to be made to get public benefits," he said, "we're going to create questions about the integrity of the people and the integrity of the process."
Mr. Goldschmid was a member of an American Bar Association task force that last week called for new professional rules that would sharply restrict, and fully disclose, the contributions lawyers make to politicians who award them business. On Tuesday, as Mr. Sirota argues in Newark, the bar association's delegates will be debating the task force's recommendations at their annual convention in Toronto.
Leading class-action lawyers are offended by Mr. Sirota's complaint. "The pejorative implication is that one cannot be appropriately appointed if one is politically engaged -- and that would be a sorry state of affairs," said Samuel D. Heins, who represents the Minnesota pension fund and who has supported two Democrats on the fund's bipartisan five-member board.
Still, Mr. Sirota's challenge and the bar association's debate are being watched carefully by executives at the large pension funds that have become active in the class-action arena since 1995. The general counsel of the $13 billion Florida state pension fund plans to fly to Newark to observe the courtroom arguments.
The funds insist their lawyer-selection procedures are immune to political influence. Yet interviews with dozens of lawyers and pension executives show that in most states the hiring of a class-action lawyer is protected from politics by little more than an honor system. Indeed, questions about a law firm's political contributions have rarely been raised in the selection process until now. And some funds do not even try to use their clout to get lower fees from lawyers.
This was not how it was supposed to work under the 1995 legislation.
Before then, the class-action arena was dominated by a handful of giant firms -- Barrack, Rodos prominent among them -- considered by critics to be quick-draw lawyers who kept "pet plaintiffs" handy so they could sue at the drop of a stock and settle quickly for substantial fees. The passive plaintiff whose lawyer got to the courthouse first was likely to be named "lead plaintiff," even if his actual loss was minimal, and his lawyer got the bulk of the fees awarded by the court.
"It was a process that hadn't been working particularly well," said the S.E.C. chairman, Arthur Levitt Jr., with his typical understatement.
To improve it, Congress decreed that the investors with the largest financial losses had a presumptive right to be lead plaintiff, an arrangement that was expected to put powerful, sophisticated institutional investors -- like mutual funds, money managers and state and city pension funds -- in the driver's seat. Congress expected public pension funds and other institutional investors to take the reins of the securities class-action process out of lawyers' hands, demanding lower fees and larger recoveries for shareholders. But that model does not exactly match what is happening in, say, Florida.
The staff of the Florida State Board Administration has elected officials on its board, but they are not involved in the selection of counsel in the handful of cases the fund has filed. But then, no one really is.
"We don't choose them; they choose us," said Horace Schow 2d, the board's general counsel. "It's first come, first served, really." As for fees, any law firm representing the fund "is entitled to whatever the court will award them," Mr. Schow said, adding, "We haven't gotten around to negotiating them yet."
Philadelphia's approach to the class-action challenge also bears little resemblance to the Congressional vision, so far as anyone outside the City Solicitor's office can tell. Mr. Barrack acknowledged that he represents the city pension fund in several cases, but he did not identify them. The fund's chairman could not identify them either, explaining that all litigation is "the exclusive domain" of the City Solicitor, who is appointed by the Mayor.
And Deborah Butler, the chief deputy city solicitor, refused to discuss any aspect of the city's securities class-action procedures, including what steps are taken to insulate them from politics.
Mr. Barrack also filed numerous class-action cases on behalf of the State of Pennsylvania's public pension funds, after being hired in 1996 by the State Treasurer, who has since left office. But the cases were filed without the required approval of the pension fund's board and the Treasurer was not authorized to hire outside counsel, said Paul Tufano, the Governor's general counsel. After learning accidentally of Mr. Barrack's work, Mr. Tufano protested. Mr. Barrack balked at dropping the cases, Mr. Tufano said, and was ultimately allowed to proceed with one or two cases that were too far advanced to withdraw.
Mr. Barrack described the dispute as "a misunderstanding."
In Louisiana, William T. Reeves, general counsel for the Teachers Retirement System, said all his class-action work so far had been done by I. Walton Bader, a partner in a two-member law firm in White Plains. Despite its tiny size, the Bader firm has done extensive work for the Florida pension fund as well.
Mr. Bader was selected after he got in touch with the fund six years ago, Mr. Reeves said, but the fund is exploring other ways to handle its class-action legal work. And while Mr. Bader's fees are left to the discretion of the court, Mr. Reeves said, "If we ever do anything with anybody else, we're not going to do it that way." Typically, court-awarded fees in such cases can be as much as 30 percent of the settlement.
Mr. McCall, the New York State Comptroller, has retained three firms to handle class-action cases on behalf of the state's various pension funds. Besides Mr. Barrack's firm, they are Bernstein, Litowitz, Berger & Grossmann and Milberg, Weiss, Bershad Hynes & Lerach, both in New York. The Bernstein firm is co-counsel with Mr. Barrack in the Cendant case. The firms agreed to a sliding scale of fees that range from 3 percent of any early settlement exceeding $400 million to 20 percent of a post-trial award up to $150 million, a McCall spokesman said.
The New York Times reported yesterday that that the three firms and their lawyers have contributed more than $165,000 to Mr. McCall's campaign since they were retained in June 1997. Mr. McCall denied any impropriety, saying that the contributions are completely unrelated to the decision to retain the firms for the state's class-action work. Lawyers in his office chose the firms after rigorously reviewing the qualifications of 14 applicants, and he merely approved the selection, Mr. McCall said.
The role played by the State of Wisconsin Investment Board, which manages that state's $55 billion pension fund, seems to be a little closer to what Congress had in mind. The board is the lead plaintiff in the class-action cases against Cellstar and the Physician Computer Network, said Keith Johnson, assistant legal counsel.
In both cases, the outside lawyers were selected after a competitive review by a four-member panel made up of Mr. Johnson, an in-house investment manager, a representative of the Attorney General, and a local lawyer, recruited for his litigation expertise.
Fees are negotiated in advance and are always "way, way below" the 30 percent that is the norm in class-action cases, Mr. Johnson said.
In California, the California Public Employees Retirement System, called Calpers, has so insulated its procedures from politics that one of its board members -- the State Controller, Kathleen Connell -- has actually sued to overturn a new fund rule that bars her from accepting campaign contributions from anyone who does business with the fund, including the lawyers it hires.
Most other states active in class-action cases fall somewhere in between.
Paul Silvester, the State Treasurer in Connecticut, is ready to become active in appropriate cases, he said. And a state law prohibits him from awarding business to anyone who has contributed to the Treasurer's campaign. Mr. Silvester's outside lawyers are selected by Richard Blumenthal, the state's elected Attorney General, who is not barred from hiring firms that have contributed to his campaign. But Mr. Blumenthal said this week that he had an internal rule against hiring campaign contributors. "So the practical effect is to insulate the selection from politics," he said.
This growing dispute could discourage pension funds from getting involved in the class-action arena, as Congress hoped they would, said Michael Perino, a law professor at St. John's University in Brooklyn and co-author of a recent study that shows a sharp increase in class-action filings.
Mr. Sirota's challenge provides further evidence that lawyers must change their campaign contribution practices, said Michael A. Cardozo, a former president of the New York City Bar Association, who supports curbs on campaign contributions. "I think many major firms realize this is a bad practice and should be stopped," he said.
But many lawyers, including Mr. Barrack, see such restrictions as irrelevant and unnecessary. "There are serious problems out there in the campaign financing area, but this isn't one of them," he said.

. . . prompting proposals for change.
As lawyers square off over political contributions, the American Bar Association is debating a plan that would do the following:
-- Condemn deals that link contributions to legal work.
-- Require disclosure of contributions.
-- Urge that merit-based hiring procedures be used.
-- Prohibit lawyers from making contributions to get legal work.

HEADLINE: Sale of Coliseum Site Receives Approval

BYLINE: By CHARLES V. BAGLI 

 BODY:
The Metropolitan Transportation Authority approved the sale of the New York Coliseum site yesterday for $345 million, the first step in a three-year, $1.3 billion project that will alter the face of the Columbus Circle area.
By next summer, The Related Companies and Time Warner plan to start demolishing the Coliseum exhibition hall and an adjacent office tower to make way for a 2.1 million-square-foot complex that will house the world headquarters of Time Warner Inc. The project will include a 425-room hotel, 325 apartments, a concert hall for Jazz at Lincoln Center and Time Warner programming, as well as a digital television studio for Cable News Network and NY1 that will overlook Columbus Circle and Central Park. 
 At a news conference yesterday, Richard D. Parsons, the president of Time Warner, described the location as "the last great site in New York City and an appropriate home for the largest and most influential media company in the world." He said he expected the building and the ground-floor television studios to become an important tourist attraction.
Although this is the second time the 3.4-acre site has been sold in 13 years, city and state officials say this twin-towered project will go forward. But before construction can begin, the M.T.A. must empty the buildings at the site. If local residents are successful in challenging the project on environmental grounds, there could be further delays. In the meantime, the city is experimenting with ways to transform the anarchic traffic patterns at Columbus Circle into a more smoothly flowing urban crossroad.
Stephen M. Ross, the chairman of Related, which will develop the project in partnership with William L. Mack's Apollo Real Estate Advisers, said yesterday that he expected to complete construction in 2002.
Despite the fanfare, Mr. Parsons acknowledged that Time Warner would ask the city for a package of tax breaks. But, he added, the purchase of the Coliseum site was not contingent on those incentives. "It's not a question of looking for a handout," Mr. Parsons said, "as much as looking to construct mutually beneficial relationships."
Mr. Parsons' request poses a dilemma for the Giuliani administration, which had previously revoked a tax break worth about $50 million for any buyer of the site, saying a revived economy had made the subsidy superfluous. At a news conference at the Coliseum site on Tuesday, a senior city official said privately that Time Warner would have "to wait a long time" if it thought that the city would grant a tax break. Moments later, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani said he would "not rule it out."
The site has a tortured history. In 1985, city and state officials selected Mortimer B. Zuckerman's Boston Properties to develop the Coliseum site. Over the next nine years, Mr. Zuckerman ran into widespread opposition, lost his main tenant, Salomon Brothers, and suffered from the impact of a recession before abandoning the project in 1994.
The M.T.A. resumed its attempt to sell the site in 1996. A year ago, the M.T.A. was about to sell it to Millennium Partners, one of five developers competing for the site, when Mayor Giuliani blocked the project, demanding that a live theater or performance space be included.
Joseph B. Rose, the chairman of the city's Planning Department, said the delay allowed the M.T.A. to get a higher price as the real estate market improved.
It was only after the project was delayed last summer that Time Warner began negotiating with Related about a building at the Coliseum site. The company had been scouring the city for a new location for its corporate headquarters, which is currently at Rockefeller Center, in the shadow of the more high-profile operations of its rival, NBC.
"This is what Time Warner wanted," said Mitchell S. Stier, executive vice president of Julien J. Studley, the real estate broker for Time Warner. "There were always alternatives, but this was clearly the city's premier site."
Earlier this month, the M.T.A. and city officials narrowed their choices to proposals from Related and Millennium, which had the edge because it was offering the same amount of money as Related and was willing to pay for the land within 60 days of signing a contract. Related was unable to do the same, but on July 2, Mr. Parsons told city officials that Time Warner would guarantee to close the deal within two years at the agreed-upon price. The prestige of Time Warner and a proposed design that city officials considered superior to those of its competitors helped seal the deal.
"I'm disappointed," said Christopher Jeffries of Millennium. "But this is a high-stakes game. Bitterness and emotion have no part in it. I guess we can console ourselves with the other projects we have to do."

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS;
Slide in Shares Moderates While Earnings Jitters Persist

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
Stocks fell yesterday amid concern that earnings growth was not keeping up with stock prices.
The Dow Jones industrial average, which lost nearly 100 points on Tuesday and 400 points last week, fell only 19.82 points yesterday, to 8,914.96, led by Wal-Mart Stores, which dropped 2 9/16, to 60 7/8. The Standard & Poor's 500 index of 500 stocks declined 5.03, to 1,125.21. The technology-heavy Nasdaq composite index dropped 15.04, to 1,881.49. 
 "Earnings are growing, but at a slower pace," said John Niedenberger, a money manager with Advanced Investment Management in Pittsburgh, which oversees $3 billion. "I haven't done much buying lately. I'm not selling either. I'm just holding."
Losses would have been greater if not for I.B.M., which was up 2 5/16, to 127 3/4, on optimism that a new line of mainframe machines will bolster the company's sales. Steven Milunovich, a Merrill Lynch analyst, increased his 1999 earnings forecast for the company.
Profits for the 407 S.& P. 500 companies that have reported earnings so far are up just 3 percent, on average, down from 11 percent a year earlier, according to First Call.
Wal-Mart, whose stock is up 55 percent this year, is expected to report a 25 percent increase in fiscal first-quarter earnings on Aug. 12, based on the average forecast from analysts surveyed by I.B.E.S. International. Yet the nation's No. 1 merchant led a broad retreat in retailing issues.
Nor did investors find relief in small companies. The small-cap barometer, the Russell 2000 index, fell for a ninth day, down 0.3 percent. It is now off 2.5 percent this year, compared with a 16 percent gain for the large-company S.& P. 500.
General Motors declined 1/8, to 74 1/8, after workers voted to settle strikes that had cost the auto maker an estimated $3 billion in profit. The settlement lets G.M. trim fewer jobs than it had wanted and restricts its ability to close unprofitable plants. G.M. had risen 5.3 percent in the previous three days as optimism for a settlement grew.
Telecomunicacoes Brasileiras surged after Brazil's Government sold its controlling stake in the national telephone network for almost $19 billion. Telebras holder shares, up 5 11/16, to 119, were created by Merrill Lynch to let foreign investors trade the 12 companies spawned in the Telebras breakup as a single security. About 4.9 million shares changed hands after the stock opened for trading.
Cendant rose 1 3/8, to 18 3/8, after the chairman, Walter A. Forbes, and eight other board members resigned. Investors had been calling for Mr. Forbes to step down since the marketer and franchiser said it would restate earnings for the last three years because of accounting irregularities at its CUC International unit. CUC, which was headed by Mr. Forbes, merged with HFS Inc. to form Cendant last year.

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
Treasuries End Lower

(By Reuters)
 Treasury prices ended lower yesterday, pressured in part by corporate supply.
The price of the 30-year Treasury bond fell 13/32, to 105 2/32. The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, rose to 5.76 percent, from 5.74 percent on Tuesday.
"The long end is being held down primarily because of deals," said Astrid Adolfson, first vice president and senior economist at MCM Moneywatch.
Analysts expect $9 billion of debt to be issued by the end of the week. While few corporate deals were priced yesterday, positioning ahead of the the new supply led to some selling of Treasuries, traders said.
Caution ahead of today's report on second-quarter employment costs also weighed on trading yesterday. But there was good demand at the Treasury's auction of $15 billion in two-year notes. Following are the results of the auction:

HEADLINE: Senators Criticize Executive Over Lobbying on Satellites

BYLINE: By ERIC SCHMITT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 29

 BODY:
A current and a former top executive of the nation's largest satellite maker urged a Senate panel today not to subject the industry to a more restrictive licensing review by the Government.
But senators accused one executive, C. Michael Armstrong, former chairman of the Hughes Electronics Corporation, of lobbying President Clinton too aggressively, and of acting as an unofficial envoy between the United States and China to lift sanctions that blocked Hughes from launching two satellites aboard Chinese rockets. 
 "American corporations should not serve as mini-State Departments," Senator Fred Thompson, a Tennessee Republican who heads the Governmental Affairs Committee, scolded Mr. Armstrong at a hearing today.
Congress is weighing whether to revert the licensing control over satellites to the State Department, which had that responsibility until 1996, from the less restrictive Commerce Department. Lawmakers fear that engineers from Hughes, which is a unit of the General Motors Corporation, and from Loral Space and Communications gave Chinese officials sensitive information after a failed launching of a Loral satellite on a Chinese rocket in 1996.
Mr. Armstrong, who became the chairman of AT&T last fall after running Hughes for five years, and Steven D. Dorfman, Hughes's vice chairman, told senators that they did support giving the State Department the authority to approve any technical exchanges in a disaster review.
But the executives said transferring licensing authority to the State Department would hurt American companies.
Much of today's hearing focused on Mr. Armstrong, 59, who has headed Mr. Clinton's advisory panel on exports since it was created three years ago.
In August 1993, when the Administration imposed sanctions on China for shipping missile technology to Pakistan, Mr. Armstrong thrust himself into the dispute, which grounded two Hughes satellites, and threatened company profits and thousands of jobs in Southern California.
Documents released by the committee today, and by the White House in the last two weeks, showed how Mr. Armstrong worked to free his satellites from the sanctions and, two years later, how he pushed the White House to shift the licensing jurisdiction to the Commerce Department.
In a letter on Oct. 29, 1993, Mr. Armstrong reminded Mr. Clinton that he had supported the President's economic package and the North American Free Trade Agreement. "I am respectfully requesting your involvement to resolve the China sanctions," Mr. Armstrong said.
A Dec. 8, 1993, internal White House memorandum to Mr. Clinton's national security adviser, Anthony Lake, said, "Armstrong is now fairly directly threatening to wage a more public campaign against the Administration's sanction." Mr. Armstrong, the memorandum said, was threatening to "write op-eds and/or spur union opposition."
Mr. Armstrong said today that he had never compromised national security. But senators voiced concern that Mr. Armstrong's blunt approach pushed the limits of corporate advocacy.
"At a bare minimum, you need some help in letter-writing," Senator Pete V. Domenici, Republican of New Mexico, told Mr. Armstrong. "That's not any way to write the President."
In seeking to have the sanctions lifted, Mr. Armstrong mixed diplomacy with business by passing messages between senior Chinese and American officials like Samuel R. Berger, Mr. Clinton's deputy national security adviser.
By early 1994, President Clinton reached a compromise that allowed Hughes to modify its satellites and refile its applications with Commerce. The Administration ended the sanctions in November 1994.
Mr. Armstrong was still waging a war to shift licensing authority over all commercial satellites to the less restrictive Commerce Department.
By March 1996, Mr. Armstrong enlisted Mr. Berger's help in reversing the State Department objections. "It was indeed some battle, two and a half years and more politics than a textbook," he told Mr. Berger in Oct. 17, 1996 "thank you" note.

HEADLINE: Japan Picks Ex-Leader To Find Way Out of Crisis

BYLINE: By STEPHANIE STROM 

DATELINE: TOKYO, July 29

 BODY:
Kiichi Miyazawa, a veteran politician who only five years ago was forced out of office as Prime Minister, was named Finance Minister today and charged with steering Japan out of its worst financial crisis in decades.
Mr. Miyazawa is a familiar face to American officials, who have worked with him for more than four decades, as he held a variety of senior posts. And he is a veteran of the Finance Ministry, the powerful institution that in recent years has fallen into disgrace in a series of scandals and accusations of mismanagement of the world's second-largest economy. 
 But it was also Mr. Miyazawa, serving as Finance Minister in the late 1980's, who oversaw the growth of the Japan's "bubble economy," and starting this week he will be charged with cleaning up the enormous mess that the bursting of that bubble has caused.
Although Mr. Miyazawa, 78, appeared hesitant to accept the post in recent days, he was persuaded to do so by Keizo Obuchi, the new president of the ruling Liberal Democratic Party who is to be confirmed as Prime Minister on Thursday.
According to Japanese news reports, Mr. Obuchi has also chosen his deputy, Masahiko Komura, to be Foreign Minister; the Deputy Chief Cabinet Secretary, Fukushiro Nukaga, as Defense Minister and the former Education Minister, Kaoru Yosano, as Trade Minister.
 Mr. Obuchi has little expertise in financial engineering himself and needs a powerful Finance Minister to give his administration credibility in a skeptical world. Many consider the choice of a Finance Minister to be more important than the selection of the Prime Minister, and in many ways, the next Finance Minister will in fact be a more pivotal and powerful figure than Mr. Obuchi. But while Mr. Miyazawa is well known internationally, and negotiated the Clinton Administration's first major trade accord with Japan, he has not been considered politically powerful at home.
Mr. Miyazawa will take the ministry's helm at a critical juncture. The economy has stalled, unemployment is climbing to unheard of levels and the banking system is seriously ill.
More and more economists are drawing comparisons to the Showa Depression of the late 1920's, which lasted more than a decade. The Japanese press has compared Mr. Miyazawa to Korekiyo Takahashi, a politician and financial expert of the time, whose suspension of the gold standard and increase of government spending helped combat the Depression.
It is not clear what Mr. Miyazawa will do to fix Japan's economic woes. He has reportedly been involved in the "total plan" to clean up the banking system, which will be debated in the special session of Parliament that begins Thursday and which is generally regarded as a step in the right direction.
World financial markets have so far greeted rumors of Mr. Miyazawa's appointment with cautious optimism. The yen has traded in a narrow band ever since his name first appeared in the press as the favorite candidate, and the Nikkei Stock Average has climbed as his appointment has seemed more certain.
A concern about Mr. Miyazawa is his age. He initially declined the job for that reason.
But his years of experience dealing with Japan's carefully orchestrated government financial system, including a stint as Finance Minister in the 1980's, as well as his outspoken support of Mr. Obuchi in the recent party election, made him an obvious choice for a job that few people seemed to want.
He will inherit the task of weeding out the banking system, which is suffocating under bad debt, and of fashioning policies to drag Japan out of its doldrums.
Mr. Miyazawa did speak out last fall, when the banking and securities industries seemed on the verge of a meltdown, urging Prime Minister Ryutaro Hashimoto to commit to using public funds to bail out the financial system.
Mr. Miyazawa is regarded as one of the more globally minded of the senior ruling party members. He speaks English well, one of the few Japanese politicians of his generation to do so, and his daughter, Keiko, is married to Christopher J. Lafleur, deputy chief of mission at the American Embassy in Tokyo.
He worked closely with Nicholas F. Brady, the Treasury Secretary during the Bush Administration, in putting together a bailout for Latin American nations.
And earlier this year, Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin sought out Mr. Miyazawa while the Japanese politician was on a trip to New York to suggest some ideas Japan might want to consider for pumping up domestic demand and jump-starting its economy.
Mr. Miyazawa was forced to resign as Finance Minister in 1988 after being implicated in a scandal surrounding the Recruit Company's efforts to buy political influence.
He is also remembered in some quarters for his economic prescriptions in the mid-1980's, when he was a high-ranking party official jockeying to become a candidate for Prime Minister. His views helped create the "bubble" that defined Japan in the 1980's and became the root of its problems today.
Mr. Miyazawa will be the first former Japanese Prime Minister in the postwar era to take a job in the Cabinet after serving as Prime Minister. He was Prime Minister from 1991 until 1993, when he resigned after the Liberal Democrats lost control of Parliament, a setback from which they have yet to fully recover.
