HEADLINE: Sexual Harassment Trial Plumbs 'Atmosphere' in a Trenton Agency

BYLINE: By IVER PETERSON 

DATELINE: TRENTON, July 29

 BODY:
Only two former assistant attorneys general are named in a sexual harassment case brought by one of their colleagues that is working its way slowly through a state court here. But, in a sense, an entire division of the Attorney General's office has ended up on trial.
In her testimony, Barbara Davis, now 40, and other witnesses have painted a picture of the Criminal Justice Division, where she worked from 1989 to 1993, as a place of blatant sexual banter and touching, where male superiors openly conducted sexual affairs with subordinate women and where a woman's physical attributes were fair game for public comment even after death, in gruesome crime scene photographs. 
 "I just think the public should know what was going on behind the closed doors of that department -- what it was like," Ms. Davis said in an interview between court sessions recently. "Women were just treated like they were nothing, and it was just devastating to be there. Just the fact of having seven or eight witnesses come forward to testify about what was going on has restored my faith a little bit."
Ms. Davis has accused Richard T. Carley, the former deputy director of the Division of Criminal Justice, of repeatedly touching and propositioning her while she worked as an assistant attorney general in the division. She has also accused the state and her immediate supervisor, Gregory Sakowicz, of ignoring her complaints about Mr. Carley. She said she had become unemployable as a result of the harassment and the civil lawsuit, which went to trial five weeks ago, and is asking for a life's salary as well as unspecified punitive damages.
The state maintains that Ms. Davis failed to notify the proper authorities, and says it acted swiftly to punish Mr. Carley once she filed her suit in 1994.
Officials at the Department of Law and Public Safety, which is led by the State Attorney General, would not comment on the record about Ms. Davis's allegation, noting that the trial, whose outcome will turn in part on the atmosphere within the Criminal Justice Division, was still in progress. But one official, speaking on the condition of anonymity, said that the department, which works closely with the police in investigating and prosecuting prominent crimes, had a reputation as a "macho" outfit in the early 1990's.
"You had 150 lawyers in there, many of whom were ex-cops, and they were working with police investigators and, yes, it had a reputation for having a precinct-house atmosphere in those days," the official said. "But it has changed since then," the official added, because of the trial, and because of stricter sexual harassment laws.
Office atmosphere is a major element in proving sexual harassment under New Jersey law, said Alan Hyde, a professor at Rutgers Law School in Newark who teaches a course on labor and employment law, because it can have the effect of discriminating against women by creating an unbearable working environment for them. Stressing that he had not followed the Davis case, Mr. Hyde said the heavily male atmosphere of police work could have the illegal effect of telling women they could not be on an equal footing with men.
"That sets up the classic sexual harassment case, which comes from the male defense of a previously male preserve," Mr. Hyde said. "Anyone who has spent any time with law enforcement people knows that there is a lot of sexual humor and gallows humor about things like the condition of corpses. Some of it seems natural, but some of it is also a way of preserving a male domain, of saying, 'This is a place for guys with tough stomachs and steel nerves.' "
Mr. Hyde later added: "You can't hang a sign on the door that says, 'Men Only' in words, but this case seems to say they hung the sign not with words but with deeds."
In fact, Ms. Davis and several supporting witnesses described such an atmosphere. If a crime under investigation was a sex crime against women, Ms. Davis said, Mr. Carley would ask for photographs of the victim and comment on her body. Mr. Sakowicz had a joke statue in his office resembling a penis, a police investigator testifying for Ms. Davis said. Mr. Carley, Ms. Davis said, displayed pictures of bathing beauties in his office.
Several witnesses testified in support of Ms. Davis that Mr. Carley was often uncomfortably familiar with women in his department. He began a meeting with Carol Stanton Meier, a deputy attorney general, by telling her she had "a nice body," Ms. Meier testified. Another witness said Mr. Carley had dangled the keys for a sports car in front of her and said, "If I give you a ride in my Porsche, will you do me?"
But although the women confirmed stories about Mr. Carley's occasionally lurid language and comments and about the atmosphere of male swagger in the Criminal Justice Division in the early 1990's, none directly confirmed witnessing the acts that brought Mr. Carley before Judge Maria Marinari Sypek and a jury of five men and five women.
Two police officers, friends of Ms. Davis, testified that she had complained of her treatment to them, and one of the officers, Uku Manikis, told the court last week that he had offered to accompany Ms. Davis to her office and to arrest Mr. Carley if he made one of his unwelcome approaches to Ms. Davis in the officer's presence.
Ms. Davis has accused Mr. Carley of touching her breasts, of stuffing money in her brassiere, of caressing her from behind and of suggesting that they have an affair. Mr. Carley admitted in court that he was conducting an affair with a division subordinate at the time, even though he was married. He has since divorced.
"He would come up behind me and grab me," Ms. Davis said in the interview. "He would ask for oral sex. He would ask me if I was wearing any underwear that day. It was just devastating."
But Mr. Carley, under intense questioning by Clifford Van Syoc, Ms. Davis's lawyer, denied that he had touched Ms. Davis or requested sex with her. A state investigator who looked into Ms. Davis's charges in 1994 testified that Mr. Carley admitted saying things like, "nice boobs," or "nice butt," but that he had said the comments had not been aimed at Ms. Davis.
Ms. Davis maintains that she complained about Mr. Carley to Mr. Sakowicz, her direct supervisor, and that Mr. Sakowicz did nothing. Mr. Sakowicz denied that Ms. Davis went to him, and the state contends that in any case, Ms. Davis failed to take her complaint to any of several offices established to enforce non-discrimination law for state employees. The state asserts that Ms. Davis concocted her charges and the lawsuit after she was passed over for a promotion. Her inability to find work as a lawyer since leaving state employment in 1994 is not due to the shock of what happened or the burden of the trial, as Ms. Davis contends, but to her own malingering and use of painkillers and alcohol, the state maintains.
Once Ms. Davis filed her suit in 1994, the state did investigate her complaints and demoted Mr. Carley in rank and in pay, moving him to the Alcoholic Beverage Control Division, where he has no supervisory authority. The sanctions against Mr. Carley do not amount to an admission of guilt by the state, said Cynthia Jacob, the lawyer defending the state and Mr. Sakowicz. Mr. Carley may be liable for further punishment, depending on how the trial ends.
Ms. Jacob maintains that the department's written policy on sexual harassment gave Ms. Davis several places to turn for help, including the department's Division on Civil Rights, the state's Equal Employment Opportunities Commission, and the equal employment opportunities office in the Department of Law.
"The bottom line is that we took prompt remedial action," Ms. Jacob said during a pause in the trial. "We did everything employers are required to do under the law when faced with a sexual harassment complaint."

HEADLINE: Tallying Losses Instead of Gains In G.M. Dispute

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: FLINT, Mich., July 29

 BODY:
Strikes against two General Motors auto parts factories ended here today after nearly eight weeks as workers ratified a settlement and trooped back into the plants, but the showdown has harmed G.M. and the United Automobile Workers while appearing to produce limited gains for either side.
The strikes cost G.M. about $3 billion in after-tax losses, the nation's costliest labor dispute ever before adjusting for inflation, yet produced a deal that mainly calls for a few small changes in work rules at the factories here and other parts factories in Ohio and Indiana. 
 The dispute has left G.M. dealerships with few popular models, so G.M.'s market share in July and possibly August most likely will fall below the Ford Motor Company's for the first time since a national strike against G.M. in 1970. And G.M. could have serious problems producing enough 1999 model cars and trucks in time to compete with rival auto makers' 1999 offerings this autumn.
U.A.W. leaders have praised the agreement since it was announced on Tuesday, but they have relatively little to show for the $1 billion in paychecks that their members gave up. The strike mostly maintained the status quo, but at the cost of giving the union a national image for taking workers out on long strikes. That could hurt the union's planned push over the next several years to organize workers at independent auto parts makers.
The strikes also cost G.M. nearly half a year's worth of earnings, which will reduce workers' profit-sharing checks at the end of the year. And the further erosion of G.M.'s market share means that the world's largest auto maker may need even fewer workers in the future.
G.M. could probably rebuild the factories here from the ground up for less than $1 billion, so Wall Street analysts questioned what the company and the union had achieved after fighting each other to a standstill.
"At the end of the day, they both are suicidal -- nobody won, but you can conclusively say both sides lost a little," said Maryann Keller, an auto analyst at Furman Selz.
The final details of the settlement, presented to workers before their ratification votes today, include some small adjustments in work rules.
Seeking to rally public opinion against the U.A.W., G.M. often painted the walkouts as a struggle over whether decades-old work rules would continue to allow about 600 welders to draw a full day's pay for four to six hours of work. The welders, who labor in a hot factory with no air-conditioning and must move 55-pound engine braces with their bare hands because there is little automation available, must produce a certain number of parts during a shift. Once they have met their quotas, they can stop work, although they are not allowed to leave the factory until eight hours have elapsed.
The final settlement maintains these work rules but raises the number of parts a welder must produce before stopping work. Yet even the increases in required parts are fairly modest and will be phased in gradually. Welders producing engine braces for the Chevrolet Astro van, for example, will be required to produce 800 braces during an eight-hour shift by next March, compared with 768 now.
One of these welders, Kim Cramer, said that he would still be able to finish work early. "I'll get done in five hours or six hours; it depends on how hard I work."
The accord protects G.M. against further strikes for a year at some of its most important auto parts factories here and in Indianapolis and Dayton, Ohio. In exchange, G.M. promised that at least through the end of next year it would not sell or close a spark plug factory here or brake factories in Dayton, a distinct possibility that had worried workers in both cities.
But the fate of these factories will be on the table again when G.M. and the U.A.W. start bargaining next summer on a new national agreement. The current accord will expire in September 1999, and many workers and U.A.W. officials said here today that they were already bracing for another possible strike then.
"It'll be just as hard fought as this one," said Dennis Savage, 55, who operates parts-making equipment. "Better start saving now."
G.M. and U.A.W. leaders agreed on Tuesday to talk more regularly to head off future strikes. But the two sides continued to aim barbed remarks at each other, suggesting that the peace may not last long. The two sides may also have a tacit understanding not to engage in further showdowns of this sort for a while, although neither party would confirm this.
In the first sign that restarting production may be a long, slow process, G.M. announced today that it would reopen just four of its 25 closed assembly plants in North America on Friday. The first assembly plants to reopen will be a highly profitable sport utility vehicle factory in Janesville, Wis., two small-car factories in Lansing, Mich., and a Cadillac factory in Hamtramck, Mich. G.M. officials have said they would like to reopen all of their assembly plants within a week, but outside experts predicted that it could take 10 days to return to full production..
Frank Ursomarso, the chairman of the G.M. section of the National Automobile Dealers Association, said that many dealers might not have a full selection of cars and trucks again until September.
Mr. Ursomarso's Pontiac and GMC dealership in Wilmington, Del., has only 27 vehicles in stock, compared with a typical inventory of 125 vehicles before the strike. He predicted that once the assembly plants reopened, it would be three weeks before he started to receive new vehicles.
Susan Jacobs, an automotive sales consultant in Rutherford, N.J., said that G.M.'s short-term market share, which was hovering around 31 percent before the strike began, would not recover until October. The company will announce its July sales next week.
G.M.'s long-term market share will most likely continue to drop because of the auto maker's inability to find a way to cut costs more quickly. Ms. Jacobs said, "I think they'll drop below 30 next year and just trend down over the coming years."
G.M.'s board is scheduled to meet on Monday to consider the company's latest plans to improve its competitiveness by eliminating unprofitable car models, closing factories and streamlining the bureaucracies at its divisions. But the odds of a drastic move affecting thousands of workers seem to have faded. G.M. and U.A.W. officials said that they had not discussed factory closings, and G.M. has also promised to start working more closely with the U.A.W., making any sudden move unlikely.
G.M. could stop production of some unprofitable models, like the Buick Riviera, without closing factories or affecting employment. The U.A.W. has expressed no opposition to such a limited move. G.M. may also cut further its white-collar staff, much of which is not unionized.
Wall Street analysts were generally unimpressed with Tuesday's settlement and predicted that G.M. would continue to lag behind more efficient rivals like Ford and Chrysler, to say nothing of low-cost producers in Japan and South Korea. Shares of G.M. edged down 12.5 cents today, to $74.125.
"G.M. doesn't get one step closer to where it needs to be on cost and competitiveness," Ms. Keller, the analyst, said. "If this is what they get in two factories after 54 days of strikes, how are they going to get this across the country?"

GRAPHIC: Photo: Workers from a G.M. metal-stamping plant in Flint, Mich., cast their votes yesterday to ratify a settlement. Employees at two plants overwhelmingly approved the deal, ending a walkout that lasted nearly eight weeks. (Associated Press)(pg. D5)

HEADLINE: Business Improvement District At Grand Central Is Dissolved

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
After a year of mounting acrimony between Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani and several of Manhattan's business improvement districts, senior city officials said yesterday that they were putting the Grand Central Partnership, one of the largest and by far the most combative of the districts, out of business.
The Department of Business Services, which is responsible for monitoring the city's 34 districts, said in a formal statement that the city would not renew the Grand Central district's contract, which expires tomorrow. Without its municipal contract, the district cannot collect the fees that property owners are assessed for a broad array of services, including garbage pickup, security and street beautification. 
 The department cited "a pattern of noncompliance with regulations and noncooperation with officials" by the board of the district. It singled out the district's president, Daniel Biederman, who holds the same salaried position at two other business improvement districts in midtown Manhattan, for failing to respond to a City Hall directive in May that he no longer head more than one of the business improvement groups.
In addition, city officials said, Mr. Biederman and his staff failed to respond to city requests for financial information, failed to abide by city rules that require competitive bidding in awarding contracts, and did not allow sufficient city oversight into the district's operations.
Joseph J. Lhota, the Deputy Mayor for operations, said in an interview that the city might have moved to disband another of Mr. Biederman's districts, the 34th Street Partnership, if its contract were up for renewal. The city contract with that district, like the Bryant Park Restoration Corporation, also headed by Mr. Biederman, does not expire until 2001.
"I think the Grand Central and 34th Street districts stick out like a sore thumb," said Mr. Lhota, adding that the city's move against Mr. Biederman's groups did not reflect widespread problems with the other districts, which were first formed in early 1980's to supplement city services with contributions by property owners.
Mr. Biederman and other officials of the Grand Central district declined to be interviewed, instead offering a terse prepared statement saying that they were "surprised" by the city's action.
"The board of the Grand Central Partnership has been negotiating in good faith on its contract with the city's Law Department and will continue to do so until this matter is amicably resolved," the statement said.
Debra R. Weeks, commissioner of the Department of Business Services, said yesterday that the district had failed to meet repeated city demands to change its practices in areas ranging from "late audits to ignoring oversight regulations."
"The Grand Central district apparently has viewed itself as a private organization rather than an entity spending public money," said Andrew Schwartz, a spokesman for the department. Although the city did not have evidence of criminal activity by anyone involved in the district, he said it had received "many complaints from merchants" about the unresponsiveness of the district.
Yesterday's announcement goes to the heart of the unusual place that business improvement districts have occupied in New York City since they were created. In one sense, they act as an arm of government, augmenting services like police protection and sanitation, while at the same time acting as a direct extension of the businesses that finance them.
The announcement also highlights the debate over the money they spend. The districts contend that the fees they receive from property owners are private funds. But Mayor Giuliani contends that since the city collects the fees on behalf of the districts, they are, in essence, public funds, and the city should have a say in how the money is spent.
In June, the Mayor angered people involved in several of the districts by ordering that their routine daily operations -- including private security patrols, the installation of planters and street signs, and garbage pickup -- be submitted to greater oversight by the Department of Business Services. But other changes in the city rules have been directed at Mr. Biederman and the districts he heads.
Unlike any of the other groups, the Grand Central and 34th Street districts have sold bonds to raise huge sums to pay for their projects, like the installation of spotlights that illuminate the facade of Grand Central Terminal on 42d Street, and planters, street benches and signs in both districts. Since first selling bonds in the late 1980's, the Grand Central district has accumulated a debt to its bondholders of $32 million, according to city records.
Last year, Mr. Giuliani forbade the districts to use bond financing, saying the city could be liable for the debt if a district encountered financial problems or disbanded.
Ms. Weeks said yesterday that property owners in the Grand Central district would continue to be assessed fees as they have in the past, and that the money would be held by the city "to insure that the holders of bonds are fully protected."
City officials and business people who control other business improvement districts said the directive raised unresolved questions. Mr. Lhota, who acknowledged that the current level of assessments being paid by property owners in the Grand Central District would exceed the amount necessary to pay off its bond debt, said the city would return some portion of the assessments to the property owners after they are collected by the Department of Finance.
He also said the city would insure that "level of service to property owners and tenants in the Grand Central area is not reduced."
Executives at other districts, citing their fears of angering City Hall, declined to comment publicly on the Mayor's action. But privately, some said that the move raised a variety of difficult legal and financial questions. One was whether the Grand Central group could continue to operate, at least temporarily, relying on bank accounts that it had already filled through its bond sales and prior assessments on property owners.
Some questioned whether Mr. Biederman and other directors of the district might challenge the city in court, maintaining that it was denying them their right to operate a public-spirited, nonprofit organization.
Although city officials declined to provide details, they said the decision to deny a contract to the Grand Central group came after months of negotiations. Although the group's five-year contract with the city had expired at the end of June, they said Mr. Biederman and his board had been granted a one-month extension. During that period, "BID officials were unresponsive to city officials," said the statement from the Department of Business Services.
After the district's contract expires tomorrow, the statement said, Mr. Biederman and his board had been ordered to provide the city with a "full financial accounting of its assets and liabilities and deliver all books, records and accounts" to the city within 10 days.
Mr. Lhota said one possible way to sustain the neighborhood services that have been provided by the Grand Central group would be to reconstitute it under new management, but added that he knew of no one who was prepared to do so.
One detail of the Grand Central group's finances that has long been a source of controversy among business people and city officials is Mr. Biederman's salary, which the city's formal statement yesterday listed at a combined total of $335,000, plus $30,000 a year in health benefits, from the three district he heads.
One executive involved in another district, who agreed to speak on the condition of anonymity, said the reason for the apparent collapse in negotiations between the Grand Central group and the city was that an extension of the group's contract would have required Mr. Biederman to step down.

HEADLINE: Senate Deadlocked on Rules for Health Bill Debate

BYLINE: By LIZETTE ALVAREZ 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 29

 BODY:
Senate Republican and Democratic leaders deadlocked on patients' rights legislation today, all but guaranteeing that the Senate would not debate the contentious but politically popular issue until September, if then.
For weeks now, Senate Republican and Democratic leaders, whose proposals differ greatly in scope and detail, have been unable to agree on how many amendments can be offered when a Republican bill is taken up. Republicans want to allow three amendments on each side, arguing that there is not enough time for limitless debate. Democrats want to offer as many as 20, saying the issue warrants a broad, full-fledged debate. 
 Without an agreement, Republicans will not bring the bill up for debate. The House narrowly approved a Republican patients' rights bill on Friday, leaving it up to the Senate to act.
Each party's Senate leader blamed the other for delay.
"If we don't take up the health care reform legislation dealing with H.M.O.'s," Senator Trent Lott of Mississippi, the majority leader, said, "it will be because the Democrats would not agree to take it up. It's their fault. I want the record to be clear."
Senator Tom Daschle of South Dakota, the Democratic leader, disagreed.
"What are they afraid of?" Mr. Daschle asked. "Why not have a good debate? What's stopping them from giving us the full opportunity to debate this issue as we are each of the appropriations' bills?"
Frustrated by the stalemate, Senator John H. Chafee, Republican of Rhode Island, and four other senators unveiled their own compromise patients' protections bill today. Mr. Chafee said the partisan logjam over the two main bills was jeopardizing the prospects for legislation this year, despite its grass-roots appeal.
Republicans and Democrats adamantly disagree on the issue of lawsuits, among other things in the two bills.
While the Democratic bill would permit patients to sue a managed care company in state court for the denial of benefits, something most consumers cannot now do, Republicans prefer to allow an outside review panel handle the dispute.
The plan by Mr. Chafee and Senator Bob Graham, Democrat of Florida, the lead co-sponsor, tries to bridge the gap, although it skews more toward the Democratic approach.
It would allow consumers to sue their managed care companies for lost wages and other economic damages, and not for emotional or punitive damages, as the Democratic bill would permit.
Senator Arlen Specter, a Pennsylvania Republican and a former District Attorney in Philadelphia who supports Mr. Chafee's bill, said the Republican leadership's bill provided "no incentive at all" for managed care companies to respond to patient concerns.

HEADLINE: BOXING;
Tyson Loses His Temper at His License Hearing

BYLINE: By TIMOTHY W. SMITH 

DATELINE: TRENTON, July 29

 BODY:
Mike Tyson, displaying the same loss of control that compelled him to bite off a piece of Evander Holyfield's ear and led to his banishment from boxing, testified today before the State Athletic Control Board to apply for the reinstatement of his New Jersey boxing license.
Accompanied by two lawyers and a parade of character witnesses, Tyson made his case inside the hearing room at the Richard J. Hughes Justice Complex here. But the efforts to portray him as a changed man who deserved the right to fight again may have been undercut by a moody appearance that ranged from a near-tearful apology to an angry outburst laced with an expletive. 
 The board did not rule on Tyson's petition, delaying a decision until it could convene in a private session on Aug. 6. The final ruling could come two days later on Aug. 8, although the board has 45 days to render a decision.
Tyson said he did not reapply for a license in Nevada, because he wanted to make a fresh start. But his adviser, Shelly Finkel, said Tyson might apply for a boxing license in other states in the future.
Flanked by a gallery of reporters and friends, including his wife, Dr. Monica Tyson, and the boxer Larry Holmes, Tyson at first expressed remorse for biting Holyfield's ears in a heavyweight bout on June 28, 1997, an act that prompted Nevada to revoke his license. But Tyson said he did it only after constant head butting from Holyfield made his "head foggy and incoherent" and that he became "desperate, irate and I just snapped. Nothing really mattered."
At one point, Tyson seemed to choke back tears when talking about how his life had been irrevocably altered by the events on June 28.
"I'm sorry for what I did," Tyson said. "It will haunt me for the rest of my life."
When Michael Haas, an assistant Attorney General, asked Tyson if he thought he would ever commit a similar act if he were granted a New Jersey boxing license, Tyson said: "I doubt it. My life has been devastated over this."
Dr. Bertram Rotman, a Wayne, N.J., psychologist who evaluated Tyson for an hour on July 21, testified that he saw no reason why Tyson shouldn't get his license back, and Raymond A. Reddin, one of Tyson's lawyers, read a brief letter from Holyfield that said the fighter did not object to Tyson's getting a license from New Jersey. Although he slumped forward on the table occasionally, Tyson patiently sat through the three-and-a-half- hour proceeding, and withstood a 43-minute questioning from Haas before he snapped.
Most of Haas's questions centered around Tyson's 1992 conviction in Indiana for the rape of Desiree Washington and the biting incident with Holyfield. Tyson, who was dressed in a dark gray pin-striped suit and wearing a black T-shirt, twice complained of the cold temperature in the room. But following the questioning from Haas, Tyson reached a slow boil. When Haas asked him if he wanted to make a final statement, Tyson declined, saying he was too angry to do so.
A few minutes later as one of his lawyers, Anthony J. Fusco Jr., was delivering an impassioned closing statement Tyson couldn't contain his anger.
"What he did was wrong," Fusco said. "But how many times does one individual have to hear are you sorry for what you did?"
Tyson angrily interjected, agreeing with Fusco: "You know what I mean, man? Why do I have to relive my . . . "
Just as Tyson uttered an expletive, Fusco reached over and stopped him and told the panel hearing the petition that Tyson was not angry at them, but was merely expressing the frustration that had built up in him during his exile from the ring.
Later, in a hastily called news conference in the lobby of the Hughes Justice Complex, Tyson said he used the wrong choice of words when he told the panel that he was too angry to deliver a final statement in his own behalf. As for the outburst and the expletive, Tyson said: "I never lost my cool. I was just expressing my hurt and frustration."
Today's public hearing, which drew a throng of fans and protesters from the New Jersey chapter of the National Organization for Women, attracted great news media interest, with television satellite broadcast trucks crowding the sidewalk outside the building and banks of television cameras corralled by wooden barricades inside. The proceeding was broadcast live by ESPN, CNN-SI and Fox News Network.
Larry Hazzard, New Jersey State boxing commissioner, said the board decided to hold a public hearing on Tyson's request because it wanted to avoid the appearance that Tyson was getting a back-room deal.
If Tyson is allowed to return to boxing, it will be a boon to the casino economy in Atlantic City. Tyson hasn't held a New Jersey boxing license since 1991. His last bout in New Jersey came on Dec. 8, 1990, against Alex Stewart. Finkel said if Tyson gets his license back, his first bout would be in New Jersey.
Under state commission rules and New Jersey law, Tyson has to provide "clear and convincing evidence of good character, honesty, responsibility and integrity" in order to get a New Jersey boxing license. There was a videotaped statement attesting to Tyson's honesty and good character from Camille Ewald, the 93-year-old woman who ran the house in Catskill, N.Y., where Cus D'Amato trained the young Tyson. And there was an explanation from Chuck Wepner and Bobby Czyz as to why a boxer would do what Tyson did to Holyfield during a bout.
Czyz, a television boxing analyst, described it as "performance blackout."
"In a world title fight, if I hit an opponent and his eye fell out of his head, I'd eat it before he could pick it up and put it back in," Czyz said regarding the state of mind that boxers have during a title bout.

HEADLINE: In West Africa, a Grisly Extension of Rebel Terror

BYLINE: By BARBARA CROSSETTE 

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, July 29

 BODY:
A rebel movement dislodged early this year from a brief period in power in Sierra Leone is waging a campaign of butchery against civilians, human rights groups and Government officials said today.
By the hundreds, poor farmers and villagers -- men, women and children -- have been shot, disemboweled or mutilated by having limbs hacked off, survivors report. Thousands are thought to have died, many of them in a rebel campaign code-named No Living Thing. 
 Some victims have been turned into messengers of death, disfigured and sent away bearing letters warning President Ahmad Tejan Kabbah and the African peacekeeping troops who restored him to office in March that armed resistance, led by remnants of a former military junta, will continue and that no one is safe.
A refugee who reached Guinea, his arms hacked to stumps, told his story to a representative of Refugees International, a Washington-based advocacy group that videotaped his testimony.
"You don't want a military government," the refugee said the rebels told him. "You say you want a civilian government. Then we will have to cut off your hands. Then go to Tejan Kabbah and he will have to give you more hands."
Another refugee, with bandaged stumps where his hands had been, shook with fear as he repeated again and again that he would never be able to use a toilet without help.
"What they are doing really is atrocious," said Fode Dabor, Sierra Leone's representative at the United Nations. "I've never seen it before. This is one of the most vicious rebel organizations in the world."
Several hundred thousand people have fled their homes in recent months for Government-controlled areas or refugee camps in Liberia and Guinea. Hospitals in Sierra Leone and in the camps are treating a steady stream of mutilated victims.
On Thursday the United Nations is to convene a conference on Sierra Leone to discuss how to begin rebuilding a country that has endured decades of ruthless military leaders since its independence in 1961.
Today Secretary General Kofi Annan met with President Kabbah, who is in New York for the conference. Mr. Annan said later that Mr. Kabbah had told him that Nigeria had turned over Foday Sankoh, the rebel leader, to Sierra Leone today to stand trial on charges of treason and crimes against humanity, including atrocities he has been associated with for more than seven years.
Mr. Sankoh, who was arrested in Nigeria last year while trying to buy weapons, told his followers in a televised message broadcast today after his return to Freetown, Sierra Leone's capital, that they should end their terrorism.
"I do not support the cutting off of people's hands and feet, and when I heard about this I felt sick," he said.
Mr. Kabbah, a civilian and the first democratically elected President, served for only a year before being overthrown in May 1997 by the Armed Forces Revolutionary Council and the Revolutionary United Front.
The Front is now trying to regain power even as Mr. Kabbah's Government begins to re-establish shaky institutions and repair a ravaged capital. Political power and economic spoils have been the object of this struggle and those that have gone before, though the rebels have no political platform, according to experts on the region.
Human rights groups came to the United Nations this week in force to press participants in the Sierra Leone conference to mount an international drive to insure that trials are held and punishments meted out to the rebels.
Mr. Dabor, Sierra Leone's representative, said in an interview that when he was in Rome recently for the conference on establishing the International Criminal Court, he spoke publicly about why such a body is important to Africa.
"If we had had a court like the I.C.C. now, these guys would face trials for crimes against humanity," he said. "We are talking of hundreds of cases."
Mr. Dabor said the rebels are strongest in eastern Sierra Leone, where they have been selling diamonds to buy weapons.
"Our problem has been the diamonds," he said. "We have a lot of people who want to lay their hands on our diamonds. I know that the rebels finance their operations by the diamonds they recover from this mining area. That's why they don't want to give up these places."
Scott Campbell, who recently completed a report on violence in Sierra Leone for Human Rights Watch, said that the rebels had been using brutal tactics for years but that they now appear to be more desperate because of the presence of the Nigerian soldiers. Nigerians make up the majority of the peacekeeping force sent by the Economic Community of West African States Monitoring Group, known as Ecomog.
The report, "Sowing Terror," was published today. Written by Mr. Campbell and Jane Lowicki, both of them consultants to the organization, it says children and women have also been singled out for sexual abuse. Accounts of gang rapes and kidnappings for purposes of sexual slavery are common.
The report includes accounts of abuses by civilian defense forces supporting President Kabbah, in particular a group known as the Kamajors, which it describes as "responsible for the majority of the most serious abuses committed by those fighting on behalf of the Kabbah Government since February 1998."
Human Rights Watch says child soldiers have been used by these forces, but the Kabbah Government has promised to end that practice.
The most brutal offenses seem to have been committed by the rebels, however, especially the Revolutionary United Front, the report says.
"Since 1991, when the R.U.F got going, in one of their first attacks they allegedly decapitated administrators, put their heads on sticks," Mr. Campbell said in an interview today. "In order to make statements of power, political statements, in 1996, they would mutilate people. They would write things like 'No election' or 'Don't vote' on peoples' backs -- they would burn it in.
"The idea of chopping someone's hands off allegedly came from rebels saying to people: We're going to cut your hands off so you can't vote."
"What's changed now," Mr. Campbell said, "is that the military balance has changed, with Ecomog there. Ecomog is really powerful. They control the urban centers and the rebels have become more desperate.
"Their new strategy to put themselves back on the political chessboard is to do these horrible things to civilians. That does two things: It makes the civilian population subservient, and it makes them somebody you have to deal with."
The report included transcripts of testimony from survivors of rebel attacks. One was from a farmer in Koidu, in the Kono district, identified as Sam R., whose home was attacked twice. This is his story:
"At about 4 A.M. I heard bombs and gunshots outside my house," he said of the first attack. "The rebels came and banged on the door. They said they would kill us all outside. My wife took five of the six children outside. I stayed inside with one.
"My wife threw herself on top of two of the children to protect them. They shot my wife, killed two of the children, shot my 7-year-old through the stomach and cut one on the buttocks. Two got away."
There was another attack in June. "They accused me of being a Kamajor," he said. "When they want to kill you, they accuse you of anything. There is no reason. I am a farmer. I don't vote. I have no money. They burned my house."

HEADLINE: Bad Debt Held by Japan's Banks Now Estimated Near $1 Trillion

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 29

 BODY:
American officials and private financial experts evaluating the depths of Japan's banking crisis have concluded that it is far worse than Tokyo has publicly acknowledged, with bad debts approaching $1 trillion, or nearly twice the official estimate.
American officials and outside experts caution that their estimates are extremely rough, in part because Japanese banks have been using accounting tricks to hide those debts that are not being repaid. 
 The higher figures greatly complicate the challenge facing Keizo Obuchi, the man set to become Japan's new Prime Minister on Thursday, these officials say. Mr. Obuchi has little experience in the financial sphere and has offered only the vaguest of plans about how he will deal with what appears to be the largest banking crisis in history.
Today, however, he persuaded an elder statesman, former Prime Minister Kiichi Miyazawa, to become Finance Minister -- placing the 78-year-old politician, a fixture in American-Japanese relations for four decades, at the center of the financial storm. Page A8.
The new estimates suggest that bailing out the Japanese banks, which include some of the world's largest, could cost Japanese taxpayers far more than the $214 billion that Tokyo has set aside. The Government has declared plans to establish a "bridge bank" that would assume bad assets from the commercial banks and sell them off, presumably for a fraction of their value. The weak lenders would be closed or merged. The cost to taxpayers, some officials believe, will need to double.
The Clinton Administration and Wall Street have long assumed that the Japanese were understating the problems they face. Officially Japan has said that its banks currently hold around $550 billion in bad debt, although in recent weeks that figure has crept up to closer to $700 billion, Japanese officials said. The far greater scale of the problem, and cost of fixing it, could make it harder for the new Japanese Government to act decisively.
The size of Japan's bad debts may well be the most important statistic in the Asian financial crisis. Since the crisis struck, Japanese banks have become so beleaguered that their lending at home and elsewhere in Asia has all but halted, and they are closing many of their offices throughout the region. This has contributed to a scarcity of credit that has worsened the recession -- in some places the depression -- that stretches from South Korea to Indonesia.
The United States is concerned for several reasons: exports to Japan have plummeted, and the Japanese banking crisis is hampering the effort to rescue other Asian nations which are heavily dependent on trade, investment and borrowing from Japan.
American officials say they have not conducted any formal study of Japan's banking troubles, and they almost never discuss it in public, for fear of scaring even more investors away from the Japanese markets. Some argue that the overall debt figure is both unknowable and less important than the cost of closing or merging some of Japan's largest banks. Because even bad loans have some value, most experts say that the cost of cleaning up the books will probably be only 60 to 70 percent of the bad loan portfolios.
The $1 trillion figure is being floated around during meetings at the White House, the Treasury and the Federal Reserve, and appears based on new estimates from Japan, unofficial estimates from investment bankers on both sides of the Pacific, and anecdotal evidence of how Japanese banks have been performing sleight-of-hand to avoid a true accounting.
For example, some banks are reported to be quietly lending even more money to apparently insolvent companies that have been long-time customers. The companies, in turn, are using the new loans to pay back interest on overdue loans that would otherwise be in default. As a result, the banks are able to report to authorities that their loans are being repaid -- even though the new loans they are providing only deepen the the problem.
"The numbers are hard to pin down because the banks go to such lengths to hide the bad news," said Richard Medley, managing partner of Medley Global Advisors, a private research firm in New York that often consults with top American officials about the Asian crisis. But referring to the savings-and-loan crisis in the United States of a decade ago, he said, "if you add up what the Japanese have admitted, and look at the usual margin of error in the American S. & L. crisis and similar crises, you get to a trillion very fast."
One senior Administration official, who declined to be identified, said "What's become clear is that Japan is going to need to spend tens of billions more in public money to get out of this mess, and they are going to have to take some banks out of their misery."
By comparison, the cost of resolving the bad debts generated in the American savings and loan crisis of a decade ago were roughly $160 billion, in an economy that in 1990 had a gross national product of $5.74 trillion. The cost amounted to about 2.7 percent of the economy's annual production.
If Japanese taxpayers spend upwards of $500 billion to rescue their banks -- and that may prove to be conservative -- it will exceed 12 percent of the country's G.N.P..
And that is only part of the problem. Most experts believe that losses in the life insurance industry could amount to another several hundred billion dollars. Like the banks, the insurance industry relied on a property market that turned out to be wildly overvalued.
"The concern is that if you are spending so much to clean up your banking system, it takes a real whack at the economy," said Bert Ely, a financial consultant who was deeply involved in evaluating the American savings and loan crisis.
Japan is hardly the only country in Asia mired in bad debt. In Indonesia, most local companies have simply stopped paying loans, and the banks are working out details of a delay in paying back overseas lenders. Many of the lenders are Japanese.
And American officials are increasingly focusing on banking troubles in China. Moody's Investors Service said today that it had placed its ratings of nine Chinese banks on review, to determine if it should downgrade its assessment of their credit worthiness.
Among the commercial Chinese banks under review are four of the largest state-owned institutions: the Agricultural Bank of China, the Bank of China, China Construction Bank, and Industrial and Commercial Bank of China.
A collapse in China could slow the nation's already-stumbling economy. And a slowdown would worsen unemployment and threaten many of the economic reforms that President Clinton celebrated on his trip to China a month ago.
But it is Japan's ability to deal with its huge banks that most worries American officials. Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin sounded that note anew today in an interview on CBS, urging the new Japanese Government to move quickly on a range of problems, including stimulating the economy and deregulating protected corners of the economy.
No one knows exactly how big the problem is because Japan's audits of its banks have been almost comically insufficient. American officials frequently note that Japan has about 400 bank inspectors; the United States has about 7,000.
What the markets are watching now is how Mr. Obuchi, and the newly-appointed Mr. Miyazawa, go about the painful process of dealing with the banks -- which in the short term may result in more corporate bankruptcies and higher unemployment. In trading early on Thursday, the markets did not seem confident of strong action. The yen weakened against the dollar, although stock prices edged higher in Tokyo.
The Government's tentative steps so far have failed. Japan closed one large insolvent bank, in the northern island of Hokkaido, late last year. The local economy immediately fell deeper into recession, and Japanese officials argue that the experience showed that closing banks could do more damage than merging them or trying to keep some open.
Moreover, there is rising concern that the Government's reluctant decision to use taxpayer money to bail out the banks is going astray. Some of the first money was used to keep insolvent banks afloat, simply prolonging their bad-loan problems.
Japanese bankers say that other banks decided not to sell off bad loans because they thought they would get a better deal selling them to the Government than selling them on the open market.
"All this did was make the problem worse," a senior Japanese banker said on a recent visit to the United States.

HEADLINE: AUTOS ON FRIDAY/Technology;
Is Toyota's Hybrid for the U.S. Road?

BYLINE: By ANDREW POLLACK 

DATELINE: LOS ANGELES

 BODY:
THE Toyota Prius, the world's first mass-produced hybrid vehicle, has been a success in Japan since it went on sale last December. In about two years, Americans will have a chance to sample the car for themselves.
The Prius (pronounced PREE-us) combines a gasoline engine with an electric motor, thereby promising pollution levels nearly as low as those of fully electric vehicles but with the everyday practicality of a conventional gasoline-powered automobile. For that reason, many engineers are more excited about the potential of hybrid vehicles than they are about electric cars. 
 In the eight months the Prius has been on the market in Japan, more than 7,700 have been sold. That certainly qualifies as a success, at least as measured against the modest expectations for any alternative vehicle.
Sales are strong partly because Toyota is selling the vehicle for 2.15 million yen, about $15,500 -- well below the car's development and production costs and only about $1,500 more than the similarly sized Corolla.
Toyota says the Prius will reach the American market late in 2000. The price in the United States and other details of the American model have not been determined, but the challenge here will be greater than in Japan. Gasoline prices in the United States are a fraction of Japanese prices. Moreover, Toyota must substantially tweak the design if the Prius is to deliver its promised fuel economy and low emissions under American driving conditions.
In Japan's standard fuel-economy test, the Prius gets a remarkable 66 miles a gallon, about double the fuel efficiency of a similar gasoline car. It also has half the gasoline car's carbon dioxide emissions and only 10 percent as much output of carbon monoxide, nitrogen oxides and hydrocarbons.
Toyota executives said at a recent news conference here that their goal was for the American version to achieve 60 m.p.h. and to qualify for California's new proposed category of "super ultra-low-emission" vehicle.
Since the Prius uses gasoline, it cannot be a zero-emission vehicle. But under a proposal before the California Air Resources Board, such "super ultra low" vehicles would be granted some credit toward meeting the future requirement that 10 percent of the vehicles sold by major manufacturers meet zero-emission standards.
While Toyota might at first concentrate in California and some other environmentally conscious markets, it eventually hopes to sell nationwide and does not want the car to be typecast as a fringe vehicle.
Hiroshi Okuda, the Toyota's president, recently announced in Japan that the company plans to export 20,000 Priuses a year starting in 2000, many of them no doubt bound for the United States. By contrast, in the last year only a few hundred electric vehicles have been sold in the United States by all manufacturers combined.
Fully electric vehicles are expensive and can only be driven about 100 miles or so before the batteries must be recharged, a process that takes several hours. The Prius, by contrast, can be filled up at a gasoline station like any other car. It uses power from the gasoline engine to recharge the batteries as the car is driven, so it is never necessary to go to a recharging station.
Because the batteries and gasoline help each other, the Prius can get by with smaller versions of each, cutting costs and weight. The batteries, for instance, weigh only about one-fifteenth as much as those in a fully electric vehicle. And the gasoline engine has a capacity of only 1.5 liters.
Under complex computer control, the Prius switches from battery power to gasoline power, or a combination of the two, automatically and seamlessly. The driver just steps on the fuel pedal and is oblivious to what is powering the car.
When the car is idle or starting up at slow speed, it is powered by the batteries alone. Therefore, there is no pollution or gasoline wasted when the car is, say, mired in traffic.
At moderate speeds, the car is powered by the gasoline engine. Some of the engine power that is not used to drive the vehicle is diverted to a generator to make electricity used to recharge the battery.
At very high speeds or during full-throttle acceleration, the battery and the gasoline engine work together.
The small gasoline engine alone, for instance, can make the car go as fast as 80 m.p.h. But with the batteries kicking in, the speed can hit 110. Once the batteries run down, the speed would drop back below 80, even though the driver maintains constant pressure on the accelerator.
This situation would not be faced by most drivers. At lower speeds, the Japanese version of the Prius performed just like a regular car. In a short test drive, it was virtually impossible to tell when the car shifted from battery power to gasoline and back.
The squat four-door sedan, designed at Toyota's studio in Newport Beach, Calif., can seat five, four quite comfortably, with enough room in the back seat for tall people. Its acceleration is somewhat sluggish, but that is not unusual for a car of its size.
The Prius's dashboard contains a small video screen that displays a schematic diagram of the car's inner workings, using moving arrows to show how the energy is flowing at any given instant, whether from the battery or the gasoline engine. This quickly becomes distracting to the driver. Most drivers probably wouldn't care what is happening under the hood anyway, since it has no bearing on how they drive.
But what was most revealing was when a button labeled "fuel economy" was pressed. The display changed to a bar graph showing the car's fuel economy over the last half-hour, broken up into five-minute intervals.
What's this? In one 20-minute test drive mainly on city streets with one two-mile stretch of Los Angeles freeway, the Prius achieved only about 24 m.p.g., far less than the 66 m.p.g. advertised and not even as good as many small all-gasoline cars.
Admittedly, test drivers accelerate more quickly than an average motorist would, but that is not the whole story. The fuel economy and low emissions of a hybrid come mainly from using batteries instead of gasoline. But that happens mainly at speeds up to about 10 m.p.h. At higher speeds, when the car relies mainly on gasoline, the Prius is not much more fuel-efficient than other cars.
In the Japanese mileage test, which simulates congested city driving, the vehicle travels at an average speed of about 14 m.p.h., according to Toshihiro Oi, the chief engineer of the Prius. Even near downtown Los Angeles, we were going much faster than that. Moreover, we had the air-conditioner running, meaning the gasoline engine was working while the car was stopped.
Toyota officials acknowledge that they have work to do to deliver the promise of the Prius under American driving conditions. Right now, for instance, the Japanese version of the car would qualify only as a "low-emission vehicle" in California, two ranks below the rating Toyota hopes to obtain with the American version.
For now, Toyota has managed to design an alternative vehicle that elegantly succeeds in functioning like a gasoline-powered car -- perhaps too much so.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Toyota must do more than move the Prius's steering wheel to appeal to U.S. motorists. Prius's technology combines battery power and a gasoline engine to reduce emissions.

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Daimler's Profit Is Up Sharply; F.T.C. Clears Deal for Chrysler

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: STUTTGART, Germany, July 30

 BODY:
Daimler-Benz A.G. said today that its profit more than doubled in the first half of 1998, lifted by higher revenue in all divisions and by asset sales.
The company, whose purchase of the Chrysler Corporation was approved in Washington today by the Federal Trade Commission, benefited from faster economic growth in Europe and the United States and a reorganization that began three years ago. Daimler lowered production costs and cut the time it takes to bring out new products, including its Mercedes A-class compact car, which went on sale in February. 
 "Excellent," said Georg Sturzer, an analyst at Bayerische Vereinsbank in Munich, about the results. He was particularly impressed by the recovery of "Daimler's problem child -- aerospace."
The company, which is Europe's largest manufacturer, said that profit rose to a record 2.01 billion marks, or $1.1 billion, from 992 million marks in the first half a year earlier. The results exceeded forecasts.
Daimler's shares closed little changed in Frankfurt today, down 10 pfennigs, at 184.20 marks, or roughly $104. On the New York Stock Exchange, Daimler's American depository receipts, each of which represents one common share, rose $1.875, to $105.
Revenue in the first half increased 21 percent, to 67.7 billion marks from 55.9 billion marks. Daimler said revenue for the full year was likely to be more than 140 billion marks, compared with 124 billion marks in all of 1997.
At Daimler-Benz Aerospace A.G., which last year reported its first profit since 1991, operating earnings more than quadrupled in the half. The military unit benefited from a joint European jet fighter project of Germany, Britain, Spain and Italy, and from higher orders by the European civil aircraft consortium Airbus Industrie. Daimler's truck unit returned to profitability.
Operating profit at the Mercedes-Benz passenger car subsidiary, Daimler's largest unit, increased 34 percent, to two billion marks.
Shareholders of Chrysler and Daimler-Benz are to decide on the companies' merger at meetings to be held here and in Wilmington, Del., on Sept. 18.

HEADLINE: New-Home Sales At Record Pace

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 30

 BODY:
Sales of new homes jumped to a record pace in June, the Government said today.
New-home sales climbed 3.8 percent, to a 935,000-unit annual rate, the fastest pace since record-keeping began in 1963, the Commerce Department said. The revised annual rate of 901,000 homes reported for May was the previous record. 
 Wall Street analysts had forecast a moderate slowdown in June sales, to a rate of 888,000. Analysts said all indicators were positive for the economy's housing sector heading into the second half of the year.
"It should remain strong as long as interest rates remain low, the labor market remains tight, the stock market remains near its highs and consumer confidence remains high," said Marilyn Schaja, an economist at the Donaldson, Lufkin and Jenrette Securities Corporation in New York.
June sales ran a robust 15.4 percent higher than a year earlier, when home sales were at a rate of 810,000 a year.

SECTION: Section D; Page 1; Column 5; Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 860 words

HEADLINE: Labor Costs Rose Slightly In the Spring

BYLINE: By SYLVIA NASAR 

 BODY:
Despite signs that economic growth is slowing sharply, wages and benefits are climbing a bit faster than a year ago.
The Labor Department's employment cost index -- a broad gauge of compensation trends closely watched by the Federal Reserve chairman, Alan Greenspan, and other policy makers -- rose nine-tenths of 1 percent this spring, slightly more than the seven-tenths of 1 percent increase in the winter. For the 12 months ended in June, pay for all civilian workers, including government workers, rose 3.5 percent, up from 2.8 percent in the previous year. 
 The pickup in labor costs reflects one of the tightest peacetime labor markets in memory. Even as the Asian crisis, the recently settled General Motors strike and a lull in inventory additions have combined to drag down growth in recent months, unemployment is well below 5 percent, as it has been for a year, hiring plans have remained robust, and thousands of jobs in industries from high technology to hotels have gone begging.
The acceleration in wage inflation is of the creeping, not galloping, variety: pay and benefits are rising just one percentage point faster than at the low-point for such cost increases nearly three years ago.
As such, the latest reading is not likely to provoke Mr. Greenspan, who only a week ago reiterated how closely he watches the index, to raise interest rates.
"We haven't moved into new territory," said Nicholas S. Perna, chief economist at the Fleet Financial Group in Boston. "This report reduces the odds that the Fed will tighten."
Ever since unemployment first slipped below the level that most economists regard as consistent with steady inflation, the Federal Reserve has been on the lookout for any hint that wage pressures are getting out of hand. But now that the economy seems to be slowing on its own, the modest pickup in pay may well show up in slimmer profit margins rather than in higher prices for consumers. Investors, in any case, seemed to take that view. Bond prices rose after the report's release, and the stock market closed higher ahead of today's much anticipated report on economic growth for the second quarter.
Workers are getting a better deal than the raw numbers imply. Thanks to a minuscule 1.6 percent rise in the cost of living over the last year, relatively restrained nominal pay increases are being translated into relatively robust real gains. Real pay is up nearly 2 percent from a year ago.
If anything, the employment cost index, though widely regarded as the broadest and most reliable gauge of employers' labor costs, may understate just how well workers are doing. It does not include one-time payments like signing bonuses and bounties, which businesses are increasingly relying on to fill vacancies without raising their entire pay structure. It also does not take account of another widespread phenomenon: promotions that are given purely to raise a worker's pay or promotions that workers give themselves by switching to better-paying jobs. One sign that these factors may add up is that another Department of Labor measure, compensation per hour, has risen about a percentage point more in the last year than the employment cost index. "We're seeing some dramatic wage inflation" in some job categories, says Maury Hanigan, a human resources consultant in New York.
Wages and salaries grew nine-tenths of 1 percent, not much more than the eight-tenths of 1 percent growth in the winter. But benefit costs jumped sharply to eight-tenths of 1 percent, twice as fast as they had risen in the previous quarter. Part of the pickup in benefit costs is probably temporary; it reflects commissions and bonuses being paid on Wall Street and elsewhere in the financial industry, where compensation has risen by 7 percent from a year ago. But at least half of the benefits, including Social Security payments and vacation pay, are tied directly to wages. And one-fifth consist of health benefits, which are once again marching higher. "Health insurance costs are picking up for the first time in three and a half years," said Wayne M. Shelly, an economist at the Bureau of Labor Statistics, which issued the report.
Wage pressures may not fade. Some of the forces holding down living costs are clearly temporary. As Asia struggles back, oil prices are apt to firm. And the strong dollar, which has made many imports a bargain, could weaken.
More important, even if the economy settles down to a growth rate of 2 percent or 2.5 percent over the next year or so -- as Mr. Greenspan and other forecasters expect -- it will not be slow enough to loosen up the labor market much. "The unemployment rate stays exactly where it is," Mr. Perna predicted. "The overall degree of tightness stays exactly where it is."
Two other reports made public yesterday suggest that this is the case right now. The Conference Board reported that its help-wanted advertising index rose to 91 in June from 90 in May and 88 a year ago. And new claims for unemployment insurance dropped to 304,000 last week from 317,000 the week before, the Labor Department said. That's the lowest level since early April.

LENGTH: 545 words

HEADLINE: Hercules to Buy BetzDearborn In $2.4 Billion Chemical Deal

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
Hercules Inc. agreed yesterday to buy BetzDearborn Inc. for $2.4 billion in cash and $700 million in assumed debt, creating one of the world's biggest makers of chemicals used in paper-making, water-treatment and other industrial processes.
The offer of $72 a share is nearly double BetzDearborn's closing price of $35.875 on Wednesday. Shares of BetzDearborn jumped $31.8125 yesterday, or 88 percent, to $67.6875. Hercules fell $1.5625, to $35.50. 
 Hercules, a specialty chemicals maker based in Wilmington, Del., had sales of $1.87 billion last year. BetzDearborn, based in Trevose, Pa., is the No. 2 producer of water-treatment and industrial-process chemicals in the United States, with $1.29 billion in 1997 sales.
The purchase allows Hercules, itself the subject of takeover speculation after it lost a bid for a European chemical company, to resume its worldwide expansion amid industry consolidation. It also gives Hercules a complementary range of paper-processing and water-treatment chemicals that it did not have.
"There's no question Hercules didn't get a bargain, but it's a perfect strategic fit,' said David Manlowe, an analyst at BT Alex. Brown Inc. "It gives them soup-to-nuts coverage on the paper industry and makes them the biggest player in that segment."
While the premium for BetzDearborn shares looks big, the price is only slightly higher than its 52-week high in September.
Chemicals made by Hercules are used primarily to improve strength and other qualities in paper. BetzDearborn's are used to protect equipment and to increase efficiency in making paper.
In addition to paper chemicals, Hercules also makes additives to thicken food, fibers used in diapers and resins used in rubber. BetzDearborn also makes chemicals used in the automotive, food processing, oil refining and other industries.
The companies expect to cut costs by about $100 million because of the combination, they said in a joint statement. The transaction is expected to close in the fourth quarter and is subject to approval by BetzDearborn shareholders.
R. Keith Elliott, the chairman and chief executive of Hercules, and William Cook, the chief executive of BetzDearborn, will be co-chief executives of the company. Mr. Elliott will be chairman and Mr. Cook will be vice chairman.

Olin to Spin Off Unit
 (By Bloomberg News)
 The Olin Corporation said yesterday that it would spin off its specialty chemical business to shield the company from price swings that dragged down earnings in its basic chemicals and materials divisions.
Shareholders will get new stock in the company, which accounted for 40 percent of Olin's $2.4 billion in sales last year. The unit makes chemicals used in dandruff shampoo, water-treatment systems, semiconductor production, oil refineries and coatings.
Olin, based in Norwalk, Conn., also warned that 1998 per-share profit would be "in the $3 range," less than the $3.24 average estimate reported by the First Call Corporation, which tracks company earnings. Second-quarter sales were hurt by falling metal prices and the Asian economic slump's effect on the semiconductor and chemical industries.
Shares of Olin rose $2.375, to $39.0625.

HEADLINE: Reforming Welfare With Education

 BODY:
An important amendment approved by the Senate to the bill reauthorizing the Higher Education Act would give states the option of allowing welfare recipients to enroll in postsecondary education or vocational training for two years without losing their welfare benefits. The provision, sponsored by Senator Paul Wellstone of Minnesota, should be retained in the final bill.
Allowing welfare recipients the opportunity to gain real skills is the most promising approach to getting families off welfare. Recipients who have more education are more likely to find higher-paying jobs that offer economic self-sufficiency. But current law allows education or vocational training to count as a work activity for only 12 months, after which the student must work 20 hours a week to continue getting benefits. For many recipients, that requirement means dropping out of school. 
 The 12-month limit is particularly constraining for people who need English-language classes in addition to vocational courses. Even among English-speakers the need for more education is substantial. Some 40 percent of all recipients lack a high school diploma. More than 60 percent of those who have been on welfare for five years or more have no diploma. The education gap may also be contributing to racial shifts in the welfare population. As recently reported in The Times, blacks and Hispanics are less likely than whites to have a high school diploma and the skills to find a job. That fact is one reason minorities are having a harder time leaving welfare than whites.
The welfare law is too tilted toward short-term work activity, allowing little attention to skills development. The current law also sets a cap on the percentage of the welfare population that can be enrolled in educational or vocational training at any one time. By 2000, all teen-age parents pursuing high school diplomas would be counted under the educational cap, thus reducing the number of adults who can enroll in training and still receive benefits.
The Wellstone amendment would prevent teen-age parents from being counted in the educational cap. Republicans are resisting these sensible changes, fearing they amount to backsliding on welfare reform. On the contrary, letting states encourage education is the best investment the nation can make toward a long-term welfare solution.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Editorial

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS;
Dow Ends Two Days of Losses as Buyers Put Jitters Aside

BYLINE: By DAVID BARBOZA 

 BODY:
Stock prices rose sharply yesterday, ending a two-day losing streak and pushing the Dow Jones industrial average back above 9,000.
The gains came with heavy trading volume and reversed a volatile summer slump that in the last weeks had seen most of the major indexes fall more than 4 percent from all-time highs set earlier this month. 
 Yesterday, the Dow rose 111.99 points, or 1.26 percent, to 9,026.95, leaving the blue-chip barometer about 300 points below its all-time high of 9,337.97, which was set on July 17. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index also gained sharply, climbing 17.74 points, or 1.58 percent, to 1,142.95.
Although investors continue to be jittery about signs that profit growth is slowing, Wall Street analysts said prices had been beaten down too sharply in the last few weeks and that share prices were ripe for a recovery yesterday.
"It's just a rubber-band situation," said Larry Wachtel, a market strategist at Prudential Securities. "We took the Dow down about 500 points. But this is the biggest and best bull market in history. It doesn't just go away on a random day in July."
Mr. Wachtel and other analysts said that although earnings had been less than spectacular, there had been some bright spots. Yesterday, for example, Clorox, one of the largest consumer products companies, said its fiscal fourth-quarter profit soared 32 percent, well above analysts' expectations.
Still, profit growth in the second quarter has been anemic. With reports in from about 80 percent of the companies that make up the S.& P. 500, operating profits have risen just 1.7 percent from the period a year earlier. In the first quarter, profit growth was equally meager, at 1.3 percent, according to I.B.E.S. International, which tracks corporate earnings.
The result has been the weakest profit growth in the first half since 1991. Expectations for the third quarter are also being trimmed, and that has many investors worried.
The bright spot, however, is a healthy economy with low interest rates and strong retail sales, which are helping offset the sharp economic downturn in Asia. Many investors are also betting that profit growth will recover later this year or next year.
Abby Joseph Cohen, the widely followed market strategist at Goldman, Sachs & Company, said in her weekly report this week that the most serious concerns revolve around Asia, but that she expected the economies in that region to stabilize and to see United States corporate profits reaccelerate in 1999.
Indeed, Ms. Cohen views the current sluggishness in the United States stock market as part of a "choppy trading range" that has persisted since April. As for second-quarter earnings, she said, "So far, so good," noting that most companies have reported results that exceed consensus estimates.
Yesterday, some of the biggest gainers were technology stocks, which have been clobbered in recent days, following sharp gains in the early part of the summer. The Nasdaq composite index, which is heavy with high-tech stocks, soared 38.09 points, gaining more than 2 percent yesterday, to 1,919.58. Microsoft, Intel, Dell Computer and Cisco Systems all rose sharply.
Frederick B. Taylor, the chief investment strategist at the United States Trust Company, said that investors had soured on many stocks in recent weeks, and that it was simply time for them to start buying again.
"Things had become so negative," he said. "And when there's a consensus on the negative side, people start moving to buy again. That's how the market works technically."
 

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
Strong Dollar Lifts Bonds
 (By Bridge News)
Treasury prices ended higher yesterday, as a strong dollar helped offset troubling economic news.
Traders said the gains showed a recent selloff had pushed prices low enough to attract investors again.
The price of the 30-year Treasury rose 22/32, to 105 24/32. The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, fell to 5.72 percent, from 5.76 on Wednesday.
Treasury prices followed the dollar higher after Japan's new Finance Minister, Kiichi Miyazawa, suggested that he was not very concerned about the level of the yen. In New York, the dollar rose to 143.66 yen from 142.35 yen yesterday.
Bond prices fell after the Labor Department reported that its employment cost index rose nine-tenths of 1 percent this spring. But prices soon recovered as buyers jumped in.

HEADLINE: TESTING OF A PRESIDENT: THE PRESIDENT;
Friendly Crowd Greets Clinton in South

BYLINE: By JOHN H. CUSHMAN Jr. 

DATELINE: RALEIGH, N.C., July 30

 BODY:
President Clinton appeared today before friendly crowds in North Carolina, never mentioning the sex and perjury inquiries as he pressed his environmental programs and helped raise money for John Edwards, a Democratic candidate for the Senate.
In Boggs, along the banks of the New River, Mr. Clinton and Vice President Al Gore joined local politicians to declare the stream an American Heritage River, a move that encourages Federal and local agencies to preserve the river's environment and stimulate economic activities in its watershed. 
 Several thousand North Carolinians cheered enthusiastically, and Mr. Clinton's luck was good enough that a drenching rain held off until moments after his speech ended.
"We support you, Mr. President!" one woman hollered from the crowd, and he beamed as he gestured at her and said, "Thank you, ma'am."
The Administration has kept Mr. Clinton on a schedule of public events, like today's, as a grand jury in Washington prepares to hear testimony from Monica S. Lewinsky, perhaps as early as next week. In an immunity deal with prosecutors, Ms. Lewinsky, the former White House intern, has said that she had a sexual relationship with the President and that they discussed ways to hide it. Mr. Clinton has denied any such relationship.
As he usually does at appearances with an environmental theme, Mr. Clinton said the Republican-controlled Congress was loading spending bills with provisions that he said would roll back environmental protections.
He called on Congress to release about $700 million that has already been set aside for buying conservation lands and to finance clean water programs in his budget request for the fiscal year 1999, which begins on Oct. 1.
The American Heritage Rivers program was established by a White House executive order last year, and communities all over the country have competed for the designation. Among the 14 waterways that were selected this week to receive the special status were the Hudson River, the Potomac River, the Rio Grande and the Connecticut River.
The New River, where Mr. Clinton spoke, is poorly named: geologists say it has been here for 350 million years, even before the Appalachian Mountains rose. Two decades ago, conservationists fought off efforts to dam a big stretch of the river.
