HEADLINE: A Union Vote Sows Bitterness in California Strawberry Fields

BYLINE: By DON TERRY 

DATELINE: WATSONVILLE, Calif., July 30

 BODY:
Tension and fear hover over the strawberry fields of the Coastal Berry Company here like the morning fog.
In the last few weeks, the lush fields outside of this small city 70 miles south of San Francisco have become a battleground in an increasingly nasty and nationally significant two-and-a-half-year struggle by the United Farm Workers to unionize the men and women who pick the sweet red berries, often for short pay and long hours: Their backs bent, their heads down, their hopes low. 
 Since July 1, there have been threats, arrests, beatings of U.F.W. supporters, a disputed union election that a rival group won, a one-day strike to protest the election and a hearing at the State Capitol on Tuesday into the entire bitter harvest.
"I was very disturbed about what I heard," said State Senator Hilda L. Solis, a Los Angeles Democrat who was co-chairwoman of the hearing. "There's violence in the fields, intimidation. A union vote should never have been taken in that kind of environment."
And today, the U.F.W. said it would file legal papers with the California Agricultural Labor Relations Board, seeking to throw out the results of the union vote at Coastal Berry, the nation's largest strawberry grower.
The U.F.W. maintains that the election on July 23 was unfair and was held in a climate of violence and fear; it refused to participate in the vote. The union also contends that the group that won the election, the Coastal Berry Farm Workers Committee, is a "sham union," a front for the company and the powerful Western Grower's Association, a farming industry organization in Irvine, Calif., which has long opposed the union's efforts.
"The stakes are so high that all of agri-business has come together to stop us," said Dolores Huerta, the co-founder of the U.F.W. There are 20,000 strawberry pickers in California, where 80 percent of the nation's strawberries are grown.
Both the company and the association deny that they had anything to do with the election or its results.
"Quite simply," said Doug Kerr, general counsel for the association, "the U.F.W. is lashing out at anyone they can over their frustration at being unable to organize the Coastal Berry employees."
The U.F.W. tried to stop the election, but the labor board allowed the vote to go forward.
And so, the Coastal Berry Farm Workers Committee, a group that did not even exist a few weeks ago, won the right to represent the company's more than 900 field workers with 54 percent of the vote, even though the committee had no agenda, no list of demands on behalf of the workers and no organizing strategy beyond defeating the U.F.W.
Even the Coastal Berry president, David Smith, conceded that the workers committee had a limited purpose.
"They don't have structure," Mr. Smith said of the new union. "They don't have management. They don't want to collect dues."
One leader of the group, Jose G. Fernandez, a 22-year-old truck driver, was arrested and charged with assaulting a police officer who interceded in a confrontation between members of the workers committee and union supporters in the fields on July 1.
"There's been a lot of tension for the last couple of years," the president of the U.F.W., Arturo S. Rodriguez, said. "But we never expected they would just openly rush a field and beat up workers loyal to the union."
Three workers were sent to the hospital, where they were treated and released, but Mr. Smith said the violence was relatively minor: rocks, fists and boxes of strawberries were thrown around and in one case into a frightened woman's face.
But when the Agricultural Labor Relations Board tried to investigate the incident before the union election, Coastal Berry refused to cooperate, citing a fear of more violence, the labor board's general counsel, Paul Richardson, said on Tuesday at a joint hearing of the state's Senate Committee on Industrial Relations and the Assembly Committee on Labor and Employment.
Mr. Richardson said the board wanted to investigate the incident to determine whether the election should go forward, but without the company's cooperation, it was decided to proceed with the voting, partly to avoid any more trouble in the fields.
"The position of the A.L.R.B.," Mr. Richardson said, "is to vote now, litigate later."
That statement and several others from labor board officials seemed to inflame State Assemblyman Richard E. Floyd, a Democrat from Wilmington. Mr. Floyd, the co-chairman of the hearing, said the board was "absolutely condoning" the violence when it refused to block the election.
Neither the growers association, the Coastal Berry Company or its new union appeared at the hearing, although each group was invited.
During an interview, Mr. Smith said that Coastal Berry was cooperating with labor board investigators and that he did not care what union, if any, represented his workers.
"We would negotiate with the U.F.W.," he said. "They have done some good things for their workers."
Mr. Smith said the union was picking on the wrong company, because Coastal Berry provided some of the highest wages and best benefits in the $600-million-a-year strawberry industry, which is known for its low wages and poor benefits. Mr. Smith said workers earned a base pay of $6.50 an hour, which can increase to $10 or $11 in the peak picking season, depending on how much a picker picks.
The union said that during the peak season the base pay fell and the amount of money the pickers earned per box of strawberries rose to 75 cents a tray from about 35 cents a tray. An average worker picks four to six trays an hour. "It's back-breaking work," said Lupe Lara, a picker for 15 years, who said he was dismissed last Monday after participating in a one-day strike to protest the election. "But I want my job back. I have no choice. I have six children."
Mr. Rodriquez, the U.F.W. president, and several workers, agreed that conditions in the fields had "improved dramatically." But they credit the union's efforts to organize, not the policies of Coastal Berry.
Before the union campaign began, said Jesus Anaya, 46, a longtime picker, conditions were poor. "Now," Mr. Anaya said, "the company is giving us health insurance because of the union."
Still, after nearly three years of effort and considerable help from the A.F.L.-C.I.O., the U.F.W. has not been able to gain a solid foothold in the strawberry fields of Watsonville.
Mr. Fernandez said that at first, the goal of his group was to keep the U.F.W. and any other unions out of the fields. It was only after it was determined to be more effective if the group represented itself as a union that the workers' committee was formed, he said.
Mr. Fernandez said the U.F.W. had failed because "we got tired of them harassing us to join the union at our homes, after work, before work, at the park, on the street."
As for his new union, Mr. Fernandez said he was not sure what it would ask for at the bargaining table, if the committee ever gets that far. "Maybe we'll try to get some bonuses," he said. "We don't promise nothing."
But the struggle for the hearts and minds of the strawberry pickers is far from over. Ms. Solis said she planned to hold another hearing about the election and intended to review the labor board's performance.
"This is the first time in 14 years we're actually having an oversight hearing of the A.L.R.B.," the Senator said. "It won't be the last."

LENGTH: 1007 words

HEADLINE: Residential Real Estate;
Giving New Rental Life To Rundown Buildings

BYLINE: By RACHELLE GARBARINE 

 BODY:
A dozen city-owned buildings in East Harlem that not long ago were abandoned or partly occupied and in various stages of disrepair are now in the hands of a private development group that is bringing them back to life as mixed-income rental housing.
When completed next year, the $9.5 million renovation project will offer 133 newly restored apartments within the buildings, which are grouped in clusters stretching from 125th to 131st Street and from Fifth to Madison Avenue. The bulk of the apartments will be rented either to existing tenants or to low-income families. 
 The remaining apartments will be rented at market rates for the area "to provide enough revenue to run the buildings on a just-above-break-even basis," said Terrence Moan, who, with Nina DeMartini-Day and Francis Syn-Moie, is one of the partners at NTF Realty Services, the developer.
Mr. Moan served as the city's Real Estate Commissioner in the administration of Edward I. Koch, and Ms. DeMartini-Day was a deputy commissioner. Mr. Syn-Moie is the president of New York Residential Works, a management company.
The project is part of New York City's Neighborhood Entrepreneur Program, which awards buildings that are close to one another to entrepreneurs and helps them secure public and private financing to do the renovation.
The program is administered by the city's Department of Housing Preservation and Development and the nonprofit New York City Housing Partnership, which serves as an intermediary between the city and private developers.
Mr. Moan said NTF became involved with the program because "it fits our philosophy of redeveloping housing, whenever possible, to keep unit density high and costs low."
He said the program also allowed "us to move into a management position." Over the last decade, Mr. Moan and Ms. DeMartini-Day rehabilitated 500 units in East Harlem for nonprofit groups as DDM Development Services. They formed NTF with Mr. Syn-Moie, who brings 12 years of management experience to the team.
NTF paid the city $1 apiece for each of the 12 East Harlem buildings, taking over their management and agreeing to relocate the 49 existing tenants, who are guaranteed a renovated apartment when the rebuilding process is completed. The work includes, if necessary, repairing and cleaning the facades and installing new buildingwide mechanical systems, as well as upgrading the bathrooms and kitchens in the apartments.
The studio to four-bedroom apartments will range in size from 450 to 1,400 square feet.
Rents for existing tenants, some of whom are on public assistance, are capped at 30 percent of income. Of the 84 vacant units, 45 are to be rented to families making less than 60 percent of the median income in the city; for a family of four, this figure is $30,120 a year. Rents for those units are to be $375 to $525 a month.
The remaining 39 apartments are to be rented to families earning up to 165 percent of the median -- a ceiling of $85,000 a year for a family of four. Their rents of $550 to $1,000 a month would be on a par with the market rate rents in the area, Mr. Moan noted.
In the year since NTF took over as landlord, 55 units in 4 buildings have been renovated. Tenants, many of them existing residents, began settling into their new apartments earlier this month.
Among them is Taneathia Perry, who with her three young children moved into a two-bedroom unit at the Marcella, a century-old, L-shaped complex at the corner of Fifth Avenue and 125th Street that contains 33 apartments over five ground-level retail stores.
"It is clean, safe and convenient, and my kids love it," Miss Perry said, noting that her old apartment on 128th Street "needed a lot of work." The floors were crooked, the windows were a problem and the ceiling in her bedroom leaked when it rained, she said, adding that "I couldn't wait to get out."
Miss Perry's old apartment is in one of the remaining eight buildings -- together totaling 78 units -- that NTF will renovate over the next year.
When the renovations are finished, the company will receive a one-time development fee of $5,477 a unit -- paid over 10 years from the project budget -- and take over ownership of the buildings, which it will run for 30 years with the same mix of low- and moderate-income tenants. Once the entire cluster is completed, the company can also receive up to $55,000 annually if it runs the buildings according to an agreed-upon budget, said Mr. Moan.
Since the Entrepreneur Program began in 1994, 237 buildings with a total of 2,359 apartments have been or will soon be renovated and a similar number of units are expected to be renovated over the next two years, according to the New York City Housing Partnership. Most of them are in neighborhoods throughout Brooklyn, the Bronx and Manhattan, especially Harlem, which has a "disproportionate share of city-owned buildings," said Veronica M. White, president of the partnership.
"The goal," she said, "is to return the buildings to private hands and in the process preserve existing housing units and create new ones."
For the projects to work economically and to receive the necessary Federal funds, Ms. White said, roughly three-quarters of the apartments are rented to low-income families, and the balance are available at market rates. Having the market-rate units, she said, also serves to bring middle-income tenants into an area.
Buildings chosen for the program often are near new housing developed by nonprofit groups, including the partnership. The result, Ms. White said, is that the rebuilding process occurs "neighborhood by neighborhood, rather than one building at a time."
Now that NTF has completed the renovation of 4 of its 12 buildings, it will take over ownership of those buildings within the next month. "The benefit," Mr. Moan said, "comes not in a quick cash return but rather in the long-term ownership of the buildings, which will appreciate in value if run properly and are in an area like East Harlem, which is improving."

HEADLINE: Something Old, Something New in Japan Chief's Cabinet

BYLINE: By STEPHANIE STROM 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Friday, July 31

 BODY:
Keizo Obuchi took office on Thursday as Prime Minister and named a Cabinet which, while it largely reflects his loyalty to factions of the governing Liberal Democratic Party that insured his election, also made a nod to younger members pushing for change.
Mr. Obuchi became Prime Minister because his party colleagues defied the wishes of their constituents, who overwhelmingly preferred his rivals and who have already voiced their displeasure with the ruling party by voting it out of power in the upper house. 
 Not only will he have to satisfy voters at home, he will have to prove to a world clamoring for action to right the Japanese economy that he can abandon the cautious consensus-building that brought him to power and make bold, even daring moves.
Mr. Obuchi was not the choice of the upper house, which chose Naoto Kan, head of the largest opposition party, the Democrats, as prime minister.
But in such a split the candidate chosen by the more powerful lower house gets the job. The division will mean, however, that Mr. Obuchi will be working overtime to curry the favor of Japan's splintered opposition parties if he wants to push through controversial legislation.
The Cabinet will have to address major issues including rising unemployment, a banking crisis and global fears of a surge in Japanese exports helped by a weak yen.
He turned responsibility for the powerful Ministry of Posts and Telecommunications, which controls a big chunk of Japan's vast pool of private savings, over to a woman, Seiko Noda, and appointed a highly regarded academic, Akito Arima, as Education Minister.
And he entrusted Japan's sick economy and fragile financial system to Kiichi Miyazawa, a former Prime Minister and party veteran who has a long record negotiating international economic circles.
While Mr. Obuchi's Cabinet reflected the factional power within the governing party, commentators suggested that in some cases he had made his own selections from among faction members rather than allowing the faction leaders to choose.
The financial markets withheld judgment of the new Cabinet, which was not officially announced until after many Asian markets closed, although the new Cabinet members' names were circulating this morning. The yen held steady against the dollar in Tokyo but fell in New York on Thursday after Mr. Miyazawa indicated he might not move to prop up the currency or the stock market as past governments have done.
"Putting it very simply, whether it is the yen or stocks, we should rely on the markets to move on their own," he said. The Nikkei basically shrugged its shoulders, rising 43.51 points to 16,201.60.
Mr. Miyazawa, 78, has been involved in crafting policies to deal with Japan's banking crisis, and his global reputation for financial savvy goes some way toward dispelling doubts in world capitals about Mr. Obuchi's ability and commitment to aggressively address that problem.
"People have serious worries about the current economic situation," Mr. Miyazawa said during a news conference with other appointees. "I think tackling it speedily is the most important thing we have to do."
For Foreign Minister, the position Mr. Obuchi held before becoming Prime Minister, he selected Masahiko Komura, who served as his right hand in the Foreign Ministry since 1996. Mr. Komura, 56, is considered an expert on Japan's relations with Russia, which Mr. Obuchi has emphasized as a focus of his administration. He, like Ms. Noda, is a member of the Komoto faction, the smallest.
In giving two prime Cabinet seats to such a politically insignificant faction, Mr. Obuchi could have been making an attempt to avoid reflecting internal party politics in the national Government.
Ms. Noda's appointment, however, could also be construed as an olive branch to Seiroku Kajiyama, one of the two men who challenged Mr. Obuchi for the party president's job, as well as to younger members of the party.
Ms. Noda, whose grandfather was a ranking Liberal Democratic politician, supported Mr. Kajiyama. She has in the past openly worried about the Liberal Democrats' ability to win the affections of voters.
One of five women currently sitting in the lower house and the youngest Cabinet minister in postwar history, Ms. Noda, 37, emphasized that in her position she can be instrumental in developing new industries and jobs for Japan in telecommunications and information services.
Mr. Arima, the new Education Minister, is a nuclear physicist who was president of Tokyo University from 1989 to 1993. He is a political novice, having won his first term in the upper house during the recent election.
He takes the job at a time when Japan is agonizing over its educational system, which has been rocked by a series of knifings in schools and by concerns that its rigidity stunts creative thinking the country needs in a competitive global economy.
"Japan's future in the international community depends on how well we can raise powerful and vivid young people," he said Thursday.
Mr. Obuchi's advisers also include a best-selling author, Taichi Sakaiya, who was named to head the Economic Planning Agency, a Cabinet-level agency whose responsibility is to come up with suggestions for the nation's economic policy. Mr. Sakaiya, 63, worked in the Ministry of Trade and Industry in the 1970's but quit the Government in 1978, when his novel about an oil crisis and the panic it created became a hit.
A political economist, he is an advocate of moving the seat of the national Government from Tokyo.

HEADLINE: U.S. Backs Off Sanctions, Seeing Poor Effect Abroad

BYLINE: By ERIC SCHMITT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, July 30

 BODY:
The White House and Congress have decided that in many cases, sanctions just are not an effective way to make foreign policy.
In recent months, the United States has backed off imposing sanctions on Asian countries so they would be free to buy American wheat, on Cuba so a court fight with Canada and European allies could be avoided, and on China so American businesses could compete in that huge market. 
 Both Administration and Congressional leaders have avoided sanctions, in large part, because of complaints from farm and business groups, including the Chamber of Commerce and USA Engage, a powerful coalition of 676 companies.
The business leaders contend that in the highly competitive global economy, now regulated by an international trade organization, economic sanctions are ineffective at getting most countries to change and are actually backfiring since they hurt American business.
"Unilateral sanctions only make foreign rivals stronger and taint us as unreliable suppliers," said Bill Lane, Washington director for Caterpillar Inc. and chairman of USA Engage.
About two dozen countries are now subjected to sanctions and about 50 more face the possibility of such penalties, trade groups say. And the trend has been growing. More than 60 laws or executive orders authorizing sanctions -- about half the total since World War I -- have been enacted in the last five years. That roughly coincides with the end of the cold war and the superpower rivalry that had been the chief arena of foreign policy.
But sanctions failed to fill the vacuum, and now legislators and policymakers are pulling the teeth out of existing sanctions or dropping proposed penalties, fearing that unilateral sanctions in particular are hurting American business or diplomatic interests more than the foreign countries or companies they are intended to punish.
This month, Congress exempted food exports from sanctions automatically imposed on India and Pakistan after the two nations conducted nuclear tests in May. The exemption was made in part because the penalties would have barred American farmers from bidding on a $37 million wheat order from Pakistan.
Last week, a Senate committee shelved a popular bill to punish nations, including China, that endorsed or permitted religious persecution after business groups protested that the punishments would alienate allies and trading partners.
"There is a very significant re-evaluation going on regarding sanctions, as there should be," said Senator Chuck Hagel, a Nebraska Republican who led the fight to set aside the religious persecution bill.
The Administration is also recalibrating its approach. In 1996, President Clinton signed into law bills aimed at isolating Cuba, Libya and Iran. But when the measures threatened to draw the United States into a legal challenge and trade war with Canada and European allies, the Administration backed down.
"Having gone through these experiences, it made people recognize we needed a better process for the executive branch," said Stuart E. Eizenstat, Under Secretary of State for Economic Affairs.
The proliferation of sanctions has prompted the State Department and the Senate to form special panels to review the usefulness of sanctions. Legislation is pending in the House and Senate that would require a cost-benefit analysis for new sanctions and would end them after two years unless they are reauthorized.
"The free ride for sanctions is over," said Richard N. Haass, a former national security aide to President Bush and an editor of a new book on sanctions and diplomacy. "Like other foreign policy tools, sanctions are now subject to greater scrutiny."
Administration and Congressional leaders say they are not abandoning sanctions entirely; instead, they want to use them sparingly and in a more focused way. At the same time, these leaders recognize that in place of sanctions, the United States has few options and will either have to rely more on diplomacy or accept its limitations and do nothing at all.
For years, sanctions have provided Congress and the White House with forceful alternatives short of war.
"As an effective and principled foreign policy tool, economic sanctions are older than this republic itself," said Senator Jesse Helms, the North Carolina Republican who heads the Foreign Relations Committee.
Congress, in particular, has been more eager in recent years to slap on an arms embargo, cut foreign aid, vote against appropriating money to the International Monetary Fund, or threaten to do all of those.
"We're less patient with diplomacy and less tolerant of foreign government actions we don't like," said Representative Lee H. Hamilton, an Indiana Democrat on the International Relations Committee. "They make us feel like a player in foreign policy, where our powers tend to be more limited."
The United States has used sanctions to protest political repression, in places like Myanmar, formerly Burma, and to deny nuclear weapons technology to countries like Iran. But other penalties aim to punish countries that mislabel tuna products sold here or withhold foreign aid from nations whose diplomats do not pay their parking tickets.
Even states and municipalities are jumping in, much to the State Department's dismay. Massachusetts has imposed sanctions on companies that do business in Myanmar. New York City is threatening sanctions against Switzerland to press for reparations for Holocaust victims.
Instances in which sanctions have achieved their goal are few. In virtually all the success stories, the United States was joined by many other nations in imposing the penalties.
The international sanctions imposed against Iraq after its invasion of Kuwait in 1990 have slowed, but not stopped, President Saddam Hussein's ability to rebuild his arsenal. And, of course, they have failed to help topple Mr. Hussein.
Multilateral sanctions helped to end apartheid in South Africa, but were requested by the black-majority population and were only one factor that influenced the white minority leadership there.
But even multilateral sanctions have a limited bite. They failed to oust Iraqi troops from Kuwait before the Persian Gulf war or force Haiti's military rulers to return President Jean-Bertrand Aristide to power. Only the use, or threat, of military force accomplished those goals.
Nor has Congress applied these sanctions with any firm consistency. Political repression in one country is punished; in another it is overlooked.
"There is hardly anybody in this room who has been entirely consistent on this subject," said Senator Mitch McConnell, a Kentucky Republican leading the Senate's review of sanctions, who has supported punishing Myanmar for political repression but not China.
Supporters of unilateral sanctions say the penalties have symbolic value on important moral issues, and underscore the United States' leadership role in fighting religious persecution, oppressive regimes or the spread of missile technology.
For instance, the House and Senate this year voted overwhelmingly to impose sanctions on foreign companies or official agencies that aid Iran's ballistic-missile program. Mr. Clinton vetoed the legislation, which is aimed mainly at Russian and Chinese companies, asserting that the bill would hamper the Administration's flexibility in conducting foreign policy. But Congress may override the veto.
"The legislation sends a message to Russian or Chinese companies that they have a choice: 'Do business with the United States or do business with Iran,' " said Howard Kohr, executive director of the American Israel Public Affairs Committee.

HEADLINE: Architect's Landfall at Columbus Circle;
At the Coliseum Site, a Timid Exploration of New Worlds

BYLINE: By HERBERT MUSCHAMP 

 BODY:
More jazz, please. Less Colossi of Memnon. With less reverence toward history and more susceptibility to rhythm, the new plan to develop the Coliseum site on Columbus Circle could become the architectural breakthrough New York has been waiting for.
Not that there's anything wrong with turning the Colossi of Memnon into ritzy apartments. Those gigantic twin-seated monuments of New Kingdom Egypt would be swell to inhabit. Like the giant Good Humor bar Claes Oldenberg proposed for Park Avenue back in the 1960's, the concept has definite Dada appeal. What's missing at this point, though, is a layer of formal invention to state one simple idea: In this town, even colossi like to swing. 
 Designed by David Childs of Skidmore, Owings & Merrill, the plan is a thoroughly reworked version of the scheme Mr. Childs prepared in the 1980's for the original developer of the Coliseum site, Mortimer B. Zuckerman. That plan, which aroused opposition from civic groups because of its size, was an explicit homage to the Century and the Majestic, Irwin S. Chanin's great twin-towered apartment buildings on Central Park West.
Mr. Childs's new design retains the old version's basic Art Deco silhouette. But instead of sheathing the project's two towers with masonry, the architect now proposes to dress them in glass. This is a major improvement, one that will open up this potentially hulking project to reflections of sky, light and space.
Mr. Childs has the best eye in the business for massing, scale and detail, and his plan is a good piece of architecture. But I think we should regard it as a great beginning and then analyze where this design might go.
This plan was chosen for financial, not esthetic, reasons. With Time Warner pledged to join in as the building's major commercial tenant, the Related Companies offered the most substantial deal to develop the site. An Egyptologist might remark on the aptness of erecting a New Kingdom monument to the New World's worship of the almighty dollar. Media mavens might regard these twin towers as a suitable temple precinct for the informational realm in which Time Warner has set such high stakes.
But Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani deserves major credit for the decision to create a theater in the building's base that will be the permanent home for Jazz at Lincoln Center. It's not possible to imagine a better site to build a place for jazz. Chistopher Columbus, when he sailed west, may not have known that he was venturing forth to discover jazz. Four centuries later, we are somewhat more informed. We know that jazz is one of this continent's greatest gifts to the rest of the world.
It is the essential art form of a modern democratic society. Jazz is urban, ethnically diverse, collaborative and mutable. It will be fantastic to enter a jazz hall beneath the pillar with Columbus standing on top. This guy insisted that Europeans explore new worlds.
The Jazz Theater will be in the center of the complex, beneath an oculus that, with luck, will glow with a deep, eternal, indigo blue. Above it will rise a base dedicated to shops, movie theaters, restaurants and other commercial uses. Time Warner will occupy the next highest level in this stacked, vertical city. The two towers, rising to a relatively discreet height of 750 feet, have been planned for residential use: apartments and a hotel. The base is, shall we say, a bit Union Square. Or middle American parking garage.
In a monograph on Irwin S. Chanin, the architect Diana Agrest says Chanin created a new architectural vocabulary for New York in the 1920's. His buildings embodied classical ideals of symmetry and scale, but were clearly different from the decoratively classical designs that were then the norm for residential buildings. Chanin's buildings were great Jazz Age spectaculars, novel exercises in height and image.
Today, one could construct a case for placing a jazz theater within an ersatz Art Deco building. But why bother? Jazz has not run out of sounds, nor has architecture run out of shapes. The Miami firm Arquitectonica, for example, has built projects throughout the world that have incorporated new spatial rhythms into buildings. Mr. Childs himself, in two unbuilt projects designed with Frank Gehry, has entered into the spirit of contemporaneity. He is fully up to the task of linking the first jazz age to the present musical moment.
If you look at the project Mr. Gehry designed for the city of Prague, you gain some insight into architecture's rhythmic possibility. This building, too, was a private development, occupying a prominent civic site. In materials and scale, Mr. Gehry's design harmonized with its context. But the wavy-gravy lines Mr. Gehry introduced on the facade made a statement about the Velvet Revolution. Vaclav Havel lives next door to this building and loves rock music. The Gehry design inserted this neighborly coincidence into Prague's urban fabric.
But while there is much building going on in New York City these days, there is little evidence of daring. When did New York architecture become the screen for the projection of fears? Decision makers who sit on boards like the Metropolitan Transportation Authority and the New York City Planning Commission seem to envision themselves as bunny rabbits scuttling around in small cages of their own making. Evidently, the best we can hope for from such people is a quality that they take to be dignity but the rest of us recognize as failure of nerve.
A design like this one happens not because an architect genuinely believes in it, but because somebody thinks that this is something that somebody else wants. Since this project is not, in fact, a genuine public work, but a private enterprise that will occupy a publicly conspicuous site, there is enormous pressure to make the architecture strive for an approximation of dignity. The result is a design that will play no role in the cultural life of New York or the history of architecture. It is skillful, earnest and devoid of meaning.
Our decision makers are evidently unaware that this city is hungry for buildings that will reflect the present moment as fully as Irwin Chanin's embodied his. As politicians, they are subject to pressures from planning boards, civic groups and other political entities that have come to equate esthetic conservatism with resistance to the radical disruption that real estate development continuously imposes upon our lives.
Groups like the Municipal Art Society, for example, have a history of taking the wrong esthetic stand for the right social reason. But it is the duty of civic leadership to represent an alternative: economic pressures deliver creative opportunities. Creative responses in architecture may not be a community organization's top priority. But a community is part of the larger entity of the city. When creative opportunities are lost, the entire city suffers.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Changing Visions -- The history of development plans for the Coliseum site on Columbus Circle produced a series of grand visions and an equal number of bitter battles. Here are a few of the proposals over the last 13 years.; AIR TRUMP -- In 1985, Donald J. Trump proposed the world's tallest building, a 137-story tower designed by Eli Attia that resembled a spaceship.; BIG GAMBLE -- Mortimer B. Zuckerman won the bidding in 1985, agreeing to pay $455.1 million and proposing a massive building designed by Moshe Safdie.; TRYING AGAIN -- Under fire, Zuckerman hired David Childs of Skidmore, Owings & Merrill to design a smaller tower, but the project ultimately fell apart.; NEW WINNER -- A revised Childs design for the Related Companies was chosen in a new round of bidding. Related and Time Warner agreed to pay about $345 million for the tract, and will build a 2.1-million-square-foot complex. (McCann)

HEADLINE: Ferraro Stresses Status as Only Woman in Race

BYLINE: By ADAM NAGOURNEY 

 BODY:
Asserting that Congress has suffered because of the scarcity of women in its ranks, Geraldine A. Ferraro said yesterday that she should be elected to the Senate because she could, as a woman, bring a perspective to a variety of issues, from child care to the economy, that her male competitors could not match.
Ms. Ferraro played what her advisers believe may be her strongest political card -- she is the only woman in the New York Senate race -- as she spoke in Manhattan to an audience of women who own businesses. She described herself as "a woman with a national profile" who would bring "a kind of suasion without parallel" to the male-dominated world of the United States Senate. 
 Ms. Ferraro, a Democrat, said there was a "need for women in leadership positions" because "we bring another dimension to the political process."
"Our roles as caretakers, nurturers and mothers cannot help but give us a different perspective," she said, looking straight into the camera and blinding lights that her campaign had set up to tape the speech for use in a television advertisement. "It's not necessarily better. But it is different."
Ms. Ferraro said that she was not "putting down men" in making what was a notably undisguised appeal for votes based on sex. But she approvingly cited Carol Gilligan, a psychologist from Harvard University, in describing characteristics that Ms. Ferraro said were particular to women and in short supply in the Government today.
"According to Dr. Gilligan, instead of engaging in confrontation, women are more apt to negotiate," Ms. Ferraro said. "Instead of looking at short-term solutions to problems, women are more apt to think in terms of generations to come. Instead of thinking in win-lose terms, women are more apt to see the gray areas in between."
The remarks by Ms. Ferraro seemed to put at least a temporary end to what has been a fallow period in her campaign. The presence of the camera crew at her speech, along with the fact that her aides encouraged coverage of it, suggested that Ms. Ferraro will try to do what a number of women running for office have tried to do without success in New York over the last decade: turn their identities as women to their political advantage.
Ms. Ferraro, a former candidate for vice president, is facing two men in the Democratic primary who are seeking to challenge Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato, a Republican, this fall: Mark Green, the public advocate for New York City, and Representative Charles E. Schumer of Brooklyn.
The appeal to the women's vote has been a constant if, until yesterday, subliminal theme in all the statewide races this year. Female voters are certain to be critical to the outcome of the Sept. 15 primaries and the general election in November. There are also women running in the Democratic primaries for governor, attorney general and lieutenant governor. And candidates from both parties have been paying particular attention to the kind of issues -- like day care and health care -- that polls indicate are of particular concern to women.
What was unusual about Ms. Ferraro was the directness of her appeal, which she made in a breakfast speech to about two dozen members of the National Association of Women Business Owners. Later in the day, both Mr. Green and Mr. Schumer questioned her remarks, each contending that his positions and record were more significant than his sex.
"It's disappointing for a candidate to run based largely on appeals to gender, ethnicity or religion," Mr. Green said by telephone from Buffalo, where he was campaigning. "My grandma said I was a nice Jewish boy, but I wouldn't dream of seeking voters purely, or largely, on that basis. We, she and I, have apparently different strategies."
Mr. Schumer pointed to legislation he has sponsored in Congress that is of particular interest to women, like allowing abortion clinics to sue protesters who block their buildings.
"Women aren't just interested in gender," Mr. Schumer said from Washington. "They're interested in who can do the most for women."
Ms. Ferraro said she was confident that she would excel in the Senate, where there are nine members who are women. There are 55 women in the House of Representatives.
Ms. Ferraro said she would have an advantage over her rivals because of the national profile she achieved as a candidate for vice president in 1984 and as a CNN commentator.
She said she expected that if she was elected, Senators running for President would routinely seek her support, increasing her influence as a first-year legislator. "When I go down there, I don't go down there as a junior senator," Ms. Ferraro said. "Both Chuck and Mark will."

HEADLINE: House Bars Use of Funds to Move Highway

BYLINE: By MIKE ALLEN 

 BODY:
In a slight to Donald J. Trump, the House of Representatives voted late Wednesday against the use of Federal highway funds to move a stretch of the West Side Highway that is next to Mr. Trump's luxury condominium project, now being built on the Upper West Side.
But Mr. Trump responded that the road suits him fine where it is. 
 In a voice vote, the House approved a one-sentence amendment to its transportation appropriation bill for the next fiscal year that bars the use of any of the money for improvements to the stretch of the West Side Highway near Mr. Trump's project, Riverside South.
The amendment was proposed by an opponent to Mr. Trump's plan, Representative Jerrold L. Nadler, a Democrat from the Upper West Side. He said that the highway relocation was an effort by Mr. Trump to "subvert hundreds of millions of dollars of taxpayer funds" for his personal benefit.
Mr. Nadler failed to kill a $6 million study of the project that Congress approved earlier this year as part of the largest public-works program in the nation's history. The study is to include an examination of design and engineering options for moving the road.
Moving the highway would clear the way for a 21.5-acre park to be included in Riverside South, which is to consist of 11 buildings stretching from 59th Street to 72d Street.
Mr. Nadler said that moving the highway would enhance the view of the Hudson River from the apartments and condominiums, allowing Mr. Trump to charge higher prices and arrange more favorable financing for the project.
The proposal is also controversial because the part of the highway that would have been moved was just renovated about three years ago at a cost of $80 million, and has an estimated life of at least 35 years, according to Mr. Nadler's office.
Last week, the Federal Government denied Mr. Trump's application for government mortgage insurance for the next phase of the project.
Despite the setbacks, Mr. Trump declared victory after learning about the latest vote.
"The job is a much simpler one for me if we leave the highway in place," he said. "It's not a highway issue. It's a park issue."
Mr. Trump said the highway would not affect the view from his high-rise condominium project, adding that he is getting fabulous rents for the apartments.
"Jerry Nadler is my best friend, economically," Mr. Trump said. "He's played right into my hands."
Mr. Nadler replied that Mr. Trump's response is belied by his efforts to get Federal funds for the project.
"Congress has told Trump very consistently, 'We are not going to let you spend money on this boondoggle,' " Mr. Nadler said. "What part of 'no' doesn't he understand?"
The battle between the two has become personal. In commenting on the amendment, Mr. Trump made a joke about Mr. Nadler's large physique. And Mr. Nadler issued a news release titled, "Rep. Nadler Fights Trump on Floor of House."
Mr. Nadler acknowledged that Mr. Trump and his supporters could seek funds again in the future.
Representative Sue Kelly, a Republican from Westchester who had requested the funds for the study, said yesterday that moving the highway continues to enjoy "broad support among local civic, labor, environmental and business groups."
Mary R. Musca, the executive director of the Riverside South Planning Corporation, a coalition of civic organizations that supports that development, said she was relieved that the financing for the study was preserved.
"We'd have to go back to Congress to get additional money to move the highway, but we weren't asking for that now," she said.
Mr. Nadler estimates that moving the highway would cost more than $300 million, but Ms. Musca said it could be done for about $120 million.
The amendment will be reviewed later this year by the conference committee that will reconcile the House and Senate transportation bills.

HEADLINE: Pataki Vetoes Vallone Plan To Favor Local Contractors

BYLINE: By RICHARD PEREZ-PENA 

DATELINE: ALBANY, July 30

 BODY:
A centerpiece of Peter F. Vallone's campaign for governor, a bill allowing New York cities to give preference to in-state contractors over those from other states, has been vetoed by Gov. George E. Pataki, who Mr. Vallone hopes to oppose in November.
Mr. Pataki said in his veto message, dated Wednesday and made public today, that while he commended the bill's aim of promoting business in New York State, it would actually hurt in-state businesses by triggering a trade war with other states. 
 "Since New York State businesses are net exporters of goods and services," the Governor wrote, "this bill will have a negative impact on New York businesses seeking to contract in other states and engender results contrary to the bill's stated purpose of promoting economic development in New York State."
But Mr. Vallone, the Democratic Speaker of the New York City Council, said the Republican Governor's motivations were political. "This is a case of, if I say yes, the Governor is going to say no," he said. "And you have a Governor who wants to run for President, and he's not going to do anything to upset business people in other states who would oppose this."
Mr. Pataki, who left today on a three-day fund-raising trip to Colorado and California, was not available for comment. But a spokesman, Charles H. Deister, said that Mr. Vallone "doesn't have a basic grasp on the facts," adding that the bill "will cost jobs, instead of creating jobs."
The dispute over the bill illumstrates a broad theme of this race: Mr. Pataki says that after three and a half years under him, the state is doing well economically, while his four Democratic rivals assert that New York continues to lag far behind the national boom and that drastic policy changes are needed. The other Democrats in the race are Lieut. Gov. Betsy McCaughey Ross, Brooklyn District Attorney Charles J. Hynes, and James Larocca, a former state commissioner of energy and transportation.
The veto is more significant in the wake of a ruling early this month by the Court of Appeals, New York's highest court, striking down a provision in the New York City Charter that allows the city to bypass the usual rule requiring that government contracts go to the lowest responsible bidder.
New York City has for years given preference to contractors based in the city, a policy that Mr. Vallone contends adds $1 billion a year to the city's economy. The court decision outlawed that practice, but the bill passed by the Legislature in June would have effectively resurrected it.
The bill, passed with broad bipartisan support, would have allowed a local government to bypass an out-of-state contractor who submitted the low bid, and instead choose an in-state contractor, as long as the New York company submitted a bid no more than 5 percent higher than the low bid.
For months, Mr. Vallone has made the contracting preference a prominent part of his economic plan, saying that as Governor, he would make it state policy. Meanwhile, he sought to make an in-state preference city policy.
Mr. Vallone had planned to have the Council pass a bill next Tuesday taking advantage of the new state law, to make it city policy. Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, a Republican, opposed the plan and would have vetoed it, but Mr. Vallone clearly had the votes to override a veto.
Today, Mr. Vallone appeared at the site of a planned bridge across the Niagara River linking Buffalo and the Canadian city of Fort Erie, to call on Mr. Pataki to give preference to New York contractors in the bidding to build the span.
"Let's try to do it in Buffalo, hire a Buffalo contractor, keep the jobs in Buffalo," he said in an interview, after the event. "I never believed that the Governor would veto this. It's putting the state at such a disadvantage."
The Business Council of New York State, the state's largest large-business group, opposed the bill for the same reason given by Mr. Pataki.
Mr. Pataki's veto message also said that the bill was poorly written, failing to make it clear that when an out-of-state bidder is bypassed, a contract must go to the lowest in-state bidder. He also contended that the bill was in conflict with Federal law, which requires that any project built with Federal money go to the lowest responsible bidder.

Sanctions May Not Be Way For U.S. Foreign Policy
The White House and Congress have decided that in many cases sanctions are not an effective way to make foreign policy. The United States has recently backed off imposing sanctions on Asian countries so they would be free to buy American wheat, on Cuba to avoid a court fight with Canada and European allies, and on China so American businesses could compete in that huge market. HEADLINE: An Admirer of Giuliani Feels His Wrath

BYLINE: By DAVID FIRESTONE 

 BODY:
It was only a few years ago that Daniel A. Biederman was so in tune with Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's thinking that he was chosen to be on the Mayor's 1993 transition team, helping to select city officials. Even yesterday, having been sent sprawling by Mr. Giuliani's wrath, Mr. Biederman claimed to be an enormous admirer of the man who is dismantling his career.
But Mr. Biederman, who runs three of the city's most prominent business improvement districts, may have emulated the Mayor's desire for control a bit too intently. On Wednesday, the Mayor ordered the largest of those districts, the Grand Central Partnership, to shut down, reacting to what aides said were several years of pent-up annoyance over the consolidation of power in Mr. Biederman's hands. Aides said Mr. Guiliani believed that Mr. Biederman had failed to recognize where the city's true center of power lies. 
 The Mayor's action remains a mystery to Mr. Biederman, who insists he never knew until recently that the Mayor was upset with him. But it was not terribly surprising to city officials and several members of Mr. Biederman's boards. Even friends of Mr. Biederman describe a difficult man whose sense of himself may have been overmatched only by Mr. Giuliani's, producing a conflict of personalities he could not win.
"It's not that he's arrogant, but he is certitudinous," said a member of the board of one of the three improvement districts, who spoke on the condition of anonymity. "He believes his views are the right ones on almost any subject, and that's not something that goes down very well with Rudy Giuliani and the others down at City Hall."
Until a few years ago, however, Mr. Biederman, who is 45, was more accustomed to receiving praise from Mr. Giuliani and his predecessors, who have long relied on the city's business improvement districts to collect fees on property owners in order to supplement city services like sanitation pickups, security patrols, and installation of street furniture. In 1980, Mr. Biederman was working as an analyst for the private management firm that helped straighten out the city's finances after the fiscal crisis when he was selected by city and business officials to direct the restoration of the Bryant Park area.
The Koch administration thought highly of his work there and asked him to apply similar management techniques to the Grand Central area in the mid-1980's. In 1990, property owners along 34th Street asked him to manage their district as well, which he agreed to do without giving up any of his other jobs. His combined salary is now $335,000. He has also explored creating similar districts along 23d Street and in Jersey City, although those attempts have not been successful.
In his first term, Mr. Giuliani frequently praised the successful restoration of Bryant Park, along with the work of the midtown improvement districts. Even now, city officials generally concede that Mr. Biederman's districts have done a good job at their assigned tasks of improving their neighborhoods, although they have accused him of failing to provide required oversight information to the city and of ignoring city rules on competitive bidding.
But about three years ago, Mr. Biederman's reputation began to dim, and the praise from Mr. Giuliani became more faint. In 1995, he was sharply criticized for hiring workers to harass homeless people away from the Grand Central district, and earlier this year, a Federal judge ruled that his districts had violated minimum-wage laws by hiring homeless people as guards and sanitation workers at $1 to $1.50 an hour. A City Council report last year also cited several complaints that Mr. Biederman ignored the wishes of property owners in his district.
Officials close to the Mayor said he grew concerned in the last two years that the districts were becoming too powerful, taking accountability away from elected officials at City Hall. The Mayor has made it clear that he loathes giving up control to any outside entity, let alone to someone making more than twice his salary who controls three districts and who has borrowed heavily to finance major capital improvements.
The beginning of the end apparently came last year when the Mayor reined in Mr. Biederman's ability to borrow money by selling bonds. Mr. Beiderman's protest of this action was perceived by the Mayor as a disrespectful lecture, aides said, and Mr. Giuliani determined to move him aside.
"Maybe we were a little tone deaf, but we did not intend to be disrespectful of the Mayor," Mr. Biederman said in an interview yesterday. "I do a fairly discreet job of running the services of this company and I don't have aspirations beyond that. Maybe I don't understand public sector things as well as I should -- I'm really a just a private BID operator."
The city told the Grand Central Partnership that Mr. Biederman would have to give up two districts in order to have its contract renewed, and mayoral aides said they were angered that Mr. Biederman and his allies would not negotiate the point.
"They thought they could ignore us, and we would just give in to avoid the bad press of shutting them down," said one official in Mr. Giuliani's office. "They thought they could win a power play with this Mayor. I don't know where they've been for the last five years since Rudy Giuliani's been in office."
But Mr. Biederman and Peter L. Malkin, who until recently was the chairman of the Grand Central Partnership, said the city would not negotiate its demand, although the partnership wanted to discuss it in good faith. Mr. Malkin said Mr. Biederman's salary from each district was actually lower than that of other district managers around the city, and said the joint operations of the three districts led to a lower percentage of overhead than other districts.
"The partnership provides the best value for the dollar of any district in the city," said Mr. Malkin, an ally of Mr. Biederman who is a major midtown property owner. "I guess someone at City Hall is unhappy that one person is running so much and acting in such an independent fashion and doing such a good job."
Mr. Biederman, a Republican who lives in Chappaqua, N.Y., said that although he admired Mr. Giuliani, he did not intend to pack up his boxes and leave without a fight, although he declined to predict what would happen next.
"I still think the Mayor has done a great job," he said. "The last thing I ever thought I'd be doing is fighting with him."

HEADLINE: New Jersey Legislature Puts Plan To Conserve Open Land on Ballot

BYLINE: By JENNIFER PRESTON 

DATELINE: TRENTON, July 30

 BODY:
In one of the nation's most ambitious efforts to preserve vanishing farmland and woodlands, the New Jersey Legislature passed a ballot measure today that will ask voters in November to approve borrowing $1 billion to protect half of the state's remaining two million acres of undeveloped land over the next 10 years.
The vote was a major political triumph for Gov. Christine Todd Whitman, who had made preserving one million acres of open space the top priority of her second term. It came just days after members of her own party dealt her a major blow by rejecting her other top priority: an increase of 4 cents a gallon in the gasoline tax, to be phased in over three years, for mass transit and highway projects. 
 Governor Whitman outlined her proposal to preserve open space in her inaugural speech in January to address mounting concern from residents and local officials about the impact of rapid development on the state's landscape and the quality of life in fast-growing towns.
She said she realized that significant steps had to be taken because, traveling around the state in her first term, she could see that much of the state's rural character was quickly disappearing because of development pressures.
Municipal officials across New Jersey had embraced development for years, mainly to expand their property tax bases. But now a growing number of local officials are finding that unchecked growth is driving up local property taxes and causing traffic congestion and overcrowded public schools.
"The people see what is happening around them," said Michael Catania, executive director of the Nature Conservancy of New Jersey, a nonprofit organization that has helped protect environmentally sensitive lands from development for more than a decade. "They see what is being lost."
Although New Jersey voters have strongly supported open-space bond referendums in the past, Governor Whitman said that she would campaign aggressively during the next several months to win voter approval for this measure.
"Future generations will look to the passage of this resolution as one of the most important and defining events in determining the quality of life in New Jersey for the next millennium," she said. "Policy directives will come and go. But the preservation effort envisioned in this program will last forever."
The Senate approved the bill unanimously; the General Assembly voted 70 to 3 in favor, with little debate. Supporters in the Legislature described the resolution as one of the most important initiatives for the state's future.
Last month, Republican leaders rejected Mrs. Whitman's initial proposal to pay for the preservation of one million acres with a gasoline tax increase of 2 cents a gallon. Mrs. Whitman agreed instead to push for legislative approval of a bond referendum that asked voters to consider the $1 billion borrowing plan.
But she continued to fight for a 4-cents-a-gallon gasoline tax increase for transportation projects until Republican legislative leaders in the Assembly canceled a scheduled vote on it this week because it lacked bipartisan support.
Assembly Democrats, who had banded together on Monday to oppose the gasoline tax, considered trying to block the open space legislation today to hand Mrs. Whitman yet another setback. But statewide polls have consistently shown that protecting the environment and open space has ranked high among New Jersey voters' priorities, making the bill politically impossible for most Democrats to dismiss. Democrats, in the end, voted overwhelmingly in both houses for the measure.
Since 1961, New Jersey voters have approved nine bond referendums that have raised $1.39 billion to help protect more than 400,000 acres of land from development. And 53 New Jersey towns and 13 counties have approved tax increases that range from 1 to 5 cents on each $100 of assessed property value to protect undeveloped land.
Over the years, the state has permanently preserved 922,000 acres from development out of a total of 4.8 million acres of land in New Jersey. Preserving an additional million acres of open space, including 500,000 acres of farmland, would mean that almost two million acres statewide -- two-fifths of the state -- would be protected from development.
State officials are looking to provide matching funds to towns and counties that come forward with plans to protect farmland or turn other undeveloped land into parks.
Under the proposal, the state would spend the proceeds from the $1 billion bond issue, along with $450 million from sales tax revenue, to preserve one million acres of open space over the next 10 years. The referendum would not increase the sales tax, but would dedicate sales tax revenue for the land acquisition and for repayment of the borrowing costs. It would take 30 years to repay the debt, which with interest is estimated to total between $2.5 billion and $3 billion.
Phyllis Myers, president of State Resource Strategies in Washington, which tracks state and local open-space programs around the country, described New Jersey's efforts as a "bold move" and said that it reflected a growing effort by state and local governments to respond to concerns about the impact of rapid development in their communities.
Governor Whitman's open-space proposal, and her renewed commitment to a statewide master plan, have caused some concern among developers who point out that they are building residential developments in outlying areas in response to market demand.
Patrick O'Keefe, head of the New Jersey Builders Association, said that the association had no position on the resolution adopted by the Legislature today. He pointed out that the association had supported smaller bond referendums for open-space protection in the past.
"But at some point," Mr. O'Keefe said, "the administration will have to take out a map and show us which million acres they are talking about, and I think at that point, it will be a much more meaningful discussion."
The Governor's Council on New Jersey Outdoors, which recommended to Governor Whitman late last year that the state protect a million acres, has suggested that the state preserve 500,000 acres of farmland, 200,000 acres of open space for recreation, 100,000 acres for watershed protection and 200,000 acres for greenways, trails and corridors.
There are now more than 800,000 acres of farmland in New Jersey, said Maureen Ogden, a former member of the Assembly who was chairman of the Governor's Council. She said the council recommended that 500,000 acres of farmland be preserved because the Department of Agriculture said that a base of that size was needed for the farming industry to remain as a viable part of the state's economy.
Most of the farmland would not be bought outright by the state. Instead, the state would seek to buy development rights from farmers, which would allow them to reap some immediate financial gain for their property while continuing to farm it. By buying the development rights, the state would require that the farmland be used only for agricultural purposes.
"This is the most important environmental initiative of this decade," said Ms. Ogden, who led the campaigns for smaller open space bond referendums, known as the Green Acres program, during her 14 years in the Legislature. "Its importance will only increase as the years go by. We are trying to take steps now to preserve open space before it is no longer available."

HEADLINE: Business District Vows to Fight City's Order to Shut It Down

BYLINE: By THOMAS J. LUECK 

 BODY:
Setting the stage for a legal battle over the rules governing business improvement districts in New York City, the Grand Central Partnership said yesterday that it would seek a court order to block the seizure of its assets and financial records by the city.
Two days ago, the Giuliani administration said it would not renew its contract with the Grand Central group, one of the most powerful of the city's 40 districts, effectively putting the partnership out of business. Without the contract, the group will as of today lose its ability to raise money from district property owners for services like street cleaning and security patrols. 
 Yesterday, Norman Sturner, chairman of the Grand Central Partnership, said in an interview that the city's intentions went beyond denying the contract. The city's Department of Business Services, he said, sent the district a letter ordering it to turn over all financial assets in its "possession or control" to the city. The Grand Central group still has an unspecified amount of money in bank accounts.
"We cannot allow this to happen," said Mr. Sturner, general partner in Murray Hill Partners, a firm that owns several office buildings in the Grand Central district. He said lawyers for the group would seek a temporary restraining order today against any seizure by the city.
"The city is threatening to come in here with armed police," he said. "This is unfair and un-American, and it is time for the courts to determine how much a BID can be separate from the city."
Last night, Michael D. Hess, the city's Corporation Counsel, said the city's rights to the group's assets and financial records were spelled out in the contract.
"We don't intend to seize assets we are not entitled to, but I think a judge will see this is clearly laid out in the contract," he said.
The threatened legal clash came amid harsh criticism of the Grand Central Partnership's business practices by several city officials, including Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, who said it had refused to submit to oversight of its financial dealings. The complaints were leveled mainly at Daniel A. Biederman, the president of the Grand Central Partnership, who also heads two other powerful districts in midtown Manhattan, the 34th Street Partnership and the Bryant Park Restoration Corporation.
The administration demanded that Mr. Biederman, who had what city officials said they considered an excessive combined salary of $335,000 a year, relinquish control of two of the districts. Mr. Biederman declined.
In addition, the city said, the Grand Central group refused to respond to requests for financial information and disobeyed rules requiring competitive bidding in awarding contracts.
"They just ignored the city and the Department of Business Services, so it is over now," Mr. Giuliani said yesterday at a news conference.
He added that he hoped a new group would be formed to replace the Grand Central Partnership, one which he said would "have an executive director who works solely for that BID" and would be "more responsive to the overall needs of the community."
But in calling for a new district, or at least new management, the Mayor apparently provoked a call to battle from Mr. Biederman, members of his board and several property owners whose support would be necessary if the group were to be reconstituted. Mr. Sturner said he and the chairmen of the 34th Street and Bryant Park groups stood behind Mr. Biederman, and would resign before the financial records sought by the city were relinquished.
Even though the 34th Street and Bryant Park groups have city contracts running until 2001, recent demands by city officials would severely undermine their effectiveness, he said, since they would be required to abandon a system of shared computers and management procedures controlled by Mr. Biederman.
"The city wants more than oversight, they want control," Mr. Sturner said. As an example, he said, aides to the mayor had said earlier this year that the Grand Central group would be denied a new contract if it tried to increase its budget of $10 million, even though the money for the budget is raised entirely from property owners and an increase had been supported by a 96-to-4 vote of its board, with only City Hall appointees voting against it.
Such questions as how much business improvement districts can intrude on what have been considered city services, and when city oversight crosses the line into government control, have been debated since business groups trying to enhance their neighborhoods and property values first organized districts like the Grand Central Partnership, which was formed in 1988.
At the core of the city's dispute with Mr. Biederman's groups is language in each district's municipal contract. Under the contract, the city's Department of Finance collects fees from the property owners who are members of each district, and transfers the proceeds to the district board.
In return for the city's assistance in collecting money, the districts agree to rules governing oversight of their finances, including how they buy goods and services and what civic improvements they take on.
To what extent the conflict between city officials and the Grand Central group will affect other business improvement districts remained unclear yesterday. But it erupted in the wake of repeated efforts by the Mayor to exert greater influence over the districts, which span sections of all five boroughs.
The mayor's most recent directive came in June, when he ordered all the districts to get approval of more of their plans for hiring, purchasing and for civic projects from the Department of Business Services.
Last year the city also banished the practice, employed by both the Grand Central District and the 34th Street Partnership, of raising money for their projects by selling bonds.
Several executives at the districts said yesterday that the Mayor's actions had provoked widespread anxiety, even though they said they did not expect City Hall to deny any additional contracts.
"You simply can't say anything that might even remotely be construed by the mayor's people as criticism of City Hall," said the manager of a district far smaller than any of Mr. Biederman's who spoke on condition that he and his group remain anonymous.
Still, at his news conference yesterday, the Mayor sought to assure property owners who have supported most of the city's business improvement districts that they still have City Hall's support.
"This is not in any way a suggestion that other BID's are doing anything wrong, just the opposite," the Mayor said. "There are BID's in the city that are doing a terrific job."
Tom Cusick, president of the Fifth Avenue Business Improvement -- the only one of several district managers approached for this article who agreed to be identified -- said he had "no concerns about the BID community in general."
"What is going on now is something specific to the Grand Central Partnership, and it's up to them to work things out with the city," he said. Despite complaints from some other district exectutives, he said, recent demands by City Hall for greater involvement in their affairs had not interfered with their effectiveness.
"Not only do I think the city's actions are acceptable, I think they are good," he said. "If we can't share information we are in trouble."

HEADLINE: End of H.M.O. for the Elderly Brings Dismay in Rural Ohio

BYLINE: By PETER T. KILBORN 

DATELINE: PIQUA, Ohio, July 26

 BODY:
For the elderly and disabled people of this weary looking city surrounded by cornfields, Anthem Blue Cross and Blue Shield's Medicare health maintenance organization was a godsend.
It bought a motorized scooter for Ronnie Hines, who has diabetes and multiple sclerosis. It diagnosed Dorothy Kloecker's shallow breathing disorder, after many tests, and helped her to control it with daily two-hour walks through the hilly cemetery. It removed the benign but disconcerting lump in Rosie Stelzner's jaw. 
 Anthem provided exactly the kind of aggressive prevention-oriented care that Congress and other health planners envisioned when they opened up the 33-year-old Medicare program to H.M.O.'s and other forms of managed care.
But it did not last long. In late May, just a year and a half after it started its Medicare H.M.O. here, Anthem announced that it was discontinuing the service as of Dec. 31 in 19 Ohio counties and parts of 3 others. The decision affected 18,235 elderly and disabled clients, including many in Piqua (pronounced pick-wah and less often pick-way).
Becky Howe, 50, who has a degenerative joint disease, expressed the view of many of the subscribers when she said:"They gave us life. Now they're trying to suck that life out of us."
Two subscribers have filed a class-action suit in an effort to stop Anthem from pulling out.
Anthem officials said their company, which has successfully operated a Medicare H.M.O. around Cincinnati, about 75 miles south, for three years, found itself over-extended, over-subscribed and under-paid by the Federal Government when it expanded. Its experience highlights the difficulties managed care companies face as they try to extend service to Medicare clients outside the bigger cities -- and the upheaval that results when these companies stumble.
Anthem's retreat, like those of other H.M.O.'s recently from 10 counties in California, 10 in South Carolina and the whole state of Utah, also casts a shadow over the decision by Congress and the Clinton Administration, as part of the 1997 Balanced Budget Act, to steer more of the nation's retirees into managed care.
The goal was to slow the 10-percent annual increases in Federal spending for Medicare and the even sharper increases some expect as the baby-boom generation retires. Encouraging competition among managed care companies for Medicare beneficiaries, they reasoned, would mean more benefits for the elderly and lower costs for the Federal Government.
Here, however, managed care for the elderly and disabled is going, not coming, and in the process it is doing exactly what many critics feared: Building expectations among the elderly, then dashing them, leaving the subscribers angry and confused.
Until just a few months ago, three commercial television channels carried commercials for Anthem Senior Advantage, the Medicare H.M.O. The benefits over traditional Medicare were clear: Prescriptions would cost just $5 to $15; doctor visits $5; there would be no deductibles, no premiums beyond the $43.80 deducted from Social Security deduction for doctors' fee, and no charges at all for hospitalization, and there would be free screenings and free preventive care.
Medicare, by contrast, charges patients 20 percent of doctors' bills and $760 of their initial hospital expenses. It does not cover preventive care or most medicine or screenings.
"I called the number," said Mrs. Kloecker, 77, and signed up immediately.
Mrs. Kloecker told Mrs. Stelzner, 73, who also called. "I asked them, 'Is my doctor in this?' " Mrs. Stelzner said. Assured that he was, she signed up, too.
Along with the television commercials, Anthem sent a sales agent around. He knocked on Dolly Floss's door. Mrs. Floss, 58, is diabetic and has been disabled and unemployable since a patient she was treating as a nurse's aide fell on her back and shattered four disks.
"He said, 'If you think this is too good to be true, wait till you see the other things coming along,' " she said. " 'Eyeglasses, hearing aids, dental.' I could get my testing strips. I'm supposed to test my blood sugar eight times a day." The strips cost $48 for a box of 50, so she had been testing just four or five times a week.
Then the cancellation notice arrived.
Virginia Hirsch, 69, who lives in a 25-year-old mobile home on a Social Security check of $906 a month after losing two husbands to death, said: "I called Anthem. I was very upset. They said, 'Nothing is guaranteed.' I had great faith in Blue Cross and Blue Shield. But that went down the drain. They had never done anything as underhanded as this."
Lawmakers are just as peeved. Gov. George V. Voinovich, a Republican candidate for the Senate seat being vacated by Senator John Glenn, a Democrat, has publicly rebuked the company and ordered an investigation. The State Legislature sharply questioned Anthem officials at a hearing.
Kathy Keller, spokeswoman for the Ohio office of the American Association of Retired Persons in Columbus, noted that managed care had been widely proclaimed as the salvation of the Medicare program, and argued that the Anthem experience should trouble Congress on two counts.
Not only does it erode the elderly's confidence in managed care, Ms. Keller said, but it can also disrupt their treatment, pushing them back into traditional fee-for-service medicine without the preventative care that many H.M.O.'s promise.
Of the 18,235 subscribers affected, 6,500 live in Miami County, which includes Piqua, and five other counties where they are left with no H.M.O. at all. Many of Anthem's H.M.O. subscribers have incomes of $10,000 or $15,000 a year, mostly from Social Security.
Some of Piqua's retirees had Medigap insurance to cover the 20 percent of doctors' fees that Medicare does not, and they gave it up. Mrs. Stelzner expects to get hers back, at a cost of $238.60 every two months. But that will not be possible for everyone affected, and some others whose whose health has deteriorated might have to pay more for it.
Anthem, with headquarters in a new granite and glass suburban office building just north of Cincinnati, acknowledges excesses in trying to enroll Medicare recipients, in part to capture them before competitors did.
Having offered its Senior Advantage program successfully in seven counties in and around Cincinnati for three years, Anthem began expanding to 37 more counties at the start of last year. By the end of the year, enrollment had leaped to 56,000 from 16,000, and it is now up to 63,000.
"We didn't know what hit us, we were growing so fast," said Lynne Gross, Anthem's vice president for government programs. "We didn't predict the high sales in rural areas."
The more beneficiaries Anthem enrolled, the more money it lost, in part because of the Balanced Budget Act, which restrains spending for Medicare. In an analysis of last year's results, Ms. Gross said the company discovered that in urban areas it collected an average of $389 a month in Medicare reimbursements for each subscriber and spent $345, for a profit of $44. But in rural areas, it collected $325 and spent $361, losing $36, with the lower reimbursement rates.
Anthem also discovered that conventional Medicare, with its traditional fee-for-service copayments and deductibles, was a luxury that relatively poor retirees in places like Miami County often shunned.
"We had people saying for the first time, 'I'm beginning to live!"' Ms. Gross said. "Fee-for-service wasn't meeting the needs of these folks. I think managed care in rural communities may have created a demand for care where there wasn't any care before."
Experts say there are cautionary lessons in Ohio for Federal health planners. Congress's new Medicare program greatly expands upon more than a decade of efforts to encourage the 33.5 million elderly and 5 million disabled beneficiaries to enroll in managed care.
Called Medicare Plus Choice, the new program still allows people to choose regular Medicare. But it also lets them choose among a variety of alternatives, primarily several forms of managed care plans.
For each subscriber, the Health Care Financing Administration pays H.M.O.'s 95 percent of the average that the agency spends each year for people who use Medicare's fee-for-service care.
The base rates vary widely by county, from less than $400 a month in rural areas to almost $800 in New York City. To make the lower-rate areas more appealing to H.M.O.'s, Congress set up a formula to shrink the variations, but so far it has not enhanced the economic appeal of rural areas for H.M.O.'s. In addition, Congress limited increases in most reimbursement rates to just 2 percent -- less than the rise in health care costs.
"It is clear," said Senator Mike DeWine, Republican of Ohio, "that Anthem made some monumental mistakes and over-promised what they could do."
But Mr. DeWine added: "It would not shock me if Anthem is just the beginning of this. The preliminary evidence would indicate that there is a bigger issue than Anthem making bad business decisions."
